Of faint stale smells of beer From the sawdust-trampled street With all its
muddy feet that press To early coffee-stands.
<|endoftext|>

With the other masquerades That time
resumes, One thinks of all the hands That are raising
dingy shades In a thousand furnished rooms.
<|endoftext|>

I am moved by fancies that are curled
Around these images, and cling: The notion of some infinitely
gentle Infinitely suffering thing.
<|endoftext|>

Wipe your hand across your mouth, and laugh;
The worlds revolve like ancient women
Gathering fuel in vacant lots.
<|endoftext|>

Half-past one, The street lamp sputtered, The street lamp muttered,
The street lamp said, "Regard that woman Who hesitates toward you in
the light of the door Which opens on her like a grin.
<|endoftext|>

You see the border of her dress
Is torn and stained with sand, And you see the corner
of her eye Twists like a crooked pin.
<|endoftext|>

A broken spring in a factory
yard, Rust that clings to the form that the strength has
left Hard and curled and ready to snap.
<|endoftext|>

Half-past two, The street-lamp said, "Remark the cat which
flattens itself in the gutter, Slips out its tongue And
devours a morsel of rancid butter.
<|endoftext|>

" So the hand of the child,
automatic, Slipped out and pocketed a
toy that was running along the quay.
<|endoftext|>

I could see
nothing behind that
child's eye.
<|endoftext|>

Half-past three, The lamp
sputtered, The lamp muttered
in the dark.
<|endoftext|>

She smooths
the hair of
the grass.
<|endoftext|>

The moon
has lost her
memory.
<|endoftext|>

The reminiscence comes Of sunless dry geraniums And
dust in crevices, Smells of chestnuts in the streets And female smells in shuttered rooms
And cigarettes in corridors And cocktail smells in bars.
<|endoftext|>

" The lamp said, "Four
o'clock, Here is the number
on the door.
<|endoftext|>

You have the key, The
little lamp spreads a ring
on the stair, Mount.
<|endoftext|>

The bed is open; the
tooth-brush hangs on the wall, Put your shoes at the
door, sleep, prepare for life.
<|endoftext|>

" The last twist
of the
knife.
<|endoftext|>

They are rattling breakfast plates in basement kitchens,
And along the trampled edges of the street I am aware of the damp
souls of housemaids Sprouting despondently at area gates.
<|endoftext|>

The readers of the Boston
Evening Transcript Sway in the wind
like a field of ripe corn.
<|endoftext|>

" Miss Helen Slingsby was my maiden
aunt, And lived in a small house near a fashionable square Cared
for by servants to the number of four.
<|endoftext|>

Now when she died there was silence
in heaven And silence at her
end of the street.
<|endoftext|>

The shutters were drawn and the undertaker
wiped his feet-- He was aware that this sort of
thing had occurred before.
<|endoftext|>

The dogs were handsomely
provided for, But shortly afterwards
the parrot died too.
<|endoftext|>

The Dresden clock continued ticking on the mantelpiece,
And the footman sat upon the dining-table Holding the second housemaid on his knees--
Who had always been so careful while her mistress lived.
<|endoftext|>

Miss Nancy Ellicott Strode across the hills
and broke them, Rode across the hills and broke them-- The barren New England
hills-- Riding to hounds Over the cow-pasture.
<|endoftext|>

Miss Nancy Ellicott smoked And danced all the
modern dances; And her aunts were not quite sure how they felt
about it, But they knew that it was modern.
<|endoftext|>

Upon the glazen shelves kept watch
Matthew and Waldo, guardians of the faith, The army
of unalterable law.
<|endoftext|>

Apollinax visited
the United States His laughter tinkled
among the teacups.
<|endoftext|>

I thought of Fragilion, that shy figure
among the birch-trees, And of Priapus in the shrubbery Gaping
at the lady in the swing.
<|endoftext|>

In the
palace of
Mrs.
<|endoftext|>

Phlaccus, at Professor
Channing-Cheetah's He laughed like an
irresponsible foetus.
<|endoftext|>

I looked
for the head of
Mr.
<|endoftext|>

Apollinax rolling under
a chair Or grinning over a screen
With seaweed in its hair.
<|endoftext|>

I heard the beat of centaur's hoofs over
the hard turf As his dry and passionate talk
devoured the afternoon.
<|endoftext|>

"He is a
charming man"--"But after all
what did he mean?
<|endoftext|>

He must be unbalanced,"--
"There was something he said that
I might have challenged.
<|endoftext|>

" Of
dowager
Mrs.
<|endoftext|>

Phlaccus, and
Professor and
Mrs.
<|endoftext|>

Cheetah I remember
a slice of lemon, and a
bitten macaroon.
<|endoftext|>

As she laughed I was aware of becoming involved
in her laughter and being part of it, until her teeth were only
accidental stars with a talent for squad-drill.
<|endoftext|>

I was drawn in by short gasps, inhaled at each
momentary recovery, lost finally in the dark caverns
of her throat, bruised by the ripple of unseen muscles.
<|endoftext|>

" I decided that if the shaking of her breasts could be
stopped, some of the fragments of the afternoon might be collected, and I concentrated
my attention with careful subtlety to this end.
<|endoftext|>

I observe: "Our
sentimental
friend the moon!
<|endoftext|>

Or possibly (fantastic, I confess) It may be Prester
John's balloon Or an old battered lantern hung aloft To
light poor travellers to their distress.
<|endoftext|>

" She then:
"How you
digress!
<|endoftext|>

" And I then: "Some one frames upon the keys That
exquisite nocturne, with which we explain The night and moonshine; music which
we seize To body forth our vacuity.
<|endoftext|>

" She then:
"Does this refer
to me?
<|endoftext|>

" "Oh no, it is
I who am
inane.
<|endoftext|>

" "You, madam, are the eternal humorist,
The eternal enemy of the absolute, Giving
our vagrant moods the slightest twist!
<|endoftext|>

With your air indifferent and
imperious At a stroke our mad poetics
to confute--" And--"Are we then so serious?
<|endoftext|>

" O quam te
memorem
Virgo.
<|endoftext|>

I should find Some way incomparably
light and deft, Some way we both should understand, Simple and
faithless as a smile and shake of the hand.
<|endoftext|>

She turned away, but with the autumn weather Compelled
my imagination many days, Many days and many hours:
Her hair over her arms and her arms full of flowers.
<|endoftext|>

And I wonder
how they should have been
together!
<|endoftext|>

I should
have lost a gesture
and a pose.
<|endoftext|>

Sometimes these cogitations
still amaze The troubled midnight and
the noon's repose.
<|endoftext|>

In winter I
get up at night, And dress
by yellow candle light.
<|endoftext|>

In summer quite the
other way, I have to go
to bed by day.
<|endoftext|>

I have to go to bed and see The
birds still hopping on the tree, Or hear the grown-up people's
feet, Still going past me in the street.
<|endoftext|>

And does it not seem hard to you, When
all the sky is clear and blue, And I should like so much
to play, To have to go to bed by day?
<|endoftext|>

All night long and every night, When my
mamma puts out the light I see the people marching
by, As plain as day, before my eye.
<|endoftext|>

Armies and emperors and kings, All
carrying different kinds of things, And marching in
so grand a way, You never saw the like by day.
<|endoftext|>

So fine a show was never seen At
the great circus on the green; For every kind
beast and man Is marching in that caravan.
<|endoftext|>

At first they move a little slow, But still
the faster on they go, And still beside them close I
keep Until we reach the Town of Sleep.
<|endoftext|>

Three of us afloat
in the meadow by
the swing.
<|endoftext|>

Three of us
aboard in the basket
on the lea.
<|endoftext|>

Winds are in the air, they are blowing
in the spring, And waves are on the meadow
like the waves there are at sea.
<|endoftext|>

Where shall we adventure,
to-day that we're afloat, Wary of the weather
and steering by a star?
<|endoftext|>

Shall it be to Africa,
a-steering of the boat, To Providence, or
Babylon, or off to Malabar?
<|endoftext|>

but here's a squadron
a-rowing on the sea-- Cattle on the
meadow a-charging with a roar!
<|endoftext|>

Quick, and we'll escape them, they're as
mad as they can be, The wicket is the harbor
and the garden is the shore.
<|endoftext|>

The coach is at the door at last;
The eager children, mounting fast And kissing hands,
in chorus sing: Good-bye, good-bye, to everything!
<|endoftext|>

To house and garden, field and lawn,
The meadow-gates we swung upon, To pump and stable, tree
and swing, Good-bye, good-bye, to everything!
<|endoftext|>

And fare you well for evermore, O
ladder at the hayloft door, O hayloft where the cobwebs
cling, Good-bye, good-bye, to everything!
<|endoftext|>

Crack goes the whip, and off we go; The
trees and houses smaller grow; Last, round the woody
turn we swing: Good-bye, good-bye, to everything!
<|endoftext|>

When I was sick and lay a-bed, I had
two pillows at my head, And all my toys beside me
lay To keep me happy all the day.
<|endoftext|>

And sometimes for an hour or so
I watched my leaden soldiers go, With different
uniforms and drills, Among the bed-clothes, through the hills.
<|endoftext|>

And sometimes sent my ships in fleets All up
and down among the sheets; Or brought my trees and houses
out, And planted cities all about.
<|endoftext|>

I was the giant great and still That
sits upon the pillow-hill, And sees before him, dale and
plain The pleasant Land of Counterpane.
<|endoftext|>

Come up
here, O
dusty feet!
<|endoftext|>

The lights from the parlor and kitchen shone out Through the
blinds and the windows and bars; And high over head and all moving
about, There were thousands of millions of stars.
<|endoftext|>

We built a ship upon the stairs
All made of the back-bedroom chairs, And filled it full of sofa pillows
To go a-sailing on the billows.
<|endoftext|>

We sailed along for days and days, And
had the very best of plays; But Tom fell out and hurt
his knee, So there was no one left but me.
<|endoftext|>

Bring the
comb and play upon
it!
<|endoftext|>

Marching,
here we
come!
<|endoftext|>

Willie cocks
his highland bonnet, Johnnie
beats the drum.
<|endoftext|>

Mary Jane commands the party,
Peter leads the rear; Feet in time, alert and
hearty, Each a Grenadier!
<|endoftext|>

All in the most martial manner
Marching double-quick; While the napkin like a
banner Waves upon the stick!
<|endoftext|>

Here's enough of fame
and pillage, Great
commander Jane!
<|endoftext|>

Now that we've been
round the village, Let's go
home again.
<|endoftext|>

Dark brown is
the river, Golden
is the sand.
<|endoftext|>

It flows along
for ever, With trees on either
hand.
<|endoftext|>

Green leaves a-floating, Castles
of the foam, Boats of mine a-boating--
Where will all come home?
<|endoftext|>

On goes the river And out
past the mill, Away down the valley,
Away down the hill.
<|endoftext|>

Away down the river, A
hundred miles or more, Other little
children Shall bring my boats ashore.
<|endoftext|>

Through all the pleasant meadow-side
The grass grew shoulder-high, Till the shining scythes went far and
wide And cut it down to dry.
<|endoftext|>

These green and sweetly smelling crops
They led in wagons home; And they piled them here
in mountain-tops For mountaineers to roam.
<|endoftext|>

Here is Mount Clear, Mount Rusty-Nail, Mount Eagle
and Mount High;-- The mice that in these mountains dwell,
No happier are than I!
<|endoftext|>

O what a joy to clamber there,
O what a place for play, With the sweet, the dim, the
dusty air, The happy hills of hay!
<|endoftext|>

Whenever Auntie
moves around Her dresses make
a curious sound.
<|endoftext|>

They trail behind
her up the floor, And trundle
after through the door.
<|endoftext|>

The friendly cow all red and white,
I love with all my heart: She gives me cream with all
her might, To eat with apple-tart.
<|endoftext|>

She wanders lowing here and there, And
yet she cannot stray, All in the pleasant open
air, The pleasant light of day.
<|endoftext|>

And blown by all the winds that pass
And wet with all the showers, She walks among the
meadow grass And eats the meadow flowers.
<|endoftext|>

Up into the
cherry tree Who should climb
but little me?
<|endoftext|>

I held the trunk
with both my hands And looked abroad
on foreign lands.
<|endoftext|>

I saw the next door garden lie, Adorned
with flowers, before my eye, And many pleasant places
more That I had never seen before.
<|endoftext|>

I saw the dimpling river pass And
be the sky's blue looking-glass; The dusty roads go up
and down With people tramping into town.
<|endoftext|>

If I could find a higher tree Farther
and farther I should see, To where the grown-up river slips
Into the sea among the ships.
<|endoftext|>

To where the roads on either hand Lead
onward into fairy land, Where all the children dine
at five, And all the playthings come alive.
<|endoftext|>

Every night my prayers I say, And
get my dinner every day; And every
day that I've been good I get an orange after food.
<|endoftext|>

The child that is not clean and neat, With
lots of toys and things to eat, He is a naughty child, I'm
sure-- Or else his dear papa is poor.
<|endoftext|>

My holes were hollow like a
cup, In every hole the sea came
up, Till it could hold no more.
<|endoftext|>

All by myself I have to go, With
none to tell me what to do-- All alone beside
the streams And up the mountain-sides of dreams.
<|endoftext|>

The strangest things are there for me, Both
things to eat and things to see, And many frightening
sights abroad Till morning in the Land of Nod.
<|endoftext|>

Try as I like to find the way, I
never can get back by day, Nor can remember plain and
clear The curious music that I hear.
<|endoftext|>

Whenever the moon and stars are
set, Whenever the wind is high, All night long in the
dark and wet, A man goes riding by.
<|endoftext|>

Late in the night when the
fires are out, Why does he gallop
and gallop about?
<|endoftext|>

Whenever the trees are crying aloud,
And ships are tossed at sea, By, on the highway, low
and loud, By at the gallop goes he.
<|endoftext|>

By at the gallop he
goes, and then By he comes back at
the gallop again.
<|endoftext|>

A birdie with a yellow bill Hopped
up on the window sill, Cocked his shining eye
and said: 'Ain't you 'shamed, you sleepy-head?
<|endoftext|>

' The rain is
raining all
around.
<|endoftext|>

It falls on field and
tree, It rains on the umbrellas here,
And on the ships at sea.
<|endoftext|>

Little Indian, Sioux
or Crow, Little frosty Eskimo, Little
Turk or Japanee, O!
<|endoftext|>

don't you wish
that you
were me?
<|endoftext|>

You have seen the scarlet trees And the
lions over seas; You have eaten ostrich eggs,
And turned the turtles off their legs.
<|endoftext|>

Such a life is very fine, But it's
not so nice as mine: You must often, as you trod,
Have wearied _not_ to be abroad.
<|endoftext|>

You have curious things to eat,
I am fed on proper meat; You must dwell beyond
the foam, But I am safe and live at home.
<|endoftext|>

When I am grown to man's estate
I shall be very proud and great, And tell the other
girls and boys Not to meddle with my toys.
<|endoftext|>

I have a little shadow that
goes in and out with me, And what can be the
use of him is more than I can see.
<|endoftext|>

He is very, very like me, from
the heels up to the head; And I see him jump before
me, when I jump into my bed.
<|endoftext|>

He hasn't got a notion of how
children ought to play, And can only make a
fool of me in every sort of way.
<|endoftext|>

He stays so close beside me, he's a coward
you can see; I'd think shame to stick to nursie
as that shadow sticks to me!
<|endoftext|>

The sun is not a-bed when I At night
upon my pillow lie; Still round the earth his way
he takes, And morning after morning makes.
<|endoftext|>

While here at home in shining day, We round
the sunny garden play, Each little Indian sleepy-head
Is being kissed and put to bed.
<|endoftext|>

And when at eve I rise from tea, Day dawns
beyond the Atlantic Sea; And all the children in the
West Are getting up and being dressed.
<|endoftext|>

Smooth it slides upon
its travel, Here a wimple, there a gleam--
O the clean gravel!
<|endoftext|>

O the
smooth
stream!
<|endoftext|>

Sailing blossoms, silver fishes,
Paven pools as clear as air-- How a
child wishes To live down there!
<|endoftext|>

See the rings pursue each other;
All below grows black as night, Just as if mother
Had blown out the light!
<|endoftext|>

Patience, children, just a minute--
See the spreading circles die; The stream
and all in it Will clear by-and-by.
<|endoftext|>

Of speckled eggs the birdie sings
And nests among the trees; The sailor sings of
ropes and things In ships upon the seas.
<|endoftext|>

The children sing in far Japan, The
children sing in Spain; The organ with the organ
man Is singing in the rain.
<|endoftext|>

My bed is like a little boat;
Nurse helps me in when I embark; She girds me in
my sailor's coat And starts me in the dark.
<|endoftext|>

At night, I go on board and say Good
night to all my friends on shore; I shut my eyes and sail
away And see and hear no more.
<|endoftext|>

And sometimes things to bed I take, As
prudent sailors have to do; Perhaps a slice
of wedding-cake, Perhaps a toy or two.
<|endoftext|>

All night across the dark we steer: But
when the day returns at last Safe in my room, beside
the pier, I find my vessel fast.
<|endoftext|>

Over the borders, a sin without pardon, Breaking
the branches and crawling below, Out through the breach in the wall of the
garden, Down by the banks of the river, we go.
<|endoftext|>

Here is the mill with the humming of thunder, Here is
the weir with the wonder of foam, Here is the sluice with the race running
under-- Marvelous places, though handy to home!
<|endoftext|>

Years may go by, and the
wheel in the river Wheel as it wheels
for us, children, to-day.
<|endoftext|>

Wheel and keep roaring and
foaming for ever Long after
all of the boys are away.
<|endoftext|>

Home from the Indies and home from the ocean, Heroes
and soldiers we all shall come home; Still we shall find the old mill wheel in
motion, Turning and churning that river to foam.
<|endoftext|>

You with the bean that I gave when we quarreled, I
with your marble of Saturday last, Honored and old and all gaily
apparelled, Here we shall meet and remember the past.
<|endoftext|>

When children are playing alone
on the green, In comes the playmate
that never was seen.
<|endoftext|>

When children are happy and
lonely and good, The Friend of the Children
comes out of the wood.
<|endoftext|>

Nobody heard him and nobody saw, His is a
picture you never could draw, But he's sure to be present, abroad or at
home, When children are happy and playing alone.
<|endoftext|>

I saw you toss the kites on high And blow the
birds about the sky; And all around I heard you pass, Like ladies'
skirts across the grass-- O wind, a-blowing all day long!
<|endoftext|>

O wind,
that sings so
loud a song!
<|endoftext|>

I saw the different
things you did, But always you
yourself you hid.
<|endoftext|>

I felt you push, I heard you call,
I could not see yourself at all-- O wind, a-blowing all day
long, O wind, that sings so loud a song!
<|endoftext|>

O you that are so
strong and cold, O blower,
are you young or old?
<|endoftext|>

Are you a beast of
field and tree, Or just a stronger
child than me?
<|endoftext|>

O wind, a-blowing all
day long, O wind, that sings
so loud a song!
<|endoftext|>

I woke before the morning, I
was happy all the day, I never said an ugly
word, but smiled and stuck to play.
<|endoftext|>

And now at last the sun is going
down behind the wood, And I am very
happy, for I know that I've been good.
<|endoftext|>

My bed is waiting cool and fresh,
with linen smooth and fair, And I must off to
sleepsin-by, and not forget my prayer.
<|endoftext|>

I know that, till to-morrow I shall see the
sun arise, No ugly dream shall fright my
mind, no ugly sight my eyes.
<|endoftext|>

But slumber hold me tightly, till
I waken in the dawn, And hear the thrushes
singing in the lilacs round the lawn.
<|endoftext|>

Children, you are very little, And
your bones are very brittle; If you would grow great and stately,
You must try to walk sedately.
<|endoftext|>

You must still be bright and quiet, And content
with simple diet; And remain, through all bewild'ring,
Innocent and honest children.
<|endoftext|>

Happy hearts and happy faces, Happy
play in grassy places-- That was how, in ancient ages,
Children grew to kings and sages.
<|endoftext|>

But the unkind and the unruly, And
the sort who eat unduly, They must never hope for
glory-- Theirs is quite a different story!
<|endoftext|>

Cruel children, crying babies, All grow
up as geese and gabies, Hated, as their age
increases, By their nephews and their nieces.
<|endoftext|>

Summer fading, winter comes-- Frosty
mornings, tingling thumbs, Window robins, winter
rooks, And the picture story-books.
<|endoftext|>

Water now is turned to stone
Nurse and I can walk upon; Still we find the
flowing brooks In the picture story-books.
<|endoftext|>

All the pretty things put by
Wait upon the childrens' eye, Sheep and shepherds, trees and
crooks, In the picture story-books.
<|endoftext|>

We may see how all things are,
Seas and cities, near and far, And the flying fairies'
looks, In the picture story-books.
<|endoftext|>

How am I to sing your
praise, Happy chimney-corner days, Sitting safe in
nursery nooks, Reading picture story-books?
<|endoftext|>

How do you like to
go up in a swing, Up in
the air so blue?
<|endoftext|>

Oh, I do think it the
pleasantest thing Ever
a child can do!
<|endoftext|>

It is very nice to think The world
is full of meat and drink With little children saying
grace In every Christian kind of place.
<|endoftext|>

The lamps now glitter down the street; Faintly
sound the falling feet And the blue even slowly
falls About the garden trees and walls.
<|endoftext|>

Now in the falling of the gloom The
red fire paints the empty room; And warmly on the roof
it looks, And flickers on the backs of books.
<|endoftext|>

Armies march by tower and spire Of
cities blazing, in the fire;-- Till as I gaze with staring
eyes, The armies fade, the lustre dies.
<|endoftext|>

Then once again the glow returns;
Again the phantom city burns; And down
the red-hot valley, lo!
<|endoftext|>

The phantom
armies marching
go!
<|endoftext|>

Blinking embers, tell me true Where are
those armies marching to, And what the burning city
is That crumbles in your furnaces!
<|endoftext|>

Down by a shining water
well I found a very little dell, No
higher than my head.
<|endoftext|>

The heather and the gorse
about In summer bloom were coming out,
Some yellow and some red.
<|endoftext|>

I called the little pool a
sea; The little hills were big to me;
For I am very small.
<|endoftext|>

I made a boat, I made
a town, I searched the caverns up and
down, And named them one and all.
<|endoftext|>

And all about was mine,
I said, The little sparrows overhead,
The little minnows, too.
<|endoftext|>

This was the world and I was
king; For me the bees came by to sing,
For me the swallows flew.
<|endoftext|>

I played there were no deeper
seas, Nor any wider plains than these,
Nor other kings than me.
<|endoftext|>

At last I heard my mother
call Out from the house at evenfall, To
call me home to tea.
<|endoftext|>

And I must rise and leave
my dell, And leave my dimpled water well,
And leave my heather blooms.
<|endoftext|>

and as my home I neared, How
very big my nurse appeared, How
great and cool the rooms!
<|endoftext|>

All round the house is the jet-black night; It
stares through the window-pane; It crawls in the corners, hiding from the
light, And it moves with the moving flame.
<|endoftext|>

Now my little heart goes a-beating like a drum, With
the breath of Bogie in my hair, And all round the candle the crooked
shadows come, And go marching along up the stair.
<|endoftext|>

The shadow of the balusters, the shadow of the
lamp, The shadow of the child that goes to bed-- All the wicked shadows
coming, tramp, tramp, tramp, With the black night overhead.
<|endoftext|>

Late lies the wintry sun a-bed, A frosty,
fiery sleepy-head; Blinks but an hour or two;
and then, A blood-red orange, sets again.
<|endoftext|>

Before the stars have left the skies, At
morning in the dark I rise; And shivering in my
nakedness, By the cold candle, bathe and dress.
<|endoftext|>

Close by the jolly fire I sit
To warm my frozen bones a bit; Or with a reindeer-sled,
explore The colder countries round the door.
<|endoftext|>

When to go out, my nurse doth wrap Me in
my comforter and cap; The cold wind burns my face and
blows Its frosty pepper up my nose.
<|endoftext|>

Black are my steps on silver sod; Thick blows
my frosty breath abroad; And tree and house, and hill
and lake, Are frosted like a wedding-cake.
<|endoftext|>

I can in the
sorrel sit Where the
ladybird alit.
<|endoftext|>

I can climb the jointed grass; And on high
See the greater swallows pass In the sky, And the round sun
rolling by Heeding no such things as I.
<|endoftext|>

Through that forest I can pass Till, as in a
looking-glass, Humming fly and daisy tree And my tiny self I see, Painted
very clear and neat On the rain-pool at my feet.
<|endoftext|>

Should a leaflet come to land Drifting
near to where I stand, Straight I'll board that tiny
boat Round the rain-pool sea to float.
<|endoftext|>

Little thoughtful creatures sit On
the grassy coasts of it; Little things with lovely
eyes See me sailing with surprise.
<|endoftext|>

Some are clad in armour
green-- (These have sure to battle
been!
<|endoftext|>

)-- Some are pied with ev'ry hue, Black
and crimson, gold and blue; Some have wings and swift are
gone; But they all look kindly on.
<|endoftext|>

Last, to the chamber where I lie My
fearful footsteps patter nigh, And come from out the cold and
gloom Into my warm and cheerful room.
<|endoftext|>

There, safe arrived, we turn about To keep
the coming shadows out, And close the happy door at
last On all the perils that we passed.
<|endoftext|>

Then, when mamma goes by to bed, She shall
come in with tip-toe tread, And see me lying warm and
fast And in the Land of Nod at last.
<|endoftext|>

When the golden day is done, Through
the closing portal, Child and garden, flower
and sun, Vanish all things mortal.
<|endoftext|>

As the blinding showers fall,
As the rays diminish, Under evening's cloak they
all Roll away and vanish.
<|endoftext|>

Garden darkened, daisy shut,
Child in bed, they slumber-- Glow-worm in the highway
rut, Mice among the lumber.
<|endoftext|>

In the darkness houses shine, Parents
move with candles Till on all, the night divine
Turns the bedroom handles.
<|endoftext|>

Till at last the day begins
In the east a-breaking, In the hedges and
the whins Sleeping birds a-waking.
<|endoftext|>

In the darkness shapes of things, Houses,
trees and hedges, Clearer grow; and sparrow's
wings Beat on window ledges.
<|endoftext|>

These shall wake the yawning maid,
She the door shall open-- Finding dew on garden
glade And the morning broken.
<|endoftext|>

There my garden grows again Green and
rosy painted, As at eve behind the
pane From my eyes it fainted.
<|endoftext|>

Just as it was shut away,
Toy-like, in the even, Here I see it glow with
day Under glowing heaven.
<|endoftext|>

Every path and every
plot, Every bush of roses, Every
blue forget-me-not Where the dew reposes.
<|endoftext|>

they cry, 'the day is come On the
smiling valleys; We have beat the morning
drum; Playmate, join your allies!
<|endoftext|>

' Birds all the sunny day Flutter
and quarrel Here in the arbor-like
Tent of the laurel.
<|endoftext|>

Here in the fork The brown
nest is seated; Four little blue eggs
The mother keeps heated.
<|endoftext|>

While we stand watching her, Staring
like gabies, Safe in each egg are the
Bird's little babies.
<|endoftext|>

Soon the frail eggs they shall Chip,
and upspringing Make all the April

Younger than we are, O children,
and frailer, Soon in blue air they'll be,
Singer and sailor.

We, so much older, Taller

In spite of our wisdom
And sensible talking, We on our feet

Fair are grown-up people's trees, But the

I should live for good and all.

me; Till you gain the world beyond the town.

Then -- I fade from your
heart, quietly; And your
fleet steps quicken.
<|endoftext|>

The strong down Smiles you welcome there;
the woods that love you Close lovely and
conquering arms above you.
<|endoftext|>

O ever-moving,
O lithe
and free!
<|endoftext|>

Fast in my linen prison I press
On impassable bars, or emptily
Laugh in my great loneliness.
<|endoftext|>

And still in the white neat bed I strive Most
impotently against that gyve; Being less now than a thought, even,
To you alone with your hills and heaven.
<|endoftext|>

I Hot through Troy's ruin Menelaus
broke To Priam's palace, sword in hand, to sate On that adulterous
whore a ten years' hate And a king's honour.
<|endoftext|>

Through red death, and smoke, And cries, and then
by quieter ways he strode, Till the still
innermost chamber fronted him.
<|endoftext|>

He swung his sword, and crashed into
the dim Luxurious bower,
flaming like a god.
<|endoftext|>

High sat white
Helen, lonely
and serene.
<|endoftext|>

He had not remembered that she
was so fair, And that her neck curved down in such
a way; And he felt tired.
<|endoftext|>

He flung the sword away, And
kissed her feet, and knelt before her there, The
perfect Knight before the perfect Queen.
<|endoftext|>

II So
far the
poet.
<|endoftext|>

How should he behold
That journey home, the long
connubial years?
<|endoftext|>

He does not tell you how white Helen
bears Child on legitimate child, becomes
a scold, Haggard with virtue.
<|endoftext|>

And her golden
voice Got shrill as he
grew deafer.
<|endoftext|>

And
both
were old.
<|endoftext|>

Often he wonders
why on earth he went Troyward, or why
poor Paris ever came.
<|endoftext|>

Oft she weeps, gummy-eyed
and impotent; Her dry shanks twitch
at Paris' mumbled name.
<|endoftext|>

So Menelaus nagged;
and Helen cried; And Paris slept on
by Scamander side.
<|endoftext|>

How should
I
know?
<|endoftext|>

The enormous wheels
of will Drove me cold-eyed on tired
and sleepless feet.
<|endoftext|>

Night was void arms and
you a phantom still, And day your far
light swaying down the street.
<|endoftext|>

As never fool for
love, I starved for you; My throat was dry and
my eyes hot to see.
<|endoftext|>

Your mouth so lying was most
heaven in view, And your remembered
smell most agony.
<|endoftext|>

I felt your hot wrist shiver
And suddenly the mad victory I planned Flashed
real, in your burning bending head.
<|endoftext|>

My conqueror's blood was cool as a deep
river In shadow; and my heart beneath your hand
Quieter than a dead man on a bed.
<|endoftext|>

Day after day you'll sit with him and note The
greasier tie, the dingy wrinkling coat; As prettiness turns to
pomp, and strength to fat, And love, love, love to habit!
<|endoftext|>

O lithe and free And lightfoot,
that the poor heart cries to see, That's how
I'll see your man and you!
<|endoftext|>

-- But you -- Oh, when
THAT time comes, you'll
be dirty too!
<|endoftext|>

My restless blood now lies a-quiver, Knowing that
always, exquisitely, This April twilight on the river
Stirs anguish in the heart of me.
<|endoftext|>

And all these, like a waiting lover,
Murmur and gleam, lift lustrous eyes, Drift close to me, and
sideways bending Whisper delicious words.
<|endoftext|>

But I Stretch terrible
hands, uncomprehending, Shaken with
love; and laugh; and cry.
<|endoftext|>

And Hatred followed
after, Shrilling madly
down the breeze.
<|endoftext|>

In peace from the wild heart of clamour, A
flower in moonlight, she was there, Was rippling down white ways
of glamour Quietly laid on wave and air.
<|endoftext|>

Her passing
left no
leaf a-quiver.
<|endoftext|>

Pale flowers
wreathed her white,
white brows.
<|endoftext|>

Her feet were
silence on the
river; And "Hush!
<|endoftext|>

" she
said, between
the boughs.
<|endoftext|>

In darkness the loud sea
makes moan; And earth is shaken, and all evils
creep About her ways.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, now
to know you
sleep!
<|endoftext|>

Your magic and your beauty and
your strength, Like hills at noon or sunlight on a
tree, Sleeping prevail in earth and air.
<|endoftext|>

In the sweet gloom above the brown and white
Night benedictions hover; and the winds of night Move
gently round the room, and watch you there.
<|endoftext|>

And still
the darkness ebbs about
your bed.
<|endoftext|>

Quiet, and
strange, and loving-kind,
you sleep.
<|endoftext|>

And holy joy about
the earth is shed; And holiness
upon the deep.
<|endoftext|>

From the candles and dumb shadows,
And the house where love had died, I stole to the vast moonlight
And the whispering life outside.
<|endoftext|>

But I found no lips of comfort, No home
in the moon's light (I, little and lone and frightened In the unfriendly
night), And no meaning in the voices.
<|endoftext|>

Far over the lands and
through The dark, beyond the ocean, I
willed to think of YOU!
<|endoftext|>

For I knew, had you been with me I'd
have known the words of night, Found peace of heart, gone
gladly In comfort of that light.
<|endoftext|>

But ever Desperately
I willed; Till all grew soft and
far And silent .
<|endoftext|>

And suddenly I found you
white and radiant, Sleeping quietly,
Far out through the tides of darkness.
<|endoftext|>

Love," they said,
"is King of Kings, And Triumph
is his crown.
<|endoftext|>

Earth fades in flame before
his wings, And Sun and
Moon bow down.
<|endoftext|>

" -- But that, I knew, would
never do; And Heaven
is all too high.
<|endoftext|>

So whenever I
meet a Queen, I said, I will
not catch her eye.
<|endoftext|>

Love," they said, and "Love," they said, "The gift
of Love is this; A crown of thorns about thy
head, And vinegar to thy kiss!
<|endoftext|>

" -- But Tragedy
is not for me; And I'm content
to be gay.
<|endoftext|>

So whenever I
spied a Tragic Lady, I
went another way.
<|endoftext|>

And so I never feared to see
You wander down the street, Or come across the
fields to me On ordinary feet.
<|endoftext|>

For what they'd never told me of,
And what I never knew; It was that all the
time, my love, Love would be merely you.
<|endoftext|>

Safe in the magic
of my woods I lay, and watched
the dying light.
<|endoftext|>

Faint in the pale high solitudes,
And washed with rain and veiled by night, Silver
and blue and green were showing.
<|endoftext|>

The spell was broken, the key denied
me And at length your flat clear voice beside me
Mouthed cheerful clear flat platitudes.

You came and
quacked beside me
in the wood.

very good!
<|endoftext|>

" You said, "It's
nice to be alone
a bit!
<|endoftext|>

" And, "How
the days are
drawing out!

You said,
"The sunset's pretty, isn't
it?
<|endoftext|>

I wish

were dead!

Lip and eye Flashed on the glory, shone and
cried, Improvident, unmemoried; And fitfully and like

Proud in their careless
transience moved The changing
faces that I loved.

otherwhence, I looked upon

an instant, knew
As God knows all.

And it
and you I, above

<|endoftext|>

could see
In witless

Light was more

<|endoftext|>

For suddenly, and
otherwhence, I looked on your

Light glinted

The eternal holiness
of you, The timeless end, you never knew,
The peace that lay, the light that shone.

You never knew that
I had gone A million miles

The laughter played
Unbroken round me;
and the jest Flashed on.

And we that knew the

I sang at heart, and talked, and

When you were there, and you, and you.

In a flowered dell

Amazed with sorrow.

She thought the

<|endoftext|>

Wing, and leaf,

Forgot to dance.

Life one eternal instant rose in dream Clear out

Till a swift

<|endoftext|>

The gold waves purled

And a bird sang.

With one sharp-taken breath, By sunlit branches and

And the immortal eyes to look on death.

The damned
ship lurched
and slithered.
<|endoftext|>

Quiet and quick My cold gorge rose; the long
sea rolled; I knew I must think hard of something, or be sick;
And could think hard of only one thing -- YOU!
<|endoftext|>

You, you alone
could hold my fancy
ever!
<|endoftext|>

And with you

sharp pain, and dole.
<|endoftext|>

Now there's a

tortured liver!
<|endoftext|>

A sea-sick body,

Retchings twist and tie

And still

<|endoftext|>

'Tis hard, I tell ye,
To choose 'twixt love and nausea,
heart and belly.

All night the ways of Heaven
were desolate, Long roads across a
gleaming empty sky.
<|endoftext|>

Outcast and doomed and driven, you and I,
Alone, serene beyond all love or hate, Terror or triumph,
were content to wait, We, silent and all-knowing.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly Swept through the heaven
low-crouching from on high, One horseman, downward
to the earth's low gate.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, perfect from the ultimate
height of living, Lightly we turned, through wet woods
blossom-hung, Into the open.
<|endoftext|>

Down the supernal roads, With plumes a-tossing, purple

Thundered the black battalions of the Gods.
<|endoftext|>

Through my heart's palace Thoughts unnumbered
throng; And there, most quiet and, as a child, most wise,
High-throned you sit, and gracious.
<|endoftext|>

All day long Great Hopes gold-armoured, jester
Fantasies, And pilgrim Dreams, and little beggar
Sighs, Bow to your benediction, go their way.
<|endoftext|>

And the grave jewelled
courtier Memories Worship and
love and tend you, all the day.
<|endoftext|>

not now, when desire burns, and the

whisper of life, woo me to wayfaring; Ah!
<|endoftext|>

not now should you come, now when the road
beckons, and good friends call, Where are songs to
be sung, fights to be fought, yea!
<|endoftext|>

and the best of all, Love,
on myriad lips fairer than

<|endoftext|>

Dearest, why should I
mourn, whimper, and whine, I
that have yet to live?
<|endoftext|>

Sorrow will I forget, tears for the best, love on

up the eastern blue; I'll forget and be glad!
<|endoftext|>

Still I abode strong
in a golden dream,
Unrecaptured.
<|endoftext|>

and, in the

labouring, you!
<|endoftext|>

Therefore I kept
ready the altar, lit
The flame, burning apart.
<|endoftext|>

Face of my dreams
vainly in vision white Gleaming
down to me, lo!
<|endoftext|>

boughs above Grated, cries like a laugh.
<|endoftext|>

Silent and black then through the

troubling the leaves, passing at length.
<|endoftext|>

Therefore I go; never to
rest, or win Peace, and worship of you more, and the
dumb wood and the shrine therein.
<|endoftext|>

So light we were, so right we were, so
fair faith shone, And the way was laid so certainly, that, when
I'd gone, What dumb thing looked up at you?
<|endoftext|>

Was it something heard, Or a sudden
cry, that meekly and without a word You broke the
faith, and strangely, weakly, slipped apart.
<|endoftext|>

You gave in
-- you, the proud of heart, unbowed
of heart!
<|endoftext|>

Was this, friend,
the end of all that
we could do?
<|endoftext|>

And have you found
the best for you,
the rest for you?
<|endoftext|>

so suddenly
(and I not by!
<|endoftext|>

) Some whispered story, that stole the glory from

the faith remains, and
I must pass Gay down the

<|endoftext|>

Under the grass You wait; the breeze
moves in the trees, and stirs, and calls, And covers you

<|endoftext|>

Good
sleep to
you!
<|endoftext|>

We have built

Time's throwing.
<|endoftext|>

We have gained a

<|endoftext|>

War
knows no

<|endoftext|>

And if these poor limbs die, safest of all.
<|endoftext|>

Blow out, you
bugles, over the
rich Dead!
<|endoftext|>

There's none of these so lonely
and poor of old, But, dying, has made us

<|endoftext|>

Blow,
bugles,
blow!
<|endoftext|>

so long, and Love, and Pain.
<|endoftext|>

Honour has come back, as a king, to earth, And

<|endoftext|>

These hearts were woven of
human joys and cares, Washed marvellously
with sorrow, swift to mirth.
<|endoftext|>

The years
had given them
kindness.
<|endoftext|>

Dawn was theirs,
And sunset, and the colours

These had seen movement, and heard music; known Slumber

sat alone; Touched flowers and furs and cheeks.
<|endoftext|>

All
this is
ended.
<|endoftext|>

There are waters blown
by changing winds to laughter And

<|endoftext|>

And after, Frost, with a
gesture, stays the waves that dance And
wandering loveliness.
<|endoftext|>

He leaves a white Unbroken glory,
a gathered radiance, A width, a

<|endoftext|>

If I should die, think only
this of me: That there's some corner of a foreign
field That is for ever England.
<|endoftext|>

Mamua, when our laughter ends, And
hearts and bodies, brown as white, Are dust about the doors of
friends, Or scent ablowing down the night, Then, oh!
<|endoftext|>

then, the wise agree,
Comes our
immortality.
<|endoftext|>

Mamua, there
waits a land Hard for us
to understand.
<|endoftext|>

Out of time, beyond the sun, All
are one in Paradise, You and Pupure are
one, And Tau, and the ungainly wise.
<|endoftext|>

And my laughter, and
my pain, Shall home to the
Eternal Brain.
<|endoftext|>

And there'll no more be one who dreams
Under the ferns, of crumbling stuff, Eyes of illusion,
mouth that seems, All time-entangled human love.
<|endoftext|>

Divinely down the scented shade, Where feet to Ambulation

<|endoftext|>

-- but we'll be missing The palms, and sunlight,
and the south; And there's an end, I think, of kissing,
When our mouths are one with Mouth.

moon, And the whispering scents that stray About

<|endoftext|>

mind of foolishness, Mamua, until the day.
<|endoftext|>

Pursuing down the soundless deep Limbs that gleam and
shadowy hair, Or floating lazy, half-asleep.
<|endoftext|>

individual, Well this side of Paradise!
<|endoftext|>

There's little
comfort in
the wise.
<|endoftext|>

Love, in you, went passing by, Penetrative,
remote, and rare, Like a bird in the wide air, And, as the bird,
it left no trace In the heaven of your face.
<|endoftext|>

In your stupidity
I found The sweet hush after
a sweet sound.
<|endoftext|>

All about you was the light That dims the greying end
of night; Desire was the unrisen sun, Joy the day not yet begun, With
tree whispering to tree, Without wind, quietly.
<|endoftext|>

Wisdom slept within your hair, And Long-Suffering
was there, And, in the flowing of your dress,
Undiscerning Tenderness.
<|endoftext|>

Infinitely, and like a sea, About the slight world you
had known Your vast unconsciousness was thrown.

O haven
without wave or
tide!
<|endoftext|>

Silence,

have died!
<|endoftext|>

Holy
book, where

<|endoftext|>

And home
at length under

O mother quiet,
breasts of peace, Where love itself

Now, ere the unthinking silence on that strife Steals

Love is a

A city:

these and I.

" Mataiea, 1914 Fish (fly-replete, in depth of June, Dawdling
away their wat'ry noon) Ponder deep wisdom, dark or clear, Each secret
fishy hope or fear.
<|endoftext|>

Fish say, they have their
Stream and Pond; But is there
anything Beyond?
<|endoftext|>

This life cannot be All,
they swear, For how unpleasant,
if it were!
<|endoftext|>

One may not doubt that, somehow, Good Shall come
of Water and of Mud; And, sure, the reverent eye must
see A Purpose in Liquidity.
<|endoftext|>

We darkly know,
by Faith we cry, The future is
not Wholly Dry.
<|endoftext|>

Mud
unto
mud!
<|endoftext|>

-- Death eddies near
-- Not here the appointed
End, not here!
<|endoftext|>

But somewhere,
beyond Space
and Time.
<|endoftext|>

Is wetter
water,
slimier slime!
<|endoftext|>

And there (they trust) there swimmeth One Who swam ere rivers were begun,
Immense, of fishy form and mind, Squamous, omnipotent, and kind; And under that
Almighty Fin, The littlest fish may enter in.
<|endoftext|>

And in that Heaven
of all their wish, There shall be
no more land, say fish.
<|endoftext|>

This I know, and
yet I know Doubts that will
not be denied.
<|endoftext|>

For if the soul
be not in place, What has laid
trouble in her face?
<|endoftext|>

And if the spirit
be not there, Why is fragrance
in the hair?
<|endoftext|>

"Oh love is fair, and love
is rare;" my dear one she said, "But love
goes lightly over.
<|endoftext|>

"  I bowed her foolish
head, And kissed her hair
and laughed at her.
<|endoftext|>

Such a child was she;
So new to love, so true to love, and
she spoke so bitterly.
<|endoftext|>

I have peace to weigh your
worth, now all is over, But if to praise
or blame you, cannot say.
<|endoftext|>

For, who decries the loved, decries
the lover; Yet what man lauds the
thing he's thrown away?
<|endoftext|>

Dear fool, pity
the fool who thought
you clever!
<|endoftext|>

Dear wisdom, do
not mock the fool
that missed you!
<|endoftext|>

Most fair, -- the blind
has lost your face
for ever!
<|endoftext|>

Most foul, -- how
could I see you while
I kissed you?
<|endoftext|>

the poor love of fools and blind
I've proved you, For, foul or lovely,
'twas a fool that loved you.
<|endoftext|>

Somewhile before the dawn I rose, and stept Softly
along the dim way to your room, And found you sleeping in the quiet
gloom, And holiness about you as you slept.
<|endoftext|>

I knelt there; till your
waking fingers crept About
my head, and held it.
<|endoftext|>

I had rest Unhoped
this side of Heaven, beneath
your breast.
<|endoftext|>

I knelt a
long time, still; nor
even wept.
<|endoftext|>

It was great wrong you did me;
and for gain Of that poor moment's kindliness,
and ease, And sleepy mother-comfort!
<|endoftext|>

Child, you know How easily
love leaps out to dreams like these,
Who has seen them true.
<|endoftext|>

And love that's wakened
so Takes all too long to
lay asleep again.

Waikiki, October 1913
Today I have been
happy.
<|endoftext|>

The Pacific, October 1913 Warm
perfumes like a breath from vine and tree Drift down the
darkness.
<|endoftext|>

Plangent, hidden from eyes Somewhere
an 'eukaleli' thrills and cries And stabs with pain
the night's brown savagery.
<|endoftext|>

And dark scents whisper; and dim waves creep to me, Gleam like
a woman's hair, stretch out, and rise; And new stars burn into the ancient
skies, Over the murmurous soft Hawaian sea.
<|endoftext|>

Down the blue night the unending columns press In
noiseless tumult, break and wave and flow, Now tread the far South, or lift
rounds of snow Up to the white moon's hidden loveliness.
<|endoftext|>

Some pause in their grave wandering comradeless, And
turn with profound gesture vague and slow, As who would pray good for the
world, but know Their benediction empty as they bless.
<|endoftext|>

They say that the Dead
die not, but remain Near to the rich
heirs of their grief and mirth.
<|endoftext|>

I think they ride the calm mid-heaven, as these, In
wise majestic melancholy train, And watch the moon, and the still-raging seas,
And men, coming and going on the earth.
<|endoftext|>

There the sure suns of these pale shadows move; There stand
the immortal ensigns of our war; Our melting flesh fixed Beauty
there, a star, And perishing hearts, imperishable Love.
<|endoftext|>

Dear, we know only that we sigh, kiss,
smile; Each kiss lasts but the kissing; and grief goes over;
Love has no habitation but the heart.
<|endoftext|>

on the dark flood we catch
awhile, Cling, and are borne into
the night apart.
<|endoftext|>

The laugh dies
with the lips, 'Love' with
the lover.
<|endoftext|>

South Kensington -- Makaweli, 1913 Now that
we've done our best and worst, and parted, I would fill my mind with
thoughts that will not rend.
<|endoftext|>

I would think of a thousand things, Lovely
and durable, and taste them slowly, One after
one, like tasting a sweet food.
<|endoftext|>

I have need
to busy my heart
with quietude.
<|endoftext|>

Love is a breach in the walls, a broken
gate, Where that comes in that shall not go again; Love sells
the proud heart's citadel to Fate.
<|endoftext|>

They have
known shame, who
love unloved.
<|endoftext|>

Some
share
that night.
<|endoftext|>

But they know love grows
colder, Grows false and dull, that
was sweet lies at most.
<|endoftext|>

Astonishment is no
more in hand or shoulder, But darkens, and
dies out from kiss to kiss.
<|endoftext|>

All this is
love; and all love
is but this.
<|endoftext|>

Heart, you are restless as a paper
scrap That's tossed down dusty pavements by the wind; Saying,
"She is most wise, patient and kind.
<|endoftext|>

Come to
her, come
to her!
<|endoftext|>

She will
not
care.
<|endoftext|>

She'll smile to see me come,
So that I think all Heaven
in flower to fold me.
<|endoftext|>

She'll give me all I ask, kiss me and hold me,
And open wide upon that holy air The gates of peace,
and take my tiredness home, Kinder than God.
<|endoftext|>

But, heart,
she will not
care.
<|endoftext|>

Your hands, my dear, adorable, Your
lips of tenderness -- Oh, I've loved you faithfully
and well, Three years, or a bit less.
<|endoftext|>

Thank
God,
that's done!
<|endoftext|>

and I'll take the road, Quit of my youth
and you, The Roman road to Wendover By Tring and
Lilley Hoo, As a free man may do.
<|endoftext|>

For youth goes over, the joys that fly, The tears
that follow fast; And the dirtiest things we do must lie
Forgotten at the last; Even Love goes past.
<|endoftext|>

What's left behind I shall not find, The splendour
and the pain; The splash of sun, the shouting wind, And the brave
sting of rain, I may not meet again.
<|endoftext|>

But the years, that take the best away, Give something
in the end; And a better friend than love have they, For none to
mar or mend, That have themselves to friend.
<|endoftext|>

I shall desire and I shall find
The best of my desires; The autumn road, the mellow
wind That soothes the darkening shires.
<|endoftext|>

And
laughter, and

than the lamp, It is the beams that lead
to progress, count.

" The ages

Earth to brake their fall.

The regions gave And
sank with all the hosts

<|endoftext|>

'Tis in those sunken regions which divide The new

Old
Guard of

Halt;

Attention!

Order One: quell the weeds

Brown, in conscience, quiet.

John's

Ye, on pension
For ancient glory,
now do duty.
<|endoftext|>

Mention Elisha's
name for countersign--and
why, it?
<|endoftext|>

Because with him, wrong, seen,
was to defy it, And act, else, was
beyond his comprehension.
<|endoftext|>

What is it that today we celebrate
With school recital, banquet and parade Of our
achievements, pageanting each trade?
<|endoftext|>

If not un-Englishing America in
mind And heart forever, vain the shrieks Of Freedom,
eagling back to dawn's first streaks.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, yea, the sun stands, and the night afar
Holds Thrall, whose craft would swamp our noblest peaks And leave
but bubbling mud show where they are!
<|endoftext|>

Glory flings his arms
round thee And proudly holds thee
in his high caress.
<|endoftext|>

What charms him, Mother,
is thy nobleness Of
spirit.

" In

glory.

Can the world else boast A harbor, like thy
heart, for every sail In flight from sea-toss, white with horror's
gale, Or icebergs from despondence Polar coast?
<|endoftext|>

Oh, fleets whose throngs, glad Freedom
well may hail; For, landing, they became
her staunchest host.
<|endoftext|>

With what wild glee, the British set on fire Yon
Capital, beholding in its flames, America, robed in her deeds
and fames, In death throes at the stake of England's ire?
<|endoftext|>

Though that was long ago and, then no pyre, The
stake still stands; 'tis Anglo-Saxon claims, And Arnolds, bearing infamy's last names,
Tilt schools to raise the stake flames high and higher.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, sight to strike the coming ages dead, My country,
were a cloud, thy mocking crown, And schools, ignited by Truth's lamps hurled
down, To feed that cloud, like craters, inly red!
<|endoftext|>

mock with cloud, Thy land and sea renown
And Washington, God's Holy Spirit--known By the

<|endoftext|>

Behold Ye Here the Happy Hunting Grounds, Where the Great
Spirit, called Democracy, Sets every heart and soul forever
free, An Equity, not royal grant, sets bounds.
<|endoftext|>

No Phaeton attempting Phoebus rounds And burning
up earth's grass and forestry, Is lust for power; 'tis love for

land of
Spirit.

from where prejudice is mentor.

a note.
<|endoftext|>

Meanwhile, brisk Freedom's fleets with justice manned,
And cosmic full momentum for their speed, Confront
the crafts, fired up by fiendish Greed.
<|endoftext|>

A
clash
and--lo!
<|endoftext|>

they pass the strait and land, Leaving in

thrall along time's vultured strand.
<|endoftext|>

Are lust for Gold and Power
not hideous spawn Of prehistoric
reptiles, that had wings?
<|endoftext|>

Where e'er those crawled, they chawed all greening things And,
when they mounted, how their lengths, full drawn, Basked barren in the sun before
the dawn, Absorbing all its rays from budding Springs?
<|endoftext|>

These drain life's dawn and by
impoverishings, Draw and reduce
to pulp, frail Consciences.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, how long a time Shall reptiles,
deadly to the Human race, Be let grow
wings and heavenward trail their slime?
<|endoftext|>

What though fine graphic sketches
In magazines show them with shoulders bold Against
the nights flood-gates of dark and cold?
<|endoftext|>

All effort
is but life in death-throw
stretches.
<|endoftext|>

O heart and brain, who see the father
load His train with food, not for the few, but all, And hear
train-whistlings in March winds, jay call And ground-hog sniffs!
<|endoftext|>

O heart and brain, it
rest with you, how long The legislative

<|endoftext|>

Ye have
the power to
balk them.
<|endoftext|>

Regain
your

<|endoftext|>

Man them strong And drive thence all

to fastnesses of wrong.
<|endoftext|>

<|endoftext|>

The red paint on her

mark.
<|endoftext|>

Alas, that she, Born to bring forth the truth,
still, is so base, She kills her child and, then, to hide all trace,
Cracks bone by bone to dust, too fine to see.
<|endoftext|>

O Press, poor harlot of

<|endoftext|>

life--oh, bring it forth!
<|endoftext|>

aye, hold Thy

<|endoftext|>

Crush not

<|endoftext|>

The focus of

and the soul and mind.
<|endoftext|>

It is the Sun of

and all the worlds.
<|endoftext|>

Such is its blaze, That had it not, at intervals,

blind, To grope in Heaven, a Homer, sighing lays.
<|endoftext|>

What less could fitly crown

of all rays in Good?
<|endoftext|>

there it shines upon the Holy Rood, Breaking through

With rays of pardon for the World's offense.
<|endoftext|>

They
know not what
they do.
<|endoftext|>

All grandeurs, gathered as a dazzling

Demon dashed to fragments to Time's end.
<|endoftext|>

Thought is truth's echo--not her

your Beauty and your Might-- Truth

<|endoftext|>

" Ah,

<|endoftext|>

Such Glory that Sun-light

Music, shell-shut sound.

profound!

<|endoftext|>

If "Holy,
Holy, Holy,

<|endoftext|>

Ah, by what other pass, are men to fare
Through mist and cloud, except the path, aflare With his

When, at God's fiat, Light flashed

For, harboring

<|endoftext|>

A spirit passed the Sun,

Ah, if where love was not, he

There are

in the dimple of
