cheer Of the might and light of the present and here.
<|endoftext|>

There is shining of flowers in the deep wet woods,
In the heart of the sensitive solitudes, The roseate bell and
the lily are there, And every leaf of their sheaf is fair.
<|endoftext|>

Careless and bold, without dream of woe, The
trilliums scatter their flags snow; But the pale wood-daffodil covers her
face, Agloom with the doom of a sorrowful race.
<|endoftext|>

The vesper-sparrow's song, the stress Of yearning
notes that gush and stream, The lyric joy, the
tenderness, And once again the dream!
<|endoftext|>

A touch of far-off joy and power, A
something it is life to learn, Comes back to earth, and one
short hour The glamours of the gods return.
<|endoftext|>

This life's old mood and cult of care Falls
smitten by an older truth, And the gray world wins back
to her The rapture of her vanished youth.
<|endoftext|>

Dead thoughts revive, and he that heeds Shall hear,
as by a spirit led, A song among the golden
reeds: "The gods are vanished but not dead!
<|endoftext|>

" For one short hour; unseen yet near,
They haunt us, a forgotten mood, A glory upon
mead and mere, A magic in the leafless wood.
<|endoftext|>

At morning we shall catch the glow Of
Dian's quiver on the hill, And somewhere in the glades
I know That Pan is at his piping still.
<|endoftext|>

" Now though no more by marsh or stream Or dewy
forest sounds the secret reed-- For Pan is gone--Ah yet, the
infinite dream Still lives for them that heed.
<|endoftext|>

An old-world joyousness supreme, The warmth
and glow of an immortal balm, The mood-touch of the gods, the
endless dream, The high lethean calm.
<|endoftext|>

They see, wide on the eternal way, The
services of earth, the life of man; And, listening to the
magic cry they say: "It is the note of Pan!
<|endoftext|>

Here the slim-pinioned swallows sweep and pass Down to
the far-off river; the black crow With wise and wary visage to and fro
Settles and stalks about the withered grass.
<|endoftext|>

Here, too, the robins, lusty as of old, Hunt
the waste grass for forage, or prolong From every quarter of
these fields the bold, Blithe phrases of their never-finished song.
<|endoftext|>

Grief was my master yesternight;
To-morrow I may grieve again; But now along the
windy plain The clouds have taken flight.
<|endoftext|>

The roads far off are towered with dust;
The cherry-blooms are swept and thinned; In yonder swaying elms
the wind Is charging gust on gust.
<|endoftext|>

But here there is no stir at all; The
ministers of sun and shadow Horde all the perfumes of
the meadow Behind a grassy wall.
<|endoftext|>

An infant rivulet wind-free Adown
the guarded hollow sets, Over whose brink the violets
Are nodding peacefully.
<|endoftext|>

From pool to pool it prattles by; The
flashing swallows dip and pass, Above the tufted marish
grass, And here at rest am I.
<|endoftext|>

I care not for the old distress,
Nor if to-morrow bid me moan; To-day is mine, and I
have known An hour of blessedness.
<|endoftext|>

I passed through the gates of the city, The
streets were strange and still, Through the doors of the open
churches The organs were moaning shrill.
<|endoftext|>

I strayed through the midst
of the city Like one
distracted or mad.
<|endoftext|>

" I kept saying,
And the very
word seemed sad.
<|endoftext|>

I passed through the gates of the city,
And I heard the small birds sing, I laid me down
in the meadows Afar from the bell-ringing.
<|endoftext|>

In the depth and the bloom of the meadows
I lay on the earth's quiet breast, The poplar fanned me with shadows,
And the veery sang me to rest.
<|endoftext|>

Blue, blue was the heaven
above me, And the earth green
at my feet; "Oh, Life!
<|endoftext|>

" I kept saying,
And the very
word seemed sweet.
<|endoftext|>

Dim surging motions and uneasy dreads Scare the light
slumber from men's busy eyes, And parted lovers on their restless
beds Toss and yearn out, and cannot sleep for sighs.
<|endoftext|>

Ah,
the
distance!
<|endoftext|>

Blue
and broad and
dim!
<|endoftext|>

Peace is not in
burgh or meadow, But
beyond the rim.
<|endoftext|>

Aye, beyond it, far beyond it; Follow
still my soul, Till this earth is lost in heaven,
And thou feel'st the whole.
<|endoftext|>

And yet a moment more, Out of the silent wood,
As if from the closing door Of another world and another
lovelier mood, Hear'st thou the hermit pour-- So sweet!
<|endoftext|>

-- His golden
music, ghostly
beautiful.
<|endoftext|>

But when the fourth day came--at noon, The darkness and
the rain were by; The sunward roofs were steaming dry; And all the world
was flecked and strewn With shadows from a fleecy sky.
<|endoftext|>

The haymakers were
forth and gone, And every
rillet laughed and shone.
<|endoftext|>

With a turn of his magical rod,
That extended and suddenly shone, From the round of his
glory some god Looks forth and is gone.
<|endoftext|>

To the summit of heaven the clouds
Are rolling aloft like steam; There's a break in their
infinite shrouds, And below it a gleam.
<|endoftext|>

O'er the drift of the river a whiff
Comes out from the blossoming shore; And the meadows are greening,
as if They never were green before.
<|endoftext|>

The islands are kindled with gold And
russet and emerald dye; And the interval
waters outrolled Are more blue than the sky.
<|endoftext|>

From my feet to the heart of the hills The
spirits of May intervene, And a vapor of azure
distills Like a breath on the opaline green.
<|endoftext|>

--and then The chill and the shadow
decline, On the eyes of rejuvenate
men That were wide and divine.
<|endoftext|>

The full, clear moon uprose and spread Her
cold, pale splendor o'er the sea; A light-strewn path that seemed to
lead Outward into eternity.
<|endoftext|>

Between the darkness and the gleam An
old-world spell encompassed me: Methought that in a godlike
dream I trod upon the sea.
<|endoftext|>

What would'st thou have for easement after grief, When
the rude world hath used thee with despite, And care sits at thine elbow
day and night, Filching thy pleasures like a subtle thief?
<|endoftext|>

On such a day the shrunken stream Spends
its last water and runs dry; Clouds like far turrets in a
dream Stand baseless in the burning sky.
<|endoftext|>

On such a day at every rod
The toilers in the hay-field halt, With dripping brows, and
the parched sod Yields to the crushing foot like salt.
<|endoftext|>

I watch the swinging currents go Far
down to where, enclosed and piled, The logs crowd, and the Gatineau
Comes rushing from the northern wild.
<|endoftext|>

I see the long low point, where close The shore-lines,
and the waters end, I watch the barges pass in rows
That vanish at the tapering bend.
<|endoftext|>

I see no more the barges pass, Nor
mark the ripple round the pier, And all the uproar, mass
on mass, Falls dead upon a vacant ear.
<|endoftext|>

Beyond the tumult of the mills, And all
the city's sound and strife, Beyond the waste, beyond the
hills, I look far out and dream of life.
<|endoftext|>

Around the long low shanties, whose rough lines Break
the sealed dreams of many an unnamed lake, Already in the frost-clear
morns awake The crash and thunder of the falling pines.
<|endoftext|>

With what doubting eyes, oh sparrow,
Thou regardest me, Underneath yon spray of
yarrow, Dipping cautiously.
<|endoftext|>

Fear me not, oh little sparrow,
Bathe and never fear, For to me both pool and
yarrow And thyself are dear.
<|endoftext|>

And on beside
the river's sober edge A long
fresh field lies black.
<|endoftext|>

Beyond, Low thickets gray and reddish stand,
Stroked white with birch; and near at hand, Over a little steel-smooth pond,
Hang multitudes of thin and withering sedge.
<|endoftext|>

Across a waste and solitary rise A
ploughman urges his dull team, A stooped gray figure with prone brow That
plunges bending to the plough With strong, uneven steps.
<|endoftext|>

The stream Rings and
re-echoes with his
furious cries.
<|endoftext|>

Sometimes the lowing of a cow,
long-drawn, Comes from far off; and crows in strings Pass
on the upper silences.
<|endoftext|>

A flock of small gray goldfinches,
Flown down with silvery twitterings, Rustle
among the birch-cones and are gone.
<|endoftext|>

It was a bleak and sandy spot, And, all
about, the vacant plot Was peopled and inhabited
By scores of mulleins long since dead.
<|endoftext|>

There was no sound
about the wood Save
the wind's secret stir.
<|endoftext|>

I stood Among the mullein-stalks as still
As if myself had grown to be One of their sombre
company, A body without wish or will.
<|endoftext|>

And the snowbirds flee, Tossing up on the far
brown field, Now flashing and now concealed, Like fringes of spray That
vanish and gleam on the gray Field of the sea.
<|endoftext|>

Flickering light, Come the last of the leaves
down borne, And patches of pale white corn In the wind complain, Like
the slow rustle of rain Noticed by night.
<|endoftext|>

Withered and thinned, The sentinel mullein looms,
With the pale gray shadowy plumes Of the goldenrod; And the
milkweed opens its pod, Tempting the wind.
<|endoftext|>

Aloft on the hill, A cloudrift opens and
shines Through a break in its gorget of pines, And it dreams at my feet
In a sad, silvery sheet, Utterly still.
<|endoftext|>

All things that be Seem plunged into silence, distraught,
By some stern, some necessitous thought: It wraps and enthralls Marsh,
meadow, and forest; and falls Also on me.
<|endoftext|>

Along the narrow sandy height I watch them swiftly
come and go, Or round the leafless wood, Like flurries of wind-driven snow, Revolving in
perpetual flight, A changing multitude.
<|endoftext|>

White are the far-off plains, and white The fading forests grow;
The wind dies out along the height, And denser still the snow, A gathering
weight on roof and tree, Falls down scarce audibly.
<|endoftext|>

The road before me smooths and fills Apace, and all
about The fences dwindle, and the hills Are blotted slowly out; The
naked trees loom spectrally Into the dim white sky.
<|endoftext|>

The meadows and far-sheeted streams Lie still without a sound;
Like some soft minister of dreams The snow-fall hoods me round; In wood and
water, earth and air, A silence everywhere.
<|endoftext|>

The evening deepens, and the gray Folds closer earth
and sky; The world seems shrouded far away; Its noises sleep, and I, As
secret as yon buried stream, Plod dumbly on, and dream.
<|endoftext|>

From this windy bridge at rest, In some former
curious hour, We have watched the city's hue, All along the orange west, Cupola
and pointed tower, Darken into solid blue.
<|endoftext|>

Subtly conscious, all awake, Let
us clear our eyes, and break Through the cloudy
chrysalis, See the wonder as it is.
<|endoftext|>

Creeping by the sullen wall, We forego the power to
see, The threads that bind us to the All, God or the Immensity; Whereof
on the eternal road Man is but a passing mode.
<|endoftext|>

Up the fielded slopes at morn, Where
light wefts of shadow pass, Films upon the bending
corn, I shall sweep the purple grass.
<|endoftext|>

Sun-crowned heights and mossy woods, And
the outer solitudes, Mountain-valleys, dim with
pine, Shall be home and haunt of mine.
<|endoftext|>

I shall stray by
many a stream, Where
the half-shut lilies gleam.
<|endoftext|>

Napping out the sultry days In the quiet
secluded bays; Where the tasseled rushes tower,
O'er the purple pickerel-flower.
<|endoftext|>

I shall hear the grasshoppers
From the parched grass rehearse, And with drowsy note prolong
Evermore the same thin song.
<|endoftext|>

I shall hear the crickets tell Stories by the humming
well, And mark the locust, with quaint eyes, Caper in his cloak of gray Like
a jester in disguise Rattling by the dusty way.
<|endoftext|>

I shall dream by upland fences, Where the season's wealth
condenses Over many a weedy wreck, Wild, uncared-for, desert places, That sovereign
Beauty loves to deck With her softest, dearest graces.
<|endoftext|>

There the long year dreams
in quiet, And the summer's
strength runs riot.
<|endoftext|>

Shall I not remember
these, Deep in winter
reveries?
<|endoftext|>

It holds
me
fast.
<|endoftext|>

A drifting, April, twilight sky, A wind which
blew the puddles dry, And slapped the river into waves That ran
and hid among the staves Of an old wharf.
<|endoftext|>

A watery light Touched bleak the granite
bridge, and white Without the slightest tinge of gold,
The city shivered in the cold.
<|endoftext|>

All day my thoughts
had lain as dead, Unborn and
bursting in my head.
<|endoftext|>

From time to time I wrote
a word Which lines and circles
overscored.
<|endoftext|>

My table seemed a
graveyard, full Of coffins waiting
burial.
<|endoftext|>

I seized these vile abortions, tore
Them into jagged bits, and swore To be
the dupe of hope no more.
<|endoftext|>

Into the evening
straight I went, Starved of a
day's accomplishment.
<|endoftext|>

Unnoticing, I wandered where The city gave a
space for air, And on the bridge's parapet I leant, while
pallidly there set A dim, discouraged, worn-out sun.
<|endoftext|>

Behind me, where the tramways run,
Blossomed bright lights, I turned to leave, When
someone plucked me by the sleeve.
<|endoftext|>

"Your pardon, Sir, but I should
be Most grateful could you lend to me A
carfare, I have lost my purse.
<|endoftext|>

" The voice
was clear, concise,
and terse.
<|endoftext|>

I turned and met
the quiet gaze Of strange
eyes flashing through the haze.
<|endoftext|>

Like some Spanish don, Conferring
favours even when Asking an alms, he
bowed again And waited.
<|endoftext|>

But my pockets proved Empty,
in vain I poked and shoved, No hidden penny
lurking there Greeted my search.
<|endoftext|>

"Sir, I declare I have
no money, pray forgive, But let me
take you where you live.
<|endoftext|>

" And so we plodded
through the mire Where street lamps
cast a wavering fire.
<|endoftext|>

I took no note of where we
went, His talk became the element Wherein
my being swam, content.
<|endoftext|>

It flashed like rapiers in the night
Lit by uncertain candle-light, When on some moon-forsaken
sward A quarrel dies upon a sword.
<|endoftext|>

It hacked and carved like a cutlass blade, And the
noise in the air the broad words made Was the cry of the wind at a
window-pane On an Autumn night of sobbing rain.
<|endoftext|>

I walked as though some
opiate Had stung and dulled my
brain, a state Acute and slumbrous.
<|endoftext|>

We stopped, a
house stood silent,
dark.
<|endoftext|>

From the chimney, red
eyes sparked the gloom, And a cricket's
chirp filled all the room.
<|endoftext|>

My host threw pine-cones on the fire
And crimson and scarlet glowed the pyre Wrapped
in the golden flame's desire.
<|endoftext|>

The chamber opened like an eye, As a
half-melted cloud in a Summer sky The soul of the house stood guessed,
and shy It peered at the stranger warily.
<|endoftext|>

A little shop with
its various ware Spread on
shelves with nicest care.
<|endoftext|>

Pitchers, and jars, and jugs, and pots, Pipkins,
and mugs, and many lots Of lacquered canisters, black and
gold, Like those in which Chinese tea is sold.
<|endoftext|>

In a corner three ancient
amphorae leaned Against the wall,
like ships careened.
<|endoftext|>

There was dusky blue of Wedgewood
ware, The carved, white figures fluttering there Like
leaves adrift upon the air.
<|endoftext|>

Classic in touch, but
emasculate, The Greek soul
grown effeminate.
<|endoftext|>

Eggshell trays where gay beaux knelt, Hand on heart, and
daintily spelt Their love in flowers, brittle and bright, Artificial
and fragile, which told aright The vows of an eighteenth-century knight.
<|endoftext|>

The cruder tones of old Dutch jugs Glared
from one shelf, where Toby mugs Endlessly drank the foaming ale,
Its froth grown dusty, awaiting sale.
<|endoftext|>

"Ah, I see,"
Said I, "you deal
in pottery.
<|endoftext|>

" The old man
turned and looked
at me.
<|endoftext|>

Shook his
head
gently.
<|endoftext|>

Then from under his cloak he took the
thing Which I had wondered to see him bring Guarded
so carefully from sight.
<|endoftext|>

The old man smiled, "It has no sheath, 'Twas a little
careless to have it beneath My cloak, for a jostle to my
arm Would have resulted in serious harm.
<|endoftext|>

But it was so fine,
I could not wait, So I brought it
with me despite its state.
<|endoftext|>

" "An amateur of
arms," I thought, "Bringing home a
prize which he has bought.
<|endoftext|>

" "You care
for this sort
of thing, Dear Sir?
<|endoftext|>

" "Not in
the way which you
infer.
<|endoftext|>

I need
them in business,
that is all.
<|endoftext|>

" And he
pointed his finger
at the wall.
<|endoftext|>

Then I saw
what I had not
noticed before.
<|endoftext|>

The walls were hung with at least five
score Of swords and daggers of every size Which
nations of militant men could devise.
<|endoftext|>

Poisoned spears from tropic seas, That
natives, under banana trees, Smear with
the juice of some deadly snake.
<|endoftext|>

Blood-dipped arrows, which savages
make And tip with feathers, orange and green, A
quivering death, in harlequin sheen.
<|endoftext|>

High up, a fan of
glancing steel Was formed of
claymores in a wheel.
<|endoftext|>

Jewelled swords worn at kings' levees Were suspended
next midshipmen's dirks, and these Elbowed stilettos come from Spain,
Chased with some splendid Hidalgo's name.
<|endoftext|>

Foils with buttons broken or
lost Lay heaped on a chair, among them tossed
The boarding-pike of a privateer.
<|endoftext|>

Against the chimney leaned
a queer Two-handed weapon, with edges dull As
though from hacking on a skull.
<|endoftext|>

The rusted
blood corroded
it still.
<|endoftext|>

My host took up a paper
spill From a heap which lay in an earthen bowl, And lighted
it at a burning coal.
<|endoftext|>

At either end of the table,
tall Wax candles were placed, each in a small, And slim,
and burnished candlestick Of pewter.
<|endoftext|>

The old man lit each wick, And the room leapt
more obviously Upon my mind, and I could see
What the flickering fire had hid from me.
<|endoftext|>

My head grew dizzy, I seemed to hear A battle-cry
from somewhere near, The clash of arms, and the squeal of balls, And
the echoless thud when a dead man falls.
<|endoftext|>

A smoky cloud had veiled the room, Shot
through with lurid glares; the gloom Pounded with shouts and dying
groans, With the drip of blood on cold, hard stones.
<|endoftext|>

Sabres and lances in streaks of light Gleamed
through the smoke, and at my right A creese, like a licking serpent's
tongue, Glittered an instant, while it stung.
<|endoftext|>

Streams, and
points, and lines of
fire!
<|endoftext|>

The livid steel, which man's
desire Had forged and welded,
burned white and cold.
<|endoftext|>

Nerveless and shaking, round and round, I stared at
the walls and at the ground, Till the room spun like a whipping
top, And a stern voice in my ear said, "Stop!
<|endoftext|>

I sell
no tools for
murderers here.
<|endoftext|>

Of what
are you
thinking!
<|endoftext|>

Please clear Your
mind of such
imaginings.
<|endoftext|>

I will
tell you
of these things.
<|endoftext|>

" He pushed me into a great chair Of russet
leather, poked a flare Of tumbling flame, with the old
long sword, Up the chimney; but said no word.
<|endoftext|>

Slowly he walked to
a distant shelf, And brought back a
crock of finest delf.
<|endoftext|>

He rested a moment a blue-veined
hand Upon the cover, then cut a band Of paper,
pasted neatly round, Opened and poured.
<|endoftext|>

A sliding sound Came from beneath
his old white hands, And I saw a little
heap of sands, Black and smooth.
<|endoftext|>

What could they
be: "Pepper,"
I thought.
<|endoftext|>

He looked
at
me.
<|endoftext|>

"What you
see is poppy
seed.
<|endoftext|>

Lethean
dreams for those in
need.
<|endoftext|>

" He took up the grains
with a gentle hand And sifted
them slowly like hour-glass sand.
<|endoftext|>

On his old white finger
the almandine Shot out its
rays, incarnadine.
<|endoftext|>

"Visions
for those too tired
to sleep.
<|endoftext|>

These seeds cast
a film over
eyes which weep.
<|endoftext|>

No single soul
in the world could dwell, Without
these poppy-seeds I sell.
<|endoftext|>

" For a moment he
played with the shining stuff, Passing
it through his fingers.
<|endoftext|>

Enough At last, he poured it back
into The china jar of Holland blue, Which
he carefully carried to its place.
<|endoftext|>

Then, with a smile on his aged
face, He drew up a chair to the open
space 'Twixt table and chimney.
<|endoftext|>

"Without preface, Young man,
I will say that what you see Is not the puzzle
you take it to be.
<|endoftext|>

" "But surely, Sir, there is something
strange In a shop with goods at so wide a range Each
from the other, as swords and seeds.
<|endoftext|>

Your neighbours must
have greatly differing
needs.
<|endoftext|>

" "My neighbours," he said, and he
stroked his chin, "Live everywhere
from here to Pekin.
<|endoftext|>

But you are wrong, my sort
of goods Is but one thing
in all its moods.
<|endoftext|>

" He took a shagreen letter
case From his pocket, and with charming grace Offered
me a printed card.
<|endoftext|>

I read
the legend,
"Ephraim Bard.
<|endoftext|>

Dealer
in
Words.
<|endoftext|>

"  And
that
was all.
<|endoftext|>

I stared at the letters,
whimsical Indeed, or was
it merely a jest.
<|endoftext|>

He answered my unasked request:
"All books are either dreams or swords, You can
cut, or you can drug, with words.
<|endoftext|>

My firm is a very ancient
house, The entries on my books would rouse Your wonder,
perhaps incredulity.
<|endoftext|>

I inherited from an ancestry Stretching remotely
back and far, This business, and my clients are As were those of my grandfather's
days, Writers of books, and poems, and plays.
<|endoftext|>

My swords are tempered for every
speech, For fencing wit, or to carve a breach Through
old abuses the world condones.
<|endoftext|>

These are purchased by men who feel The
need of stabbing society's heel, Which egotism has
brought them to think Is set on their necks.
<|endoftext|>

I have foils to pink An adversary
to quaint reply, And I have customers who buy Scalpels
with which to dissect the brains And hearts of men.
<|endoftext|>

Ultramundanes Even
demand some finer kinds To open
their own souls and minds.
<|endoftext|>

But the other
half of my business deals With
visions and fancies.
<|endoftext|>

Under seals, Sorted,
and placed in vessels here, I keep the seeds
of an atmosphere.
<|endoftext|>

Each jar contains
a different kind Of
poppy seed.
<|endoftext|>

From farthest Ind Come the purple flowers,
opium filled, From which the weirdest myths are distilled; My
orient porcelains contain them all.
<|endoftext|>

Every castle of the air Sleeps in the
fine black grains, and there Are seeds for every romance,
or light Whiff of a dream for a summer night.
<|endoftext|>

I supply to
every want
and taste.
<|endoftext|>

" 'Twas slowly said, in no
great haste He seemed to push his wares,
but I Dumfounded listened.
<|endoftext|>

By and by
A log on the
fire broke in two.
<|endoftext|>

He looked
up quickly, "Sir,
and you?
<|endoftext|>

" I groped for something
I should say; Amazement
held me numb.
<|endoftext|>

"To-day You sweated
at a fruitless
task.
<|endoftext|>

" He spoke
for me, "What do
you ask?
<|endoftext|>

How
can I serve
you?
<|endoftext|>

"  "My kind host, My
penniless state was not a boast; I
have no money with me.
<|endoftext|>

"Not for that money
I beguiled You here; you
paid me in advance.
<|endoftext|>

" Again I felt as
though a trance Had dimmed
my faculties.
<|endoftext|>

Again He
spoke, and this time
to explain.
<|endoftext|>

"The money I demand
is Life, Your nervous force,
your joy, your strife!
<|endoftext|>

" What infamous
proposal now Was made
me with so calm a brow?
<|endoftext|>

Bursting through my lethargy,
Indignantly I hurled the cry: "Is this a nightmare,
or am I Drunk with some infernal wine?
<|endoftext|>

I am no
Faust, and what is mine Is
what I call my soul!
<|endoftext|>

Devil
or
Ghost!
<|endoftext|>

Your hellish
plan Revolts
me.
<|endoftext|>

"  "My child," And the old tones were very
mild, "I have no wish to barter souls; My traffic
does not ask such tolls.
<|endoftext|>

I am
no devil;
is there one?
<|endoftext|>

Surely
the age of
fear is gone.
<|endoftext|>

We live within a daylight world Lit by
the sun, where winds unfurled Sweep clouds to scatter pattering
rain, And then blow back the sun again.
<|endoftext|>

I sell my fancies, or my swords, To those
who care far more for words, Ideas, of which they
are the sign, Than any other life-design.
<|endoftext|>

I claim for all the goods I
sell That they will serve their purpose well,
And though you perish, they will live.
<|endoftext|>

Full measure
for your pay
I give.
<|endoftext|>

To-day you
worked, you
thought, in vain.
<|endoftext|>

What since has happened
is the train Your toiling
brought.
<|endoftext|>

I spoke to you
For my share of the
bargain, due.
<|endoftext|>

And is
that all you crave
In pay?
<|endoftext|>

What
even
childhood gave!
<|endoftext|>

I have
been dedicate
from youth.
<|endoftext|>

Before my
God I speak
the truth!
<|endoftext|>

" Fatigue, excitement
of the past Few hours broke me
down at last.
<|endoftext|>

All day I had
forgot to eat, My nerves betrayed
me, lacking meat.
<|endoftext|>

I bowed my head and
felt the storm Plough shattering
through my prostrate form.
<|endoftext|>

The
tearless sobs
tore at my heart.
<|endoftext|>

My host withdrew himself apart;
Busied among his crockery, He paid
no farther heed to me.
<|endoftext|>

Exhausted, spent, I
huddled there, Within the arms
of the old carved chair.
<|endoftext|>

A long half-hour dragged away, And then
I heard a kind voice say, "The day will soon be dawning, when
You must begin to work again.
<|endoftext|>

Here are the
things which you
require.
<|endoftext|>

" By the fading light of the dying
fire, And by the guttering candle's flare,
I saw the old man standing there.
<|endoftext|>

He handed me a
packet, tied With crimson
tape, and sealed.
<|endoftext|>

"Inside Are seeds of many differing
flowers, To occupy your utmost powers Of storied vision,
and these swords Are the finest which my shop affords.
<|endoftext|>

Go home and use them; do
not spare Yourself; let that
be all your care.
<|endoftext|>

Whatever you have means
to buy Be very sure
I can supply.
<|endoftext|>

" He slowly walked to the window,
flung It open, and in the grey air rung The
sound of distant matin bells.
<|endoftext|>

I
took my
parcels.
<|endoftext|>

Then, as tells An ancient mumbling
monk his beads, I tried to thank for his
courteous deeds My strange old friend.
<|endoftext|>

"Nay, do not talk," He
urged me, "you have a long
walk Before you.
<|endoftext|>

" And gently sped upon my way I stumbled
out in the morning hush, As down the empty street a flush
Ran level from the rising sun.
<|endoftext|>

Another
day was just
begun.
<|endoftext|>

Over the housetops, Above the rotating chimney-pots,
I have seen a shiver of amethyst, And blue and cinnamon have flickered
A moment, At the far end of a dusty street.
<|endoftext|>

Through sheeted rain Has come a lustre
of crimson, And I have watched moonbeams Hushed by
a film of palest green.
<|endoftext|>

It was
her wings,
Goddess!
<|endoftext|>

Who stepped over the clouds, And
laid her rainbow feathers Aslant on
the currents of the air.
<|endoftext|>

I followed her for
long, With gazing eyes and
stumbling feet.
<|endoftext|>

I followed,
And watched for the flashing
of her wings.
<|endoftext|>

In the city
I found her, The narrow-streeted
city.
<|endoftext|>

In the market-place I
came upon her, Bound
and trembling.
<|endoftext|>

Her fluted wings were fastened to
her sides with cords, She was naked and cold, For that
day the wind blew Without sunshine.
<|endoftext|>

Men chaffered for her, They bargained
in silver and gold, In copper, in wheat, And
called their bids across the market-place.
<|endoftext|>

The tall yellow hollyhocks stand,
Still and straight, With their round blossoms spread open,
In the quiet sunshine.
<|endoftext|>

And still is the old Roman wall, Rough
with jagged bits of flint, And jutting stones, Old and
cragged, Quite still in its antiquity.
<|endoftext|>

The pear-trees press their branches against it,
And feeling it warm and kindly, The little
pears ripen to yellow and red.
<|endoftext|>

They hang heavy,
bursting with juice, Against
the wall.
<|endoftext|>

So
old,
so still!
<|endoftext|>

The sky
is
still.
<|endoftext|>

The clouds make no sound As they
slide away Beyond the Cathedral Tower,
To the river, And the sea.
<|endoftext|>

It is
very quiet, Very
sunny.
<|endoftext|>

The myrtle flowers
stretch themselves in the sunshine,
But make no sound.
<|endoftext|>

The roses push their
little tendrils up, And climb
higher and higher.
<|endoftext|>

In spots
they have climbed
over the wall.
<|endoftext|>

But they are very
still, They do not
seem to move.
<|endoftext|>

And the old wall carries them
Without effort, and quietly Ripens
and shields the vines and blossoms.
<|endoftext|>

A bird in a plane-tree Sings
a few notes, Cadenced and perfect They
weave into the silence.
<|endoftext|>

The Cathedral
bell knocks, One, two, three, and again,
And then again.
<|endoftext|>

It is a quiet sound, Calling
to prayer, Hardly scattering the stillness, Only
making it close in more densely.
<|endoftext|>

The gardener
picks ripe gooseberries For the
Dean's supper to-night.
<|endoftext|>

It is very quiet,
Very regulated
and mellow.
<|endoftext|>

But the wall
is old, It has known
many days.
<|endoftext|>

It is a Roman
wall, Left-over and
forgotten.
<|endoftext|>

Beyond the Cathedral
Close Yelp and mutter the discontents of
people not mellow, Not well-regulated.
<|endoftext|>

People who care more for bread than for beauty,
Who would break the tombs of saints, And give the painted windows
of churches To their children for toys.
<|endoftext|>

People who
say: "They are dead,
we live!
<|endoftext|>

The world is
for the
living.
<|endoftext|>

It is
always the
dead who breed.
<|endoftext|>

Crush the ripe fruit, and cast it
aside, Yet its seeds shall fructify, And
trees rise where your huts were standing.
<|endoftext|>

But the little
people are ignorant,
They chaffer, and swarm.
<|endoftext|>

They gnaw like rats, And
the foundations of the Cathedral
are honeycombed.
<|endoftext|>

The Dean is in the Chapter House; He
is reading the architect's bill For the completed
restoration of the Cathedral.
<|endoftext|>

He will have ripe gooseberries for supper, And then he will walk
up and down the path By the wall, And admire the snapdragons and dahlias,
Thinking how quiet and peaceful The garden is.
<|endoftext|>

The old wall will watch
him, Very quietly and
patiently it will watch.
<|endoftext|>

For the wall
is old, It is
a Roman wall.
<|endoftext|>

Spread on the roadway, With open-blown
jackets, Like black, soaring pinions, They swoop down
the hillside, The Cyclists.
<|endoftext|>

Seeming dark-plumaged Birds, after
carrion, Careening and circling,
Over the dying Of England.
<|endoftext|>

She lies with her bosom Beneath
them, no longer The Dominant Mother, The
Virile--but rotting Before time.
<|endoftext|>

The smell of
her, tainted, Has
bitten their nostrils.
<|endoftext|>

What charm is yours, you faded old-world
tapestries, Of outworn, childish mysteries, Vague
pageants woven on a web of dream!
<|endoftext|>

And we, pushing and fighting
in the turbid stream Of modern life, find solace
in your tarnished broideries.
<|endoftext|>

Old lichened halls, sun-shaded by huge
cedar-trees, The layered branches horizontal
stretched, like Japanese Dark-banded prints.
<|endoftext|>

Carven cathedrals, on a sky
Of faintest colour, where the gothic spires fly
And sway like masts, against a shifting breeze.
<|endoftext|>

Worm-eaten pages, clasped in
old brown vellum, shrunk From over-handling,
by some anxious monk.
<|endoftext|>

Or Virgin's Hours, bright with gold and graven
With flowers, and rare birds, and all the Saints of Heaven, And Noah's
ark stuck on Ararat, when all the world had sunk.
<|endoftext|>

They have watered the street, It shines in
the glare of lamps, Cold, white lamps, And lies Like a
slow-moving river, Barred with silver and black.
<|endoftext|>

Cabs go down it,
One, And then
another.
<|endoftext|>

Between them I
hear the shuffling
of feet.
<|endoftext|>

Tramps doze on
the window-ledges, Night-walkers pass along
the sidewalks.
<|endoftext|>

The city is squalid and
sinister, With the silver-barred street in the
midst, Slow-moving, A river leading nowhere.
<|endoftext|>

Opposite my window,
The moon cuts, Clear and round,
Through the plum-coloured night.
<|endoftext|>

She cannot
light the city;
It is too bright.
<|endoftext|>

It has
white lamps, And
glitters coldly.
<|endoftext|>

I stand in
the window and watch
the moon.
<|endoftext|>

She is thin and
lustreless, But
I love her.
<|endoftext|>

I know the
moon, And this is an
alien city.
<|endoftext|>

To Ezra Pound With much friendship and admiration
and some differences of opinion The Poet took his
walking-stick Of fine and polished ebony.
<|endoftext|>

Set in the close-grained wood Were quaint
devices; Patterns in ambers, And
in the clouded green of jades.
<|endoftext|>

The top was of smooth, yellow ivory,
And a tassel of tarnished gold Hung by a faded cord from
a hole Pierced in the hard wood, Circled with silver.
<|endoftext|>

For years the
Poet had wrought upon
this cane.
<|endoftext|>

His wealth had gone to enrich it, His
experiences to pattern it, His labour
to fashion and burnish it.
<|endoftext|>

To him it was perfect,
A work of art and a weapon, A
delight and a defence.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet
took his walking-stick
And walked abroad.
<|endoftext|>

Peace be
with you,
Brother.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet
came to a
meadow.
<|endoftext|>

Sifted through the grass
were daisies, Open-mouthed, wondering,
they gazed at the sun.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet
struck them with his
cane.
<|endoftext|>

The little heads flew off, and
they lay Dying, open-mouthed and
wondering, On the hard ground.
<|endoftext|>

They are not
roses," said
the Poet.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet
came to
a stream.
<|endoftext|>

Purple and blue flags waded in
the water; In among them hopped the speckled frogs;
The wind slid through them, rustling.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet lifted his
cane, And the iris heads fell
into the water.
<|endoftext|>

They floated
away, torn
and drowning.
<|endoftext|>

"Wretched flowers,"
said the Poet, "They are
not roses.
<|endoftext|>

It
is your
affair.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet
came to a
garden.
<|endoftext|>

Dahlias ripened against a wall, Gillyflowers
stood up bravely for all their short stature, And a trumpet-vine covered
an arbour With the red and gold of its blossoms.
<|endoftext|>

Red and gold
like the brass notes
of trumpets.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet knocked off the stiff heads
of the dahlias, And his cane lopped the
gillyflowers at the ground.
<|endoftext|>

Then he
severed the trumpet-blossoms
from their stems.
<|endoftext|>

Red and gold they lay scattered,
Red and gold, as on a battle field; Red
and gold, prone and dying.
<|endoftext|>

"They were not
roses," said
the Poet.
<|endoftext|>

But behind you
is destruction,
and waste places.
<|endoftext|>

The Poet came home
at evening, And in the candle-light He
wiped and polished his cane.
<|endoftext|>

The orange candle flame leaped
in the yellow ambers, And made the jades
undulate like green pools.
<|endoftext|>

It played along the
bright ebony, And glowed in
the top of cream-coloured ivory.
<|endoftext|>

But these things were
dead, Only the candle-light made
them seem to move.
<|endoftext|>

"It is a pity
there were no roses,"
said the Poet.
<|endoftext|>

He perches in
the slime, inert, Bedaubed with
iridescent dirt.
<|endoftext|>

The ground is here
and there bestud With lumps
of only part-burned coal.
<|endoftext|>

Their sharp and glistening
edges cut His stiffened
fingers.
<|endoftext|>

Through the smut
Gleam red the wounds which
will not shut.
<|endoftext|>

Wet through and shivering
he kneels And digs the slippery coals; like
eels They slide about.
<|endoftext|>

His force all
spent, He counts his small
accomplishment.
<|endoftext|>

A half-a-dozen clinker-coals Which
still have fire
in their souls.
<|endoftext|>

And in his thought
there burns The topaz fire
of votive urns.
<|endoftext|>

He sees it fling
from hill to hill, And still consumed,
is burning still.
<|endoftext|>

Higher and higher
leaps the flame, The smoke an
ever-shifting frame.
<|endoftext|>

He sees a Spanish
Castle old, With silver steps
and paths of gold.
<|endoftext|>

From myrtle bowers comes the plash Of
fountains, and the emerald flash Of parrots in the
orange trees, Whose blossoms pasture humming bees.
<|endoftext|>

He knows he feeds the urns whose smoke Bears
visions, that his master-stroke Is out of dirt and misery
To light the fire of poesy.
<|endoftext|>

He sees the glory,
yet he knows That others cannot
see his shows.
<|endoftext|>

He sighs
and grubs another
coal.
<|endoftext|>

How should I sing when buffeting
salt waves And stung with bitter surges, in
whose might I toss, a cockleshell?
<|endoftext|>

No parting cloud reveals a watery star, My
cries are washed away upon the wind, My cramped and blistering hands can find
no spar, My eyes with hope o'erstrained, are growing blind.
<|endoftext|>

But painted on the sky
great visions burn, My voice, oblation
from a shattered urn!
<|endoftext|>

So up, and down, and forward, inch by
inch, He gains upon the shore, where poppies glow And
sandflies dance their little lives away.
<|endoftext|>

When the stooping sky Leans down upon the
hills And tenderly, as one who soothing stills An anguish,
gathers earth to lie Embraced and girdled.
<|endoftext|>

Do the
sun-filled men Feel patience
then?
<|endoftext|>

When pain's iron bars Their rivets tighten, stern
To bend and break their victims; as they turn, Hopeless, there stand the
purple jars Of night to spill oblivion.
<|endoftext|>

Do these
men Feel
patience then?
<|endoftext|>

My sun
and
moon!
<|endoftext|>

My
basketful
of flowers!
<|endoftext|>

My money-bag
of shining
dreams!
<|endoftext|>

My hours, Windless
and still, of
afternoon!
<|endoftext|>

You are my world
and I your
citizen.
<|endoftext|>

What meaning
can have patience
then?
<|endoftext|>

Be not angry with me that I bear Your
colours everywhere, All through each crowded street, And meet The
wonder-light in every eye, As I go by.
<|endoftext|>

Each plodding wayfarer looks up to gaze, Blinded
by rainbow haze, The stuff of happiness, No less, Which wraps
me in its glad-hued folds Of peacock golds.
<|endoftext|>

Before my feet the
dusty, rough-paved way Flushes
beneath its gray.
<|endoftext|>

My steps fall ringed with light,
So bright, It seems a myriad suns
are strown About the town.
<|endoftext|>

Around me is the sound of steepled
bells, And rich perfumed smells Hang like a wind-forgotten cloud, And shroud
Me from close contact with the world.
<|endoftext|>

You blazon me
with jewelled
insignia.
<|endoftext|>

A flaming
nebula Rims in
my life.
<|endoftext|>

And yet You set
The word upon me, unconfessed
To go unguessed.
<|endoftext|>

I pray to be the tool which to your hand Long
use has shaped and moulded till it be Apt for your need, and,
unconsideringly, You take it for its service.
<|endoftext|>

I demand To be forgotten in the woven strand
Which grows the multi-coloured tapestry Of your bright life, and through its tissues
lie A hidden, strong, sustaining, grey-toned band.
<|endoftext|>

I wish to dwell around your daylight dreams, The railing to
the stairway of the clouds, To guard your steps securely up, where streams A
faery moonshine washing pale the crowds Of pointed stars.
<|endoftext|>

Remember not
whereby You mount, protected,
to the far-flung sky.
<|endoftext|>

Before me lies a mass of shapeless
days, Unseparated atoms, and I must Sort
them apart and live them.
<|endoftext|>

Sifted dust Covers
the formless
heap.
<|endoftext|>

Reprieves, delays,
There are none,
ever.
<|endoftext|>

As a monk who prays The sliding beads
asunder, so I thrust Each tasteless particle aside, and
just Begin again the task which never stays.
<|endoftext|>

And I have known a glory
of great suns, When days flashed by, pulsing
with joy and fire!
<|endoftext|>

Drunk bubbled wine in goblets
of desire, And felt the whipped
blood laughing as it runs!
<|endoftext|>

Spilt is that liquor, my too
hasty hand Threw down the cup, and
did not understand.
<|endoftext|>

Dearest, forgive that
with my clumsy touch I
broke and bruised your rose.
<|endoftext|>

I hardly could suppose It
were a thing so fragile that my
clutch Could kill it, thus.
<|endoftext|>

It stood so proudly up upon its stem, I knew
no thought of fear, And coming very near Fell, overbalanced,
to your garment's hem, Tearing it down.
<|endoftext|>

Now, stooping, I upgather,
one by one, The crimson petals, all Outspread
about my fall.
<|endoftext|>

They hold their
fragrance still, a blood-red cone Of
memory.
<|endoftext|>

And with my words I carve a little jar To
keep their scented dust, Which, opening, you must Breathe to your soul,
and, breathing, know me far More grieved than you.
<|endoftext|>

An arid daylight shines along the beach Dried to a grey
monotony of tone, And stranded jelly-fish melt soft upon The sun-baked pebbles,
far beyond their reach Sparkles a wet, reviving sea.
<|endoftext|>

Here bleach The skeletons of fishes, every
bone Polished and stark, like traceries of stone, The joints
and knuckles hardened each to each.
<|endoftext|>

And they are dead while
waiting for the sea, The moon-pursuing
sea, to come again.
<|endoftext|>

Their hearts are
blown away on the
hot breeze.
<|endoftext|>

Only the
shells and stones can wait
to be Washed bright.
<|endoftext|>

For living things, who
suffer pain, May not endure
till time can bring them ease.
<|endoftext|>

Happiness, to
some, elation; Is, to others,
mere stagnation.
<|endoftext|>

Days of passive
somnolence, At its wildest,
indolence.
<|endoftext|>

Hours of empty
quietness, No delight, and
no distress.
<|endoftext|>

Happiness to me
is wine, Effervescent,
superfine.
<|endoftext|>

Full of tang and fiery
pleasure, Far too hot to leave me leisure For
a single thought beyond it.
<|endoftext|>

This the bond:  it
Means to give one's soul to
gain Life's quintessence.
<|endoftext|>

Even pain Pricks to livelier
living, then Wakes the nerves to laugh again, Rapture's
self is three parts sorrow.
<|endoftext|>

Although we must die
to-morrow, Losing every thought but this;
Torn, triumphant, drowned in bliss.
<|endoftext|>

Happiness:
 We rarely
feel it.
<|endoftext|>

How long shall I tarnish
the mirror of life, A spatter of
rust on its polished steel!
<|endoftext|>

The seasons reel
Like a goaded
wheel.
<|endoftext|>

Half-numb,
half-maddened, my days
are strife.
<|endoftext|>

The night is sliding
towards the dawn, And upturned
hills crouch at autumn's knees.
<|endoftext|>

A torn moon flees Through
the hemlock trees, The hours
have gnawed it to feed their spawn.
<|endoftext|>

Pursuing and jeering
the misshapen thing A rabble
of clouds flares out of the east.
<|endoftext|>

Like dogs unleashed After
a beast, They stream on the sky,
an outflung string.
<|endoftext|>

A desolate wind, through the unpeopled dark, Shakes the
bushes and whistles through empty nests, And the fierce unrests I keep
as guests Crowd my brain with corpses, pallid and stark.
<|endoftext|>

Leave me in peace, O Spectres,
who haunt My labouring mind, I
have fought and failed.
<|endoftext|>

The moon drops into
the silver day As waking
out of her swoon she comes.
<|endoftext|>

I hear the drums Of
millenniums Beating the mornings
I still must stay.
<|endoftext|>

The years I must watch go in and out, While I build
with water, and dig in air, And the trumpets blare Hollow despair,
The shuddering trumpets of utter rout.
<|endoftext|>

An atom tossed in
a chaos made Of yeasting
worlds, which bubble and foam.
<|endoftext|>

Whence
have
I come?
<|endoftext|>

What would
be
home?
<|endoftext|>

I
hear no
answer.
<|endoftext|>

I crave
to be lost like
a wind-blown flame.
<|endoftext|>

Pushed into nothingness by a breath, And
quench in a wreath Of engulfing death This fight
for a God, or this devil's game.
<|endoftext|>

There once was a man
whom the gods didn't love, And a
disagreeable man was he.
<|endoftext|>

He loathed his neighbours, and his
neighbours hated him, And he
cursed eternally.
<|endoftext|>

He damned the sun, and
he damned the stars, And he blasted
the winds in the sky.
<|endoftext|>

He sent to Hell every
green, growing thing, And he raved at
the birds as they fly.
<|endoftext|>

His oaths were many, and his range was wide,
He swore in fancy ways; But his meaning was plain:  that no
created thing Was other than a hurt to his gaze.
<|endoftext|>

He dwelt all alone, underneath a leaning
hill, And windows toward the hill there were none, And on the other
side they were white-washed thick, To keep out every spark of the sun.
<|endoftext|>

When he went to market he
walked all the way Blaspheming
at the path he trod.
<|endoftext|>

He cursed at those he bought
of, and swore at those he sold to, By all
the names he knew of God.
<|endoftext|>

For his heart was soured in
his weary old hide, And his hopes
had curdled in his breast.
<|endoftext|>

His friend had been untrue,
and his love had thrown him over For the
chinking money-bags she liked best.
<|endoftext|>

The rats had devoured the contents of
his grain-bin, The deer had trampled on his corn, His brook had shrivelled in
a summer drought, And his sheep had died unshorn.
<|endoftext|>

His hens wouldn't lay, and
his cow broke loose, And his old horse perished
of a colic.
<|endoftext|>

In the loft his wheat-bags were
nibbled into holes By little,
glutton mice on a frolic.
<|endoftext|>

So he slowly lost
all he ever had, And the blood
in his body dried.
<|endoftext|>

Shrunken and mean he
still lived on, And cursed that future
which had lied.
<|endoftext|>

So he dug, and he
delved, with care and pain, And the veins in
his forehead stood taut.
<|endoftext|>

At the end of an hour,
when every bone cracked, He
gathered up what he had sought.
<|endoftext|>

A dim old vase of
crusted glass, Prismed while it
lay buried deep.
<|endoftext|>

Shifting reds and greens, like
a pigeon's neck, At the touch of
the sun began to leap.
<|endoftext|>

It was dull in the tree-shade, but glowing in the light;
Flashing like an opal-stone, Carved into a flagon; and the colours
glanced and ran, Where at first there had seemed to be none.
<|endoftext|>

It had handles on
each side to bear it up, And a belly
for the gurgling wine.
<|endoftext|>

Its neck was slender,
and its mouth was wide, And its
lip was curled and fine.
<|endoftext|>

Then he carried it home, and
put it on a shelf, But it was
only grey in the gloom.
<|endoftext|>

So he fetched a pail,
and a bit of cloth, And he
went outside with a broom.
<|endoftext|>

The old man forgot to swear,
Watching its shadow grown a mammoth
size, Dancing in the kitchen there.
<|endoftext|>

He forgot to revile
the sun next morning When he found his
vase afire in its light.
<|endoftext|>

And he carried it out
of the house that day, And kept it
close beside him until night.
<|endoftext|>

And so it
happened from day
to day.
<|endoftext|>

The old man fed his
life On the beauty of his vase,
on its perfect shape.
<|endoftext|>

And his soul
forgot its former
strife.
<|endoftext|>

And the village-folk came
and begged to see The flagon which
was dug from the ground.
<|endoftext|>

And the old man never
thought of an oath, in his joy At
showing what he had found.
<|endoftext|>

One day the master of the village
school Passed him as he stooped at toil, Hoeing for a bean-row, and at
his side Was the vase, on the turned-up soil.
<|endoftext|>

"My friend," said the schoolmaster, pompous and
kind, "That's a valuable thing you have there, But it might get broken
out of doors, It should meet with the utmost care.
<|endoftext|>

What are you
doing with
it out here?
<|endoftext|>

" "Why, Sir," said the poor
old man, "I like to have it
about, do you see?
<|endoftext|>

To be with
it all
I can.
<|endoftext|>

" "You will smash it," said
the schoolmaster, sternly right,
"Mark my words and see!
<|endoftext|>

" And he walked away,
while the old man looked At his treasure
despondingly.
<|endoftext|>

Then he
smiled to himself, for
it was his!
<|endoftext|>

He had
toiled for it,
and now he cared.
<|endoftext|>

loved its shape, and
its subtle, swift hues, Which his
own hard work had bared.
<|endoftext|>

He would carry it
round with him everywhere, As
it gave him joy to do.
<|endoftext|>

A fragile
vase should not stand in
a bean-row!
<|endoftext|>

Who would dare
to say
so?
<|endoftext|>

Then his heart was rested,
and his fears gave way, And he bent
to his hoe again.
<|endoftext|>

A clod rolled down, and
his foot slipped back, And he lurched
with a cry of pain.
<|endoftext|>

For the blade of the hoe crashed
into glass, And the vase fell to
iridescent sherds.
<|endoftext|>

The old
man's body heaved with slow,
dry sobs.
<|endoftext|>

He did
not curse, he had
no words.
<|endoftext|>

He gathered the fragments,
one by one, And his fingers
were cut and torn.
<|endoftext|>

Then he made a hole
in the very place Whence the
beautiful vase had been borne.
<|endoftext|>

He covered the hole, and he
patted it down, Then he hobbled to
his house and shut the door.
<|endoftext|>

He tore up his coat and
nailed it at the windows That no
beam of light should cross the floor.
<|endoftext|>

He sat down in front
of the empty hearth, And he
neither ate nor drank.
<|endoftext|>

In three days they found
him, dead and cold, And they said:
 "What a queer old crank!
<|endoftext|>

" Have
at you, you
Devils!
<|endoftext|>

My back's to this tree, For
you're nothing so nice That the hind-side of
me Would escape your assault.
<|endoftext|>

Come
on now,
all three!
<|endoftext|>

Here's a dandified gentleman,
Rapier at point, And a wrist which whirls
round Like a circular joint.
<|endoftext|>

That's just to
anoint And make supple
your limbs.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis a pity
the silk Of your waistcoat
is stained.
<|endoftext|>

Your heart's full of
milk, And so full,
it spills over!
<|endoftext|>

I'm
not of
your ilk.
<|endoftext|>

You said so, and laughed At
my old-fashioned hose, At the cut of my hair,
At the length of my nose.
<|endoftext|>

To carve it
to pattern I
think you propose.
<|endoftext|>

Your pardon, young Sir, But
my nose and my sword Are proving themselves
In quite perfect accord.
<|endoftext|>

I grieve to
have spotted
Your shirt.
<|endoftext|>

That blade's not a stick To slash
right and left, And my skull is too thick To
be cleft with such cuffs Of a sword.
<|endoftext|>

Now a
lick Down the side
of your face.
<|endoftext|>

What a
pretty, red
line!
<|endoftext|>

Tell the taverns
that scar Was an
honour.
<|endoftext|>

Don't whine That
a stranger
has marked you.
<|endoftext|>

The
tree's there, You
Swine!
<|endoftext|>

Did you think to get in
At the back, while your friends Made a little
diversion In front?
<|endoftext|>

So it ends, With
your sword clattering
down On the ground.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis amends I make for your
courteous Reception of me, A foreigner,
landed From over the sea.
<|endoftext|>

Your welcome
was fervent I
think you'll agree.
<|endoftext|>

My shoes are not buckled With gold, nor my
hair Oiled and scented, my jacket's Not satin, I wear Corded
breeches, wide hats, And I make people stare!
<|endoftext|>

So I do, but my heart Is the heart of
a man, And my thoughts cannot twirl In the limited span 'Twixt
my head and my heels, As some other men's can.
<|endoftext|>

I have business more strange Than the shape of my
boots, And my interests range From the sky, to the roots Of this
dung-hill you live in, You half-rotted shoots Of a mouldering tree!
<|endoftext|>

Here's
at you,
once more.
<|endoftext|>

You can show me the
door, And jeer at my ways, But
you're pinked to the core.
<|endoftext|>

And before I have done, I will
prick my name in With the front of my steel, And your lily-white
skin Shall be printed with me.
<|endoftext|>

For
I've come here
to win!
<|endoftext|>

My cup is empty to-night,
Cold and dry are its sides, Chilled by the
wind from the open window.
<|endoftext|>

Empty and
void, it sparkles white
in the moonlight.
<|endoftext|>

The room is filled with
the strange scent Of wistaria
blossoms.
<|endoftext|>

They sway in the
moon's radiance And tap
against the wall.
<|endoftext|>

But the cup of my
heart is still, And cold,
and empty.
<|endoftext|>

When you come, it brims Red and trembling
with blood, Heart's blood for your drinking; To fill your mouth
with love And the bitter-sweet taste of a soul.
<|endoftext|>

My words are little
jars For you to take and
put upon a shelf.
<|endoftext|>

Their shapes are quaint and beautiful,
And they have many pleasant colours and lustres
To recommend them.
<|endoftext|>

Also the scent from them
fills the room With sweetness of
flowers and crushed grasses.
<|endoftext|>

You glow in my heart
Like the flames of
uncounted candles.
<|endoftext|>

But when I go to warm my hands, My
clumsiness overturns the light, And then I stumble
Against the tables and chairs.
<|endoftext|>

Outside the long window, With
his head on the stone sill, The dog is
lying, Gazing at his Beloved.
<|endoftext|>

His eyes are wet
and urgent, And his body
is taut and shaking.
<|endoftext|>

It is cold on the terrace; A pale
wind licks along the stone slabs, But the dog gazes
through the glass And is content.
<|endoftext|>

The Beloved
is writing a
letter.
<|endoftext|>

Occasionally
she speaks to the dog, But she is
thinking of her writing.
<|endoftext|>

Does she, too,
give her devotion to
one Not worthy?
<|endoftext|>

Licking passions have bitten their arabesques
into it, And the mark of them lies, in and out, Worm-like, With the beauty
of corroded copper patterning white steel.
<|endoftext|>

My brain is curved like a
scimitar, And sighs at its cutting
Like a sickle mowing grass.
<|endoftext|>

But of what
use is all
this to me!
<|endoftext|>

I, who am
set to crack stones In a
country lane!
<|endoftext|>

My heart is like a cleft
pomegranate Bleeding crimson seeds
And dripping them on the ground.
<|endoftext|>

My heart gapes because
it is ripe and over-full, And its
seeds are bursting from it.
<|endoftext|>

But how is
this other than a
torment to me!
<|endoftext|>

I, who am shut
up, with broken crockery,
In a dark closet!
<|endoftext|>

I have been temperate
always, But I am like to be
very drunk With your coming.
<|endoftext|>

There have been times I feared to walk down
the street Lest I should reel with the wine of you, And
jerk against my neighbours As they go by.
<|endoftext|>

I will mix me a drink of stars,-- Large stars
with polychrome needles, Small stars jetting maroon
and crimson, Cool, quiet, green stars.
<|endoftext|>

It will lap and scratch As I swallow
it down; And I shall feel it as a serpent of
fire, Coiling and twisting in my belly.
<|endoftext|>

The rain gullies the garden
paths And tinkles on the broad
sides of grass blades.
<|endoftext|>

A tree, at
the end of my arm,
is hazy with mist.
<|endoftext|>

Even so, I can see
that it has red berries, A scarlet fruit,
Filmed over with moisture.
<|endoftext|>

It seems as though the rain,
Dripping from it, Should be
tinged with colour.
<|endoftext|>

I desire the berries,
But, in the mist, I only scratch
my hand on the thorns.
<|endoftext|>

Probably,
too, they are
bitter.
<|endoftext|>

Hold your apron wide That I may pour my
gifts into it, So that scarcely shall your two arms
hinder them From falling to the ground.
<|endoftext|>

I would pour them upon you And
cover you, For greatly do I feel this need Of giving
you something, Even these poor things.
<|endoftext|>

Dearest
of my
Heart!
<|endoftext|>

When I go away
from you The world beats dead Like
a slackened drum.
<|endoftext|>

I call out for you against
the jutted stars And shout into
the ridges of the wind.
<|endoftext|>

Streets coming fast, One after the other, Wedge
you away from me, And the lamps of the city prick my
eyes So that I can no longer see your face.
<|endoftext|>

Why should I leave you,
To wound myself upon the sharp
edges of the night?
<|endoftext|>

Hold your soul
open for my
welcoming.
<|endoftext|>

Let the quiet of your spirit bathe me With
its clear and rippled coolness, That, loose-limbed and weary, I find rest, Outstretched
upon your peace, as on a bed of ivory.
<|endoftext|>

Between us
leapt a gold and
scarlet flame.
<|endoftext|>

Into the hollow
of the cupped, arched blue Of
Heaven it rose.
<|endoftext|>

Its flickering
tongues up-drew And vanished
in the sunshine.
<|endoftext|>

How it came We
guessed not, nor what thing could
be its name.
<|endoftext|>

From each to each had
sprung those sparks which flew Together
into fire.
<|endoftext|>

But we knew The
winds would slap and quench
it in their game.
<|endoftext|>

And so we graved and fashioned
marble blocks To treasure it, and placed
them round about.
<|endoftext|>

With pillared porticos
we wreathed the whole, And roofed
it with bright bronze.
<|endoftext|>

Behind carved
locks Flowered the tall
and sheltered flame.
<|endoftext|>

Without, The baffled
winds thrust at a column's
bole.
<|endoftext|>

Beneath this sod lie
the remains Of one who
died of growing pains.
<|endoftext|>

You ask
me for a
sonnet.
<|endoftext|>

Ah, my Dear, Can
clocks tick back to yesterday
at noon?
<|endoftext|>

Can cracked and fallen leaves
recall last June And leap up on
the boughs, now stiff and sere?
<|endoftext|>

Yes,
'tis
that!
<|endoftext|>

My shadow stretches forward,
and the ground Is dark in front because
the light's behind.
<|endoftext|>

It is grotesque, with such a funny
hat, In watching it and walking I have found More
than enough to occupy my mind.
<|endoftext|>

I cannot
turn, the light would
make me blind.
<|endoftext|>

1 A yellow band of light upon the street
Pours from an open door, and makes a wide Pathway of bright gold
across a sheet Of calm and liquid moonshine.
<|endoftext|>

From inside Come shouts and streams of laughter, and
a snatch Of song, soon drowned and lost again in mirth, The clip of tankards
on a table top, And stir of booted heels.
<|endoftext|>

Against the patch Of candle-light
a shadow falls, its girth Proclaims the host
himself, and master of his shop.
<|endoftext|>

2 This is the tavern
of one Hilverdink, Jan Hilverdink,
whose wines are much esteemed.
<|endoftext|>

Within his cellar men can have to drink The rarest
cordials old monks ever schemed To coax from pulpy grapes, and with nice art
Improve and spice their virgin juiciness.
<|endoftext|>

Here froths the amber beer of many a brew, Crowning
each pewter tankard with as smart A cap as ever in his wantonness
Winter set glittering on top of an old yew.
<|endoftext|>

3 Tall candles stand upon the
table, where Are twisted glasses, ruby-sparked
with wine, Clarets and ports.
<|endoftext|>

Those topaz bumpers
were Drained from slim, long-necked bottles
of the Rhine.
<|endoftext|>

The centre of the board is piled
with pipes, Slender and clean, the still unbaptized
clay Awaits its burning fate.
<|endoftext|>

Behind, the vault Stretches from dim to dark, a
groping way Bordered by casks and puncheons, whose brass stripes And
bands gleam dully still, beyond the gay tumult.
<|endoftext|>

4 "For good
old Master
Hilverdink, a toast!
<|endoftext|>

" Clamoured
a youth with tassels
on his boots.
<|endoftext|>

"Bring out your oldest brandy
for a boast, From that small barrel in the
very roots Of your deep cellar, man.
<|endoftext|>

Why
here is
Max!
<|endoftext|>

Welcome,
Max, you're scarcely
here in time.
<|endoftext|>

We want to drink to old
Jan's luck, and smoke His best tobacco
for a grand climax.
<|endoftext|>

Here, Jan, a paper, fragrant
as crushed thyme, We'll have the best to wish
you luck, or may we choke!
<|endoftext|>

"Well thought
of, Franz; here's luck to Mynheer
Jan.
<|endoftext|>

" The host set down a jar;
then to a vat Lost in the distance
of his cellar, ran.
<|endoftext|>

Max took a pipe as graceful as the
stem Of some long tulip, crammed it full, and drew The pungent
smoke deep to his grateful lung.
<|endoftext|>

It curled all blue throughout
the cave and flew Into
the silver night.
<|endoftext|>

At once there flung Into the crowded shop
a boy, who cried to them: 6 "Oh, sirs, is there some learned
lawyer here, Some advocate, or all-wise counsellor?
<|endoftext|>

My master sent me to inquire
where Such men do mostly be, but every door
Was shut and barred, for late has grown the hour.
<|endoftext|>

I pray you tell me where I
may now find One versed in law, the
matter will not wait.
<|endoftext|>

" "I am a lawyer,
boy," said Max, "my mind Is not locked to
my business, though 'tis late.
<|endoftext|>

I shall be glad
to serve what way is in
my power.
<|endoftext|>

7 Then once more, cloaked and ready, he set out,
Tripping the footsteps of the eager boy Along the dappled cobbles,
while the rout Within the tavern jeered at his employ.
<|endoftext|>

Through new-burst elm leaves filtered the white moon, Who peered and splashed
between the twinkling boughs, Flooded the open spaces, and took flight Before
tall, serried houses in platoon, Guarded by shadows.
<|endoftext|>

Past the Custom House
They took their hurried way
in the Spring-scented night.
<|endoftext|>

8 Before a
door which fronted a canal
The boy halted.
<|endoftext|>

A dim
tree-shaded
spot.
<|endoftext|>

The boy
knocked twice, and steps
approached.
<|endoftext|>

A flame Winked through the keyhole, then a
key was turned, And through the open door Max went toward
Another door, whence sound of voices came.
<|endoftext|>

He entered a
large room where
candelabra burned.
<|endoftext|>

9 An aged man
in quilted dressing gown
Rose up to greet him.
<|endoftext|>

"Sir," said Max, "you sent Your
messenger to seek throughout
the town A lawyer.
<|endoftext|>

I have small accomplishment,
But I am at your service, and my
name Is Max Breuck, Counsellor, at your command.
<|endoftext|>

" "Mynheer," replied the
aged man, "obliged Am I,
and count myself much privileged.
<|endoftext|>

I am Cornelius
Kurler, and my fame Is better known
on distant oceans than on land.
<|endoftext|>

10 My ship has tasted
water in strange seas, And bartered goods
at still uncharted isles.
<|endoftext|>

She's oft coquetted with
a tropic breeze, And sheered off
hurricanes with jaunty smiles.
<|endoftext|>

" "Tush, Kurler," here broke in the
other man, "Enough of poetry,
draw the deed and sign.
<|endoftext|>

" The old man seemed to
wizen at the voice, "My good friend, Grootver,--"
he at once began.
<|endoftext|>

"No introductions, let
us have some wine, And business, now that
you at last have made your choice.
<|endoftext|>

" 11 A harsh and
disagreeable man he proved to be, This Grootver,
with no single kindly thought.
<|endoftext|>

Kurler explained,
his old hands nervously
Twisting his beard.
<|endoftext|>

His vessel
he had bought From
Grootver.
<|endoftext|>

12 Little by little
Max made out the way That Grootver
pressed that poor harassed old man.
<|endoftext|>

His money he must have,
too long delay Had turned the usurer
to a ruffian.
<|endoftext|>

13 Such light and foamy silks, like crinkled cream, And
indigo more blue than sun-whipped seas, Spices and fragrant trees, a massive beam Of
sandalwood, and pungent China teas, Tobacco, coffee!
<|endoftext|>

"  Grootver
only
laughed.
<|endoftext|>

Max heard it all, and
worse than all he heard The deed to
which the sailor gave his word.
<|endoftext|>

He shivered, 'twas as if the villain
gaffed The old man with a boat-hook; bleeding, spent, He begged for
life nor knew at all the road he went.
<|endoftext|>

14 For Kurler had
a daughter, young and gay, Carefully reared
and shielded, rarely seen.
<|endoftext|>

But on one black and most unfriendly
day Grootver had caught her as she passed between The
kitchen and the garden.
<|endoftext|>

She had run In fear of him, his evil
leering eye, And when he came she, bolted in her room,
Refused to show, though gave no reason why.
<|endoftext|>

The spinning of her future
had begun, On quiet nights she heard
the whirring of her doom.
<|endoftext|>

15 Max mended an
old goosequill by the fire, Loathing his
work, but seeing no thing to do.
<|endoftext|>

He felt his hands were building
up the pyre To burn two souls, and seized with
vertigo He staggered to his chair.
<|endoftext|>

Before him lay White
paper still unspotted
by a crime.
<|endoftext|>

"Now, young man, write,"
said Grootver in
his ear.
<|endoftext|>

"`If in two years my vessel should yet
stay From Amsterdam, I give Grootver, sometime A
friend, my daughter for his lawful wife.
<|endoftext|>

" 16 And Kurler swore, a palsied,
tottering sound, And traced his name, a
shaking, wandering line.
<|endoftext|>

Then dazed
he sat there, speechless
from his wound.
<|endoftext|>

Grootver got
up:  "Fair voyage,
the brigantine!
<|endoftext|>

" He shuffled
from the room, and left
the house.
<|endoftext|>

His footsteps wore
to silence down
the street.
<|endoftext|>

At last the
aged man began
to rouse.
<|endoftext|>

With help he once
more gained his trembling
feet.
<|endoftext|>

Will
you watch over
her?
<|endoftext|>

I ask
a solemn
vow.
<|endoftext|>

" 17 Max laid his hand upon the old
man's arm, "Before God, sir, I vow, when you are gone, So to protect
your daughter from all harm As one man may.
<|endoftext|>

"  Thus sorrowful, forlorn, The situation
to Max Breuck appeared, He gave his promise almost without
thought, Nor looked to see a difficulty.
<|endoftext|>

"Bred Gently to watch a mother left alone; Bound by
a dying father's wish, who feared The world's accustomed harshness when he should
be dead; 18 Such was my case from youth, Mynheer Kurler.
<|endoftext|>

Last Winter she died also, and my
days Are passed in work, lest I should grieve for her, And undo
habits used to earn her praise.
<|endoftext|>

My leisure I will gladly
give to see Your household and your
daughter prosperous.
<|endoftext|>

" The sailor
said his thanks, but
turned away.
<|endoftext|>

He could not brook that his humility,
So little wonted, and so tremulous, Should first before
a stranger make such great display.
<|endoftext|>

19 "Come here to-morrow as the bells
ring noon, I sail at the full sea, my daughter
then I will make known to you.
<|endoftext|>

'Twill be a boon If after I have
bid good-by, and when Her eyeballs scorch with watching
me depart, You bring her home again.
<|endoftext|>

She lives with one
Old serving-woman, who has
brought her up.
<|endoftext|>

But that
is no friend for so free
a heart.
<|endoftext|>

No head
to match her
questions.
<|endoftext|>

It
is
done.
<|endoftext|>

And I must
sail away to come
and brim her cup.
<|endoftext|>

20 My ship's the fastest
that owns Amsterdam As home, so not
a letter can you send.
<|endoftext|>

I shall be back, before
to where I am Another
ship could reach.
<|endoftext|>

Now your
stipend--" Quickly Breuck
interposed.
<|endoftext|>

"When you once more
Tread on the stones which
pave our streets.
<|endoftext|>

To-morrow I will be,
at stroke of noon,
At the great wharf.
<|endoftext|>

"  Then hurrying, in spite Of cake
and wine the old man pressed upon Him ere he
went, he took his leave and shut the door.
<|endoftext|>

21 'Twas noon in
Amsterdam, the day was clear, And sunshine
tipped the pointed roofs with gold.
<|endoftext|>

The brown canals ran liquid
bronze, for here The sun sank deep into
the waters cold.
<|endoftext|>

And every clock
and belfry in the town Hammered,
and struck, and rang.
<|endoftext|>

Such peals of bells, To shake the sunny morning
into life, And to proclaim the middle, and the
crown, Of this most sparkling daytime!
<|endoftext|>

The crowd swells, Laughing
and pushing toward the quays in
friendly strife.
<|endoftext|>

22 The "Horn
of Fortune" sails away
to-day.
<|endoftext|>

At highest tide
she lets her anchor go, And
starts for China.
<|endoftext|>

Giddy in freshest
paint she curtseys low, And beckons to
her boats to let her start.
<|endoftext|>

Blue is the
ocean, with a flashing
breeze.
<|endoftext|>

The shining
waves are quick to take
her part.
<|endoftext|>

They
push and spatter
her.
<|endoftext|>

Her sails are loose, Her tackles
hanging, waiting men to seize And haul
them taut, with chanty-singing, as they choose.
<|endoftext|>

23 At the great wharf's
edge Mynheer Kurler stands, And by his side, his
daughter, young Christine.
<|endoftext|>

Max Breuck is there,
his hat held in his hands, Bowing
before them both.
<|endoftext|>

The brigantine Bounces
impatient at the long delay, Curvets
and jumps, a cable's length from shore.
<|endoftext|>

A heavy galliot unloads on
the walls Round, yellow cheeses, like gold cannon
balls Stacked on the stones in pyramids.
<|endoftext|>

Once more Kurler has
kissed Christine, and now
he is away.
<|endoftext|>

24 Christine stood rigid like
a frozen stone, Her hands wrung pale in
effort at control.
<|endoftext|>

Max moved aside and
let her be alone, For grief exacts each
penny of its toll.
<|endoftext|>

The dancing
boat tossed on the glinting
sea.
<|endoftext|>

A sun-path swallowed it in flaming
light, Then, shrunk a cockleshell, it came again
Upon the other side.
<|endoftext|>

Now on the
lee It took the "Horn
of Fortune".
<|endoftext|>

Straining sight Could see
it hauled aboard, men pulling
on the crane.
<|endoftext|>

25 Then up above the eager
brigantine, Along her slender masts, the sails took
flight, Were sheeted home, and ropes were coiled.
<|endoftext|>

The shine Of the wet
anchor, when its heavy weight
Rose splashing to the deck.
<|endoftext|>

These things they saw, Christine
and Max, upon the
crowded quay.
<|endoftext|>

They saw the sails grow white, then blue in shade, The ship had
turned, caught in a windy flaw She glided imperceptibly away,
Drew farther off and in the bright sky seemed to fade.
<|endoftext|>

26 Home, through the emptying
streets, Max took Christine, Who would have hid
her sorrow from his gaze.
<|endoftext|>

Before the iron
gateway, clasped between Each garden
wall, he stopped.
<|endoftext|>

She, in amaze, Asked,
"Do you enter not
then, Mynheer Breuck?
<|endoftext|>

My father
told me of your
courtesy.
<|endoftext|>

Since I am now your charge, 'tis meet for
me To show such hospitality as maiden may,
Without disdaining rules must not be broke.
<|endoftext|>

Katrina will
have coffee, and she
bakes today.
<|endoftext|>

" 27 She straight
unhasped the tall, beflowered
gate.
<|endoftext|>

Max started at
the beauty, at
the glare Of tints.
<|endoftext|>

At either end was set a wide Path
strewn with fine, red gravel, and such shows Of tulips in
their splendour flaunted everywhere!
<|endoftext|>

28 From side to side, midway
each path, there ran A longer one which
cut the space in two.
<|endoftext|>

Then the dame Katrina shook
them down, in pelting showers They plunged to earth,
and died transformed to sugared food.
<|endoftext|>

29 Against the high, encircling
walls were grapes, Nailed close to feel the baking of
the sun From glowing bricks.
<|endoftext|>

Their microscopic
shapes Half hidden by
serrated leaves.
<|endoftext|>

And one Old cherry
tossed its branches
near the door.
<|endoftext|>

Bordered along the wall, in beds between,
Flickering, streaming, nodding in the air, The pride of all the garden,
there were more Tulips than Max had ever dreamed or seen.
<|endoftext|>

They
jostled, mobbed, and
danced.
<|endoftext|>

Max stood
at helpless
stare.
<|endoftext|>

"  With girlish zest
To please her guest
she flew.
<|endoftext|>

A moment more
She came again, with her
old nurse behind.
<|endoftext|>

Then, sitting on the bench and knitting fast, She talked
as someone with a noble store Of hidden fancies, blown upon the
wind, Eager to flutter forth and leave their silent past.
<|endoftext|>

31 The little apple
leaves above their heads Let fall a
quivering sunshine.
<|endoftext|>

Quiet,
cool, In blossomed
boughs they sat.
<|endoftext|>

Beyond, the beds Of tulips blazed,
a proper vestibule And antechamber
to the rainbow.
<|endoftext|>

Dyes Of
prismed richness:
 Carmine.
<|endoftext|>

Blues Tinging
dark browns to
purple.
<|endoftext|>

Silvers flushed
To amethyst
and tinct with gold.
<|endoftext|>

Round eyes Of
scarlet, spotting tender
saffron hues.
<|endoftext|>

Violets sunk
to blacks, and reds in
orange crushed.
<|endoftext|>

32 Of every
pattern and in every
shade.
<|endoftext|>

Nacreous,
iridescent,
mottled, checked.
<|endoftext|>

Some purest sulphur-yellow,
others made An ivory-white
with disks of copper flecked.
<|endoftext|>

Sprinkled and
striped, tasselled, or keenest
edged.
<|endoftext|>

Striated, powdered,
freckled, long or
short.
<|endoftext|>

They bloomed, and seemed strange wonder-moths
new-fledged, Born of the spectrum wedded
to a flame.
<|endoftext|>

The shade within the arbour
made a port To o'ertaxed eyes, its
still, green twilight rest became.
<|endoftext|>

33 Her knitting-needles clicked and
Christine talked, This child matured to woman unaware,
The first time left alone.
<|endoftext|>

Now dreams
once balked Found
utterance.
<|endoftext|>

Max thought her
very
fair.
<|endoftext|>

Beneath her cap
her ornaments shone gold, And
purest gold they were.
<|endoftext|>

Kurler
was rich
And heedful.
<|endoftext|>

Her old maiden
aunt had died Whose darling
care she was.
<|endoftext|>

Now, growing
bold, She asked, had Max
a sister?
<|endoftext|>

Dropped a stitch
At her own
candour.
<|endoftext|>

Then she
paused and softly
sighed.
<|endoftext|>

34 Two
years was
long!
<|endoftext|>

She loved her
father well, But fears
she had not.
<|endoftext|>

He had always
been Just sailed or
sailing.
<|endoftext|>

And she must not dwell On
sad thoughts, he had told her so, and seen
Her smile at parting.
<|endoftext|>

But
she sighed
once more.
<|endoftext|>

Two years was
long; 'twas not one hour
yet!
<|endoftext|>

Mynheer
Grootver she would not
see at all.
<|endoftext|>

Yes, yes, she knew, but ere
the date so set, The "Horn of Fortune"
would be at the wall.
<|endoftext|>

When Max had
bid farewell, she
watched him from the door.
<|endoftext|>

Such things were Christine's world,
and his was she Winter drew near,
his sun was in her smiles.
<|endoftext|>

Another Spring, and
at his law he toiled, Unspoken hope
counselled a wise efficiency.
<|endoftext|>

36 Max Breuck was honour's soul,
he knew himself The guardian of
this girl; no more, no less.
<|endoftext|>

As one in charge of guineas on a shelf
Loose in a china teapot, may confess His need, but may
not borrow till his friend Comes back to give.
<|endoftext|>

So Max, in honour, said No word
of love or marriage; but the days He
clipped off on his almanac.
<|endoftext|>

The
end Must
come!
<|endoftext|>

The second year, with feet
of lead, Lagged slowly by till Spring
had plumped the willow sprays.
<|endoftext|>

37 Two years had made Christine
a woman grown, With dignity and
gently certain pride.
<|endoftext|>

But all her childhood
fancies had not flown, Her thoughts in lovely
dreamings seemed to glide.
<|endoftext|>

Max was her trusted
friend, did she confess A closer
happiness?
<|endoftext|>

Max
could
not tell.
<|endoftext|>

Two years were over
and his life he found Sphered
and complete.
<|endoftext|>

In restless eagerness
He waited for the "Horn
of Fortune".
<|endoftext|>

Well Had he
his promise kept, abating
not one pound.
<|endoftext|>

38 Spring
slipped away to
Summer.
<|endoftext|>

Still no
glass Sighted the
brigantine.
<|endoftext|>

His trespass
Was justified, for he had
won the game.
<|endoftext|>

Christine
begged time, more
time!
<|endoftext|>

Midsummer went,
And Grootver waxed
impatient.
<|endoftext|>

Still
the ship
Tarried.
<|endoftext|>

Christine, betrayed
and weary, sank To
dreadful terrors.
<|endoftext|>

One day,
crazed, she sent
For Max.
<|endoftext|>

"Come quickly," said her
note, "I skip The worst distress
until we meet.
<|endoftext|>

The
world is
blank.
<|endoftext|>

" 39 Through the long
sunshine of late afternoon
Max went to her.
<|endoftext|>

In the pleached alley,
lost In bitter reverie,
he found her soon.
<|endoftext|>

And sitting down beside her,
at the cost Of all his secret, "Dear," said he, "what thing
So suddenly has happened?
<|endoftext|>

"  Then, in tears, She told that Grootver, on the
following morn, Would come to marry her, and shuddering:
"I will die rather, death has lesser fears.
<|endoftext|>

" Max felt the shackles
drop from the oath which
he had sworn.
<|endoftext|>

40 "My Dearest
One, the hid joy
of my heart!
<|endoftext|>

I
love you,
oh!
<|endoftext|>

you must
indeed have
known.
<|endoftext|>

In strictest honour I have
played my part; But all this misery has
overthrown My scruples.
<|endoftext|>

If you love me, marry
me Before the sun has dipped
behind those trees.
<|endoftext|>

You cannot be wed
twice, and Grootver, foiled, Can
eat his anger.
<|endoftext|>

My care it shall be To pay your
father's debt, by such degrees As I can compass,
and for years I've greatly toiled.
<|endoftext|>

41 This is not haste, Christine,
for long I've known My love, and silence
forced upon my lips.
<|endoftext|>

I worship you with
all the strength I've shown
In keeping faith.
<|endoftext|>

"  With pleading
finger tips He
touched her arm.
<|endoftext|>

Dearest, speak,
I love
you.
<|endoftext|>

Do my words
fall too
swift now?
<|endoftext|>

They've been
in leash so long upon
the brink.
<|endoftext|>

" She sat quite
still, her body
loose and weak.
<|endoftext|>

Then into
him she melted,
all her soul at flow.
<|endoftext|>

42 And they were married ere
the westering sun Had disappeared
behind the garden trees.
<|endoftext|>

Within the arbour, long they lay embraced, In such
enraptured sweetness as they found Close-partnered each to each, and thinking soon To
be enwoven, long ere night to morning faced.
<|endoftext|>

Then, far spent, Our
eyes will close to
undisturbed rest.
<|endoftext|>

For that
desired thing I leave
you now.
<|endoftext|>

To pinnacle this day's accomplishment,
By telling Grootver that a bootless quest Is his, and
that his schemes have met a knock-down blow.
<|endoftext|>

" 44 But Christine clung to
him with sobbing cries, Pleading for love's
sake that he leave her not.
<|endoftext|>

And wound her arms
about his knees and thighs As
he stood over her.
<|endoftext|>

With dread, begot Of Grootver's
name, and silence, and the night,
She shook and trembled.
<|endoftext|>

Words in moaning
plaint Wooed him to
stay.
<|endoftext|>

She feared,
she knew not why, Yet
greatly feared.
<|endoftext|>

She seemed some
anguished saint Martyred
by visions.
<|endoftext|>

Max Breuck soothed her fright
With wisdom, then stepped out under
the cooling sky.
<|endoftext|>

45 But at the gate once
more she held him close And quenched her heart
again upon his lips.
<|endoftext|>

"My Sweetheart,
why this
terror?
<|endoftext|>

I propose
But to be gone one
hour!
<|endoftext|>

Evening slips Away,
this errand must
be done.
<|endoftext|>

First goes my
father, if I lose
you now!
<|endoftext|>

" She grasped him
