quietly sitting on the window-sill,
eating human eyes.
<|endoftext|>

IV How
hot the sheets
are!
<|endoftext|>

His skin is tormented
with pricks, and over him sticks,
and never moves, an eye.
<|endoftext|>

It lights the
sky with blood,
and drips blood.
<|endoftext|>

And the drops sizzle on his bare
skin, and he smells them burning in, and branding
his body with the name "Annette".
<|endoftext|>

The blood-red sky
is outside his
window now.
<|endoftext|>

And his heart
wrenches and
pounds "Annette!
<|endoftext|>

" The lead of the roof is scorching,
he ricochets, gets to the edge, bounces
over and disappears.
<|endoftext|>

The bellying
clouds are red as they swing over
the housetops.
<|endoftext|>

V The air is of
silver and pearl, for the night
is liquid with moonlight.
<|endoftext|>

How the ruin
glistens, like a palace
of ice!
<|endoftext|>

Only two black
holes swallow the brilliance
of the moon.
<|endoftext|>

Deflowered
windows, sockets without
sight.
<|endoftext|>

A man
stands before
the house.
<|endoftext|>

He sees the silver-blue moonlight, and
set in it, over his head, staring and flickering,
eyes of geranium red.
<|endoftext|>

I Over
the yawning chimney
hangs the fog.
<|endoftext|>

Drip--hiss--drip--hiss-- fall the raindrops
on the oaken log which burns, and steams,
and smokes the ceiling beams.
<|endoftext|>

Drip--hiss--the
rain never
stops.
<|endoftext|>

The wide, state bed
shivers beneath its
velvet coverlet.
<|endoftext|>

Above, dim, in
the smoke, a tarnished
coronet gleams dully.
<|endoftext|>

Overhead
hammers and chinks
the rain.
<|endoftext|>

Fearfully wails the wind down
distant corridors, and there comes the swish and
sigh of rushes lifted off the floors.
<|endoftext|>

The arras blows
sidewise out from the wall,
and then falls back again.
<|endoftext|>

It is my lady's
key, confided with much nice
cunning, whisperingly.
<|endoftext|>

He enters on a sob of
wind, which gutters the candles
almost to swaling.
<|endoftext|>

The fire
flutters
and drops.
<|endoftext|>

He shuts
the
door.
<|endoftext|>

The rushes fall
again to stillness along
the floor.
<|endoftext|>

Outside, the
wind goes
wailing.
<|endoftext|>

The velvet coverlet
of the wide bed is
smooth and cold.
<|endoftext|>

Above, in the
firelight, winks the
coronet of tarnished gold.
<|endoftext|>

The knight shivers in
his coat of fur, and holds out his hands
to the withering flame.
<|endoftext|>

She is always
the same, a sweet
coquette.
<|endoftext|>

He
will wait for
her.
<|endoftext|>

How the log
hisses
and drips!
<|endoftext|>

How warm and
satisfying will
be her lips!
<|endoftext|>

Is it guilt to free a lady
from her palsied lord, absent and fighting,
terribly abhorred?
<|endoftext|>

He stirs a
booted heel and kicks
a rolling coal.
<|endoftext|>

His spur
clinks on the
hearth.
<|endoftext|>

Overhead,
the rain hammers
and chinks.
<|endoftext|>

She
is so pure
and whole.
<|endoftext|>

Only because he has her soul will
she resign herself to him, for where the soul has gone,
the body must be given as a sign.
<|endoftext|>

He takes her by
the divine right of
the only lover.
<|endoftext|>

He has sworn
to fight her lord, and wed
her after.
<|endoftext|>

Should he be overborne,
she will die adoring him, forlorn,
shriven by her great love.
<|endoftext|>

Above, the
coronet winks in
the darkness.
<|endoftext|>

Drip--hiss--fall
the
raindrops.
<|endoftext|>

The arras blows
out from the wall, and a door bangs
in a far-off hall.
<|endoftext|>

The
candles
swale.
<|endoftext|>

In the gale
the moat below plunges
and spatters.
<|endoftext|>

Will the lady
lose courage and not
come?
<|endoftext|>

The rain claps
on a loosened
rafter.
<|endoftext|>

Is
that
laughter?
<|endoftext|>

The room
is filled with lisps and
whispers.
<|endoftext|>

One candle
drowns and the other
gutters.
<|endoftext|>

Is that the rain which pads
and patters, is it the wind through the winding
entries which chatters?
<|endoftext|>

The state bed is very
cold and he is
alone.
<|endoftext|>

How far from
the wall the arras
is blown!
<|endoftext|>

It is no
storm which makes these little
chuckling sounds.
<|endoftext|>

By the Great Wounds of Holy
Jesus, it is his dear lady, kissing
and clasping someone!
<|endoftext|>

Through the sobbing storm
he hears her love take form and
flutter out in words.
<|endoftext|>

They prick into his ears and stun
his desire, which lies within him,
hard and dead, like frozen fire.
<|endoftext|>

And the
little noise
never stops.
<|endoftext|>

He tears down the arras
from before an inner chamber's
bolted door.
<|endoftext|>

II The state
bed shivers in the
watery dawn.
<|endoftext|>

For
the storm never
stops.
<|endoftext|>

On the velvet coverlet
lie two bodies, stripped and fair in
the cold, grey air.
<|endoftext|>

Drip--hiss--fall the blood-drops, for
the bleeding never
stops.
<|endoftext|>

At each side of
the bed, on the
floor, is a head.
<|endoftext|>

A man's on this side, a woman's
on that, and the red blood oozes
along the rush mat.
<|endoftext|>

A wisp of paper is
twisted carefully into the strands
of the dead man's hair.
<|endoftext|>

It says, "My Lord:  Your
wife's paramour has paid with
his life for the high favour.
<|endoftext|>

" Through the lady's
silver fillet is wound
another paper.
<|endoftext|>

It reads, "Most noble
Lord:  Your wife's misdeeds are as a double-stranded
necklace of beads.
<|endoftext|>

But I have engaged
that, on your return, she
shall welcome you here.
<|endoftext|>

She will not spurn your
love as before, you have still
the best part of her.
<|endoftext|>

Her blood was red, her
body white, they will both be
here for your delight.
<|endoftext|>

The soul inside was a
lump of dirt, I have rid you of that with
a spurt of my sword point.
<|endoftext|>

Good luck
to your
pleasure.
<|endoftext|>

She will be
quite complaisant,
my friend, I wager.
<|endoftext|>

" The end was
a splashed flourish
of ink.
<|endoftext|>

In the passage is heard
the clink of armour, the tread
of a heavy man.
<|endoftext|>

The door bursts open and standing there,
his thin hair wavering in the glare of steely
daylight, is my Lord of Clair.
<|endoftext|>

Over the
yawning chimney
hangs the fog.
<|endoftext|>

Overhead hammers
and chinks the rain which never
stops.
<|endoftext|>

The velvet coverlet
is sodden and wet, yet the
roof beams are tight.
<|endoftext|>

Overhead, the
coronet gleams with its blackened
gold, winking and blinking.
<|endoftext|>

Among the rushes
three corpses are
growing cold.
<|endoftext|>

III In the castle church you may see
them stand, Two sumptuous tombs on either hand Of the choir, my
Lord's and my Lady's, grand In sculptured filigrees.
<|endoftext|>

And where the transepts of the church expand,
A crusader, come from the Holy Land, Lies with
crossed legs and embroidered band.
<|endoftext|>

The page's
name became a brand
For shame.
<|endoftext|>

He was buried in crawling
sand, After having been burnt by
royal command.
<|endoftext|>

The Bell
in the convent tower
swung.
<|endoftext|>

High overhead the
great sun hung, A navel
for the curving sky.
<|endoftext|>

The air
was a blue
clarity.
<|endoftext|>

Swallows
flew, And
a cock crew.
<|endoftext|>

The iron clanging
sank through the light air, Rustled
over with blowing branches.
<|endoftext|>

A flare Of spotted green, and a
snake had gone Into the bed where the snowdrops shone
In green new-started, Their white bells parted.
<|endoftext|>

Two by two, in a long
brown line, The nuns were walking to breathe
the fine Bright April air.
<|endoftext|>

They must go in
soon And work at their tasks all
the afternoon.
<|endoftext|>

But
this time
is theirs!
<|endoftext|>

They walk
in
pairs.
<|endoftext|>

First comes the Abbess, preoccupied
And slow, as a woman often tried, With
her temper in bond.
<|endoftext|>

Then the
oldest
nun.
<|endoftext|>

Then younger and younger,
until the last one Has a laugh on
her lips, And fairly skips.
<|endoftext|>

They wind about the
gravel walks And all the long
line buzzes and talks.
<|endoftext|>

They step in time
to the ringing bell, With scarcely
a shadow.
<|endoftext|>

The sun is well In
the core of a sky
Domed silverly.
<|endoftext|>

Sister Marguerite
said:  "The pears will
soon bud.
<|endoftext|>

" Sister Angelique
said she must get her spud And free the
earth round the jasmine roots.
<|endoftext|>

Sister
Veronique said:
 "Oh, look at those shoots!
<|endoftext|>

There's a
crocus up, With
a purple cup.
<|endoftext|>

" But Sister Clotilde said nothing
at all, She looked up and down the old grey wall To
see if a lizard were basking there.
<|endoftext|>

She looked across the
garden to where A sycamore
Flanked the garden door.
<|endoftext|>

She was restless, although her little feet
danced, And quite unsatisfied, for it chanced Her morning's work
had hung in her mind And would not take form.
<|endoftext|>

She could not find The
beautifulness For
the Virgin's dress.
<|endoftext|>

Should it be
of pink, or
damasked blue?
<|endoftext|>

Or perhaps
lilac with gold
shotted through?
<|endoftext|>

Should it be banded
with yellow and white Roses,
or sparked like a frosty night?
<|endoftext|>

Or a crimson
sheen Over some sort
of green?
<|endoftext|>

But Clotilde's eyes saw nothing new In
all the garden, no single hue So lovely or so
marvellous That its use would not seem impious.
<|endoftext|>

So on she
walked, And the others
talked.
<|endoftext|>

Sister Elisabeth edged away From what her
companion had to say, For Sister Marthe saw the world in little, She
weighed every grain and recorded each tittle.
<|endoftext|>

She did plain
stitching And worked
in the kitchen.
<|endoftext|>

"Sister Radegonde
knows the apples won't last, I
told her so this Friday past.
<|endoftext|>

I must speak
to her before
Compline.
<|endoftext|>

" Her words were like
dust motes in slanting
sunshine.
<|endoftext|>

The other
nun sighed, With her pleasure
quite dried.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly Sister
Berthe cried out: "The snowdrops
are blooming!
<|endoftext|>

"  They
turned
about.
<|endoftext|>

The little white cups bent over the
ground, And in among the light stems wound A crested
snake, With his eyes awake.
<|endoftext|>

His crest was amber
glittered with blue, And opaque
so the sun came shining through.
<|endoftext|>

It seemed a
crown with
fiery points.
<|endoftext|>

When he quivered all down
his scaly joints, From every
slot The sparkles shot.
<|endoftext|>

The nuns huddled
tightly together, fear Catching
their senses.
<|endoftext|>

But Clotilde must peer
More closely at the beautiful snake,
She seemed entranced and eased.
<|endoftext|>

Could she make
Colours so rare,
The dress were there.
<|endoftext|>

The Abbess
shook off her
lethargy.
<|endoftext|>

"Sisters,
we will walk
on," said she.
<|endoftext|>

Sidling away from the
snowdrop bed, The line curved forwards,
the Abbess ahead.
<|endoftext|>

Only Clotilde
Was the last to
yield.
<|endoftext|>

When the recreation
hour was done Each went
in to her task.
<|endoftext|>

Alone In the library, with
its great north light, Clotilde wrought at an exquisite
Wreath of flowers For her Book of Hours.
<|endoftext|>

They framed the
picture she had
made, Half-delighted and half-afraid.
<|endoftext|>

In a courtyard with a lozenged
floor The Virgin watched, and through the arched door The angel
came Like a springing flame.
<|endoftext|>

His wings were dipped in violet
fire, His limbs were strung to
holy desire.
<|endoftext|>

He lowered his head and
passed under the arch, And the air
seemed beating a solemn march.
<|endoftext|>

The Virgin waited
With eyes
dilated.
<|endoftext|>

Her face was quiet
and innocent, And beautiful
with her strange assent.
<|endoftext|>

A silver thread
about her head Her halo
was poised.
<|endoftext|>

But in the stead Of
her gown, there remained The
vellum, unstained.
<|endoftext|>

Clotilde painted the flowers
patiently, Lingering over
each tint and dye.
<|endoftext|>

She could spend great pains,
now she had seen That curious,
unimagined green.
<|endoftext|>

A colour
so strange It had seemed
to change.
<|endoftext|>

She thought it
had altered while
she gazed.
<|endoftext|>

At first it had been simple green; then glazed All
over with twisting flames, each spot A molten colour, trembling
and hot, And every eye Seemed to liquefy.
<|endoftext|>

She had made
a plan, and her
spirits danced.
<|endoftext|>

After all, she had only glanced At that
wonderful snake, and she must know Just what hues made the creature
throw Those splashes and sprays Of prismed rays.
<|endoftext|>

When evening prayers
were sung and said, The nuns lit
their tapers and went to bed.
<|endoftext|>

And soon in the convent there was no
light, For the moon did not rise until late that night,
Only the shine Of the lamp at the shrine.
<|endoftext|>

Clotilde lay
still in her trembling
sheets.
<|endoftext|>

Her heart
shook her body
with its beats.
<|endoftext|>

She could not see till the moon should
rise, So she whispered prayers and kept her eyes
On the window-square Till light should be there.
<|endoftext|>

The faintest shadow
of a branch Fell
on the floor.
<|endoftext|>

Clotilde, grown staunch With solemn
purpose, softly rose And fluttered down between
the rows Of sleeping nuns.
<|endoftext|>

She
almost
runs.
<|endoftext|>

She must go out through the little
side door Lest the nuns who were always praying before
The Virgin's altar should hear her pass.
<|endoftext|>

She pushed the bolts, and
over the grass The red moon's
brim Mounted its rim.
<|endoftext|>

Her shadow crept up the convent wall As
she swiftly left it, over all The garden lay the level glow Of
a moon coming up, very big and slow.
<|endoftext|>

The
gravel
glistened.
<|endoftext|>

She stopped
and
listened.
<|endoftext|>

It was still, and
the moonlight was
getting clearer.
<|endoftext|>

She laughed a little,
but she felt queerer Than ever
before.
<|endoftext|>

The snowdrop bed
Was reached and she bent
down her head.
<|endoftext|>

On the striped
ground The snake
was wound.
<|endoftext|>

For a moment Clotilde
paused in alarm, Then she rolled up
her sleeve and stretched out her arm.
<|endoftext|>

She thought she
heard steps, she must
be quick.
<|endoftext|>

She darted her hand
out, and seized the thick Wriggling
slime, Only just in time.
<|endoftext|>

The old gardener came muttering
down the path, And his shadow fell like a broad, black swath,
And covered Clotilde and the angry snake.
<|endoftext|>

He bit her,
but what difference
did that make!
<|endoftext|>

The Virgin
should dress In his
loveliness.
<|endoftext|>

The gardener was covering
his new-set plants For the night was chilly, and nothing daunts
Your lover of growing things.
<|endoftext|>

He spied Something to do
and turned aside, And the moonlight
streamed On Clotilde, and gleamed.
<|endoftext|>

His business
finished the gardener
rose.
<|endoftext|>

He shook and swore, for the moonlight shows
A girl with a fire-tongued serpent, she Grasping him,
laughing, while quietly Her eyes are weeping.
<|endoftext|>

Is
he
sleeping?
<|endoftext|>

He thinks it is some holy vision, Brushes that aside
and with decision Jumps--and hits the snake with his stick, Crushes his spine,
and then with quick, Urgent command Takes her hand.
<|endoftext|>

The gardener sucks the poison
and spits, Cursing and praying as befits A poor
old man half out of his wits.
<|endoftext|>

"Whatever possessed you, Sister,
it's Hatched of a devil And
very evil.
<|endoftext|>

It's one of them
horrid basilisks
You read about.
<|endoftext|>

They say a man risks
His life to touch it, but I guess I've
sucked it Out by now.
<|endoftext|>

Lucky I
chucked it Away
from you.
<|endoftext|>

He is dead now, but his
colours won't fade At once, and by noon I
shall have made The Virgin's robe.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, Francois, see
How kindly the moon shines
down on me!
<|endoftext|>

I can't die
yet, For the
task was set.
<|endoftext|>

" "You won't die now, for I've
sucked it away," Grumbled old
Francois, "so have your play.
<|endoftext|>

If the Virgin is set on
snake's colours so strong,--" "Francois, don't say
things like that, it is wrong.
<|endoftext|>

" So Clotilde
vented
Her creed.
<|endoftext|>

"He can't do
no more harm, Sister,"
said he.
<|endoftext|>

"Paint as
much as
you like.
<|endoftext|>

"  And gingerly
He picked up the snake
with his stick.
<|endoftext|>

Clotilde Thanked him, and begged that
he would shield Her secret, though itching
To talk in the kitchen.
<|endoftext|>

At dawn's first
saffron-spired warning Clotilde
was up.
<|endoftext|>

And all that morning, Except when she went
to the chapel to pray, She painted, and when the April
day Was hot with sun, Clotilde had done.
<|endoftext|>

She drooped, though her heart beat loud At
the beauty before her, and her spirit bowed To the
Virgin her finely-touched thought had made.
<|endoftext|>

A lady,
in excellence arrayed,
And wonder-souled.
<|endoftext|>

From long fasting Clotilde felt weary
and faint, But her eyes were starred like those of a saint Enmeshed
in Heaven's beatitude.
<|endoftext|>

A sudden clamour
hurled its rude Force to break
Her vision awake.
<|endoftext|>

The door nearly leapt
from its hinges, pushed By the
multitude of nuns.
<|endoftext|>

They hushed When they saw Clotilde,
in perfect quiet, Smiling, a little
perplexed at the riot.
<|endoftext|>

And all
the hive Buzzed
"She's alive!
<|endoftext|>

He had found the strain Of
silence too great, and preferred the pain
Of a conscience outraged.
<|endoftext|>

The news had spread,
And all were convinced Clotilde
must be dead.
<|endoftext|>

For Francois, to
spite them, Had not seen
fit to right them.
<|endoftext|>

How could we have
guessed Our convent
so blessed!
<|endoftext|>

To
have
you die!
<|endoftext|>

And I, who am
A hollow, living shell, the
grave Is empty of me.
<|endoftext|>

Holy Mary, I
crave To be taken, Dear Mother, Instead
of this other.
<|endoftext|>

" She dropped on her knees and
silently prayed, With anguished hands and
tears delayed To a painful slowness.
<|endoftext|>

The minutes
drew To
fractions.
<|endoftext|>

Then the west wind
blew The sound of a bell, On
a gusty swell.
<|endoftext|>

It came skipping over the slates of
the roof, And the bright bell-notes seemed a reproof To
grief, in the eye of so fair a day.
<|endoftext|>

The Abbess,
comforted, ceased
to pray.
<|endoftext|>

And the sun lit
the flowers In Clotilde's
Book of Hours.
<|endoftext|>

It glistened the green of the Virgin's dress And
made the red spots, in a flushed excess, Pulse and start; and the violet
wings Of the angel were colour which shines and sings.
<|endoftext|>

The book seemed
a choir Of rainbow
fire.
<|endoftext|>

The Abbess crossed herself, and each nun Did
the same, then one by one, They filed to the chapel, that incensed prayers
Might plead for the life of this sister of theirs.
<|endoftext|>

Clotilde,
the
Inspired!
<|endoftext|>

She only
felt
tired.
<|endoftext|>

The old chronicles say
she did not die Until
heavy with years.
<|endoftext|>

And that is why There hangs in the convent
church a basket Of osiered silver, a holy casket,
And treasured therein A dried snake-skin.
<|endoftext|>

Panels of claret
and blue which shine Under the
moon like lees of wine.
<|endoftext|>

A coronet done in a golden
scroll, And wheels which blunder and creak as they roll Through
the muddy ruts of a moorland track.
<|endoftext|>

They
daren't
look back!
<|endoftext|>

They are whipping
and cursing the
horses.
<|endoftext|>

What brutes
men are when
they think they're scored.
<|endoftext|>

They are scared
half out of their wits,
poor souls.
<|endoftext|>

For my lord has a casket
full of rolls Of minted sovereigns,
and silver bars.
<|endoftext|>

I laugh to
think how he'll show his scars
In London to-morrow.
<|endoftext|>

He whines with
rage In his varnished
cage.
<|endoftext|>

My lady
has shoved her rings over
her toes.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis an ancient
trick every night-rider
knows.
<|endoftext|>

But I shall relieve her of them yet, When
I see she limps in the minuet I must beg to
celebrate this night, And the green moonlight.
<|endoftext|>

There's nothing to hurry
about, the plain Is hours
long, and the mud's a strain.
<|endoftext|>

My gelding's uncommonly
strong in the loins, In half an
hour I'll bag the coins.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis a clear,
sweet night on the
turn of Spring.
<|endoftext|>

The
chase is
the thing!
<|endoftext|>

How the coach flashes
and wobbles, the moon Dripping
down so quietly on it.
<|endoftext|>

A tune Is beating out
of the curses and screams, And the cracking
all through the painted seams.
<|endoftext|>

Steady, old
horse, we'll keep
it in sight.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis
a rare fine
night!
<|endoftext|>

There's a clump of trees on
the dip of the down, And the sky shimmers
where it hangs over the town.
<|endoftext|>

It seems a shame to break the air
In two with this pistol, but I've my share
Of drudgery like other men.
<|endoftext|>

Hold up,
you beast, now
what the devil!
<|endoftext|>

Confound this
moor for a pockholed, evil,
Rotten marsh.
<|endoftext|>

My
right
leg's snapped.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis a mercy
he's rolled, but I'm nicely
capped.
<|endoftext|>

A broken-legged
man and a
broken-legged horse!
<|endoftext|>

They'll
get me, of
course.
<|endoftext|>

The cursed coach will reach the town And
they'll all come out, every loafer grown A
lion to handcuff a man that's down.
<|endoftext|>

Oh, the
coachman's bulleted
hat!
<|endoftext|>

I'll give
it a head
to fit it pat.
<|endoftext|>

_They handcuffed the body just
for style, And they hung him in chains for the
volatile Wind to scour him flesh from bones.
<|endoftext|>

Way out on the moor you
can hear the groans His gibbet makes
when it blows a gale.
<|endoftext|>

'Tis a
common
tale.
<|endoftext|>

_ Paul Jannes was working very late, For
this watch must be done by eight To-morrow or the
Cardinal Would certainly be vexed.
<|endoftext|>

So they picked his pocket,
And he paid in jewels for his
slobbering kisses.
<|endoftext|>

This watch was made to buy him blisses
From an Austrian countess on her way Home,
and she meant to start next day.
<|endoftext|>

Paul worked by the pointed, tulip-flame Of a tallow
candle, and became So absorbed, that his old clock made him
wince Striking the hour a moment since.
<|endoftext|>

Its echo, only
half apprehended, Lingered
about the room.
<|endoftext|>

He ended Screwing the little rubies in,
Setting the wheels to lock and spin, Curling the infinitesimal
springs, Fixing the filigree hands.
<|endoftext|>

Chippings Of
precious stones lay
strewn about.
<|endoftext|>

The table before
him was a rout Of splashes
and sparks of coloured light.
<|endoftext|>

There was yellow gold
in sheets, and quite A heap of
emeralds, and steel.
<|endoftext|>

Here was
a gem, there was
a wheel.
<|endoftext|>

And glasses lay like limpid lakes Shining and
still, and there were flakes Of silver, and shavings of pearl, And little
wires all awhirl With the light of the candle.
<|endoftext|>

He took the watch And wound its hands
about to match The time, then glanced up to
take the hour From the hanging clock.
<|endoftext|>

Good,
Merciful
Power!
<|endoftext|>

How came that shadow
on the wall, No woman
was in the room!
<|endoftext|>

His tall Chiffonier
stood gaunt behind
His chair.
<|endoftext|>

His old
cloak, rabbit-lined,
Hung from a peg.
<|endoftext|>

The
door was
closed.
<|endoftext|>

Just for a
moment he must
have dozed.
<|endoftext|>

He looked again,
and saw it
plain.
<|endoftext|>

The silhouette made a blue-black stain On
the opposite wall, and it never wavered Even when the
candle quavered Under his panting breath.
<|endoftext|>

What made That beautiful, dreadful thing, that
shade Of something so lovely, so exquisite, Cast from a substance which
the sight Had not been tutored to perceive?
<|endoftext|>

Paul brushed
his eyes across
his sleeve.
<|endoftext|>

Clear-cut, the Shadow on
the wall Gleamed black, and never
moved at all.
<|endoftext|>

Paul's watches were like amulets, Wrought into
patterns and rosettes; The cases were all set with stones,
And wreathing lines, and shining zones.
<|endoftext|>

He knew the beauty in a curve, And
the Shadow tortured every nerve With its perfect
rhythm of outline Cutting the whitewashed wall.
<|endoftext|>

So fine Was the neck he
knew he could have spanned It about with
the fingers of one hand.
<|endoftext|>

The lady
was edged with the
stamp of race.
<|endoftext|>

A
singular vision
in such a place.
<|endoftext|>

He moved the candle to
the tall Chiffonier; the
Shadow stayed on the wall.
<|endoftext|>

He threw his cloak upon
a chair, And still the lady's
face was there.
<|endoftext|>

From every corner
of the room He saw, in the patch of
light, the gloom That was the lady.
<|endoftext|>

Her violet bloom
Was almost brighter than that
which came From his candle's tulip-flame.
<|endoftext|>

He set the filigree hands; he laid
The watch in the case which he had made; He put on his
rabbit cloak, and snuffed His candle out.
<|endoftext|>

The room seemed
stuffed With
darkness.
<|endoftext|>

Softly he crossed
the floor, And let himself
out through the door.
<|endoftext|>

The sun was flashing
from every pin And wheel,
when Paul let himself in.
<|endoftext|>

The whitewashed
walls were hot
with light.
<|endoftext|>

The room was the core of a
chrysolite, Burning and shimmering
with fiery might.
<|endoftext|>

The sun was so bright that
no shadow could fall From the furniture
upon the wall.
<|endoftext|>

Paul sighed as he looked at
the empty space Where a glare
usurped the lady's place.
<|endoftext|>

He settled himself to his work, but his mind Wandered,
and he would wake to find His hand suspended, his eyes grown dim, And
nothing advanced beyond the rim Of his dreaming.
<|endoftext|>

The Cardinal sent to
pay For his watch, which had purchased
so fine a day.
<|endoftext|>

But Paul could hardly
touch the gold, It seemed the price
of his Shadow, sold.
<|endoftext|>

With the first twilight he struck a
match And watched the little blue stars hatch Into
an egg of perfect flame.
<|endoftext|>

He lit his candle,
and almost in shame At his
eagerness, lifted his eyes.
<|endoftext|>

The Shadow was there,
and its precise Outline etched
the cold, white wall.
<|endoftext|>

The young
man swore, "By
God!
<|endoftext|>

You, Paul, There's
something the matter
with your brain.

Go home now

" The next day was
a storm, the rain Whispered
and scratched at the window-pane.
<|endoftext|>

A grey and

The little shop.
<|endoftext|>

The watches,

<|endoftext|>

<|endoftext|>

Paul's head was heavy,
his hands obeyed No orders,
for his fancy strayed.
<|endoftext|>

His work became a
simple round Of watches repaired
and watches wound.
<|endoftext|>

The slanting ribbons of the

saw the silver lines in vain.
<|endoftext|>

In the morning

perfect jacinths.
<|endoftext|>

When, at dusk, the
Shadow grew Upon the

<|endoftext|>

Holding the watch, he spoke to

Into one brief sign of being.
<|endoftext|>

Turn your eyes this way, and seeing This watch, made from
those sweet curves Where your hair from your forehead swerves, Accept the gift
which I have wrought With your fairness in my thought.
<|endoftext|>

Grant me this, and
I shall be Honoured
overwhelmingly.

" The Shadow rested
black and still, And the wind sighed
over the window-sill.
<|endoftext|>

The face was of crystal, and engraved
Upon it the figures flashed and waved With zircons,
and beryls, and amethysts.
<|endoftext|>

and his trysts At night with the
Shadow were his alone.

task was done.
<|endoftext|>

The night that
the jewel was
worthy to give.
<|endoftext|>

Paul watched the long hours of daylight live To the
faintest streak; then lit his light, And sharp against the wall's pure white
The outline of the Shadow started Into form.

His burning-hearted Words
so long imprisoned swelled To
tumbling speech.

The
Shadow

<|endoftext|>

The Shadow
kept Its perfect
passiveness.
<|endoftext|>

He wooed her with the work

<|endoftext|>

Eased the ache of his

<|endoftext|>

His days passed in a

<|endoftext|>

Seasons shot by,
uncognisant
He worked.
<|endoftext|>

The Shadow
came to haunt Even

<|endoftext|>

Sometimes quite plain He

<|endoftext|>

No sun was bright
Enough to dazzle that
from his sight.
<|endoftext|>

Then he
would curse Himself
and her!
<|endoftext|>

And more, the beauty
he could not make, And give her,
for her comfort's sake!
<|endoftext|>

why She scorned the best which he could buy.
<|endoftext|>

He would pray as to
some high-niched saint, That she would cure him of

<|endoftext|>

He would clutch the wall With his bleeding

hold her, and make her live!
<|endoftext|>

With sobs he would
ask her to forgive All
he had done.
<|endoftext|>

driven To madness by the sight of Heaven.
<|endoftext|>

patience, said his skill Should break the spell.
<|endoftext|>

A man's sworn will
Could compass life, even

He

<|endoftext|>

The edge of

<|endoftext|>

The lips of the

<|endoftext|>

<|endoftext|>

The crisis passed, he would wake and smile With a vacant
joy, half-imbecile And quite confused, not being certain Why he was suffering; a curtain
Fallen over the tortured mind beguiled His sorrow.

and gems, with glee Calling the Shadow to look and see How the spots

"Mother, the green Has

Holy Mother

very

His little child

In the darkness.

Was blown from the mountain beds.

The heart of a

<|endoftext|>

Life is a stream On which we strew Petal by petal
the flower of our heart; The end lost in dream, They float past
our view, We only watch their glad, early start.
<|endoftext|>

Freighted with hope, Crimsoned with joy, We scatter
the leaves of our opening rose; Their widening scope,
Their distant employ, We never shall know.
<|endoftext|>

And the stream as it flows Sweeps
them away, Each one is gone Ever
beyond into infinite ways.
<|endoftext|>

We alone stay While years
hurry on, The flower fared forth, though
its fragrance still stays.
<|endoftext|>

So through the vacancy of busy
life At intervals you cross my path and bring The
deep solemnity of passing years.
<|endoftext|>

For you I have shed bitter tears,
for you I have relinquished that for which my
heart Cried out in selfish longing.
<|endoftext|>

And to-night Having
just left you, I can say:
 "'T is well.
<|endoftext|>

Thank God that I have known
a soul so true, So nobly just,
so worthy to be loved!
<|endoftext|>

" Stupefy my heart to every day's monotony,
Seal up my eyes, I would not look so far, Chasten my steps to peaceful
regularity, Bow down my head lest I behold a star.
<|endoftext|>

Fill my days with work, a thousand calm necessities Leaving
no moment to consecrate to hope, Girdle my thoughts within the dull circumferences
Of facts which form the actual in one short hour's scope.
<|endoftext|>

Give me dreamless sleep, and loose night's power over me, Shut
my ears to sounds only tumultuous then, Bid Fancy slumber, and steal away
its potency, Or Nature wakes and strives to live again.
<|endoftext|>

Let each day pass, well ordered in its usefulness,
Unlit by sunshine, unscarred by storm; Dower me with strength and curb all foolish
eagerness -- The law exacts obedience.
<|endoftext|>

High up above the open,
welcoming door It hangs, a piece
of wood with colours dim.
<|endoftext|>

Once, long ago, it was a waving
tree And knew the sun and shadow through the leaves Of
forest trees, in a thick eastern wood.
<|endoftext|>

Dark midnight storms had roared and crashed among Its
branches, breaking here and there a limb; But every now and then broad
sunlit days Lovingly lingered, caught among the leaves.
<|endoftext|>

An artist once, with patient,
careful knife, Had fashioned it like to
the untamed sea.
<|endoftext|>

Here waves uprear themselves, their tops blown
back By the gay, sunny wind, which whips the blue And
breaks it into gleams and sparks of light.
<|endoftext|>

Among the flashing waves are
two white birds Which swoop, and soar, and scream
for very joy At the wild sport.
<|endoftext|>

Now diving quickly
in, Questing some glistening
fish.
<|endoftext|>

Now flying up, Their dripping feathers shining
in the sun, While the wet drops like little glints of light, Fall
pattering backward to the parent sea.
<|endoftext|>

Gliding along the green and foam-flecked hollows, Or
skimming some white crest about to break, The spirits of the sky deigning
to stoop And play with ocean in a summer mood.
<|endoftext|>

When you, my Dear, are away,
away, How wearily goes
the creeping day.
<|endoftext|>

A year drags after morning,
and night Starts another year
of candle light.
<|endoftext|>

O Pausing
Sun and Lingering
Moon!
<|endoftext|>

Grant me, I
beg of you,
this boon.
<|endoftext|>

Whirl round the earth as never
sun Has his diurnal
journey run.
<|endoftext|>

And, Moon, slip past the ladders of air In a
single flash, while your streaming hair Catches the stars and pulls them down
To shine on some slumbering Chinese town.
<|endoftext|>

O
Kindly
Sun!
<|endoftext|>

Bring evening
to crowd the footsteps
of noon.
<|endoftext|>

But when that long
awaited day Hangs ripe in the
heavens, your voyaging stay.
<|endoftext|>

Be
morning,
O Sun!
<|endoftext|>

with the lark in
song, Be afternoon
for ages long.
<|endoftext|>

And, Moon, let you and
your lesser lights Watch over
a century of nights.

Here terrace sinks to terrace, arbors close The ends of
dreaming paths; a wanton wind Jostles the half-ripe pears, and then, unkind, Tumbles a-slumber
in a pillar rose, With content Grown indolent.
<|endoftext|>

By night my garden
is o'erhung with gems Fixed
in an onyx setting.
<|endoftext|>

Fireflies
Flicker their lanterns in
my dazzled eyes.

In serried rows I
guess the straight, stiff stems Of hollyhocks
Against the rocks.
<|endoftext|>

Who shall
declare the joy
of the running!
<|endoftext|>

Who shall tell
of the pleasures
of flight!
<|endoftext|>

Springing and spurning the
tufts of wild heather, Sweeping, wide-winged, through
the blue dome of light.
<|endoftext|>

Everything mortal has
moments immortal, Swift and God-gifted,
immeasurably bright.
<|endoftext|>

Joy in the
touch of the wind and
the sunlight!
<|endoftext|>

With the vigorous
earth I am
one.
<|endoftext|>

To be a flower Nodding in the sun, Bending,
then upspringing As the breezes run; Holding up A scent-brimmed cup, Full
of summer's fragrance to the summer sun.
<|endoftext|>

To be a butterfly Still,
upon a flower, Winking with its painted
wings, Happy in the hour.
<|endoftext|>

Blossoms hold Mines of
gold Deep within the farthest heart
of each chaliced flower.
<|endoftext|>

To be a cloud Blowing through the blue, Shadowing
the mountains, Rushing loudly through Valleys deep Where torrents keep Always
their plunging thunder and their misty arch of blue.
<|endoftext|>

To be a wave Splintering
on the sand, Drawing back, but leaving
Lingeringly the land.
<|endoftext|>

Rainbow light Flashes
bright Telling tales of coral caves
half hid in yellow sand.
<|endoftext|>

Soon they die, the flowers; Insects
live a day; Clouds dissolve in showers; Only
waves at play Last forever.
<|endoftext|>

Shall endeavor Make
a sea of purpose mightier
than we dream to-day?
<|endoftext|>

He whistled a little frivolous tune Which he felt
to be pulsing with ecstasy, For he thought that success always followed
desire, Such a very superlative fool was he.
<|endoftext|>

A maiden came by on an ambling
mule, Her gown was rose-red and her kerchief blue, On her lap
she carried a basket of eggs.
<|endoftext|>

Thought the fool,
"There is certainly
room for two.
<|endoftext|>

" So he jauntily swaggered
towards the maid And put out
his hand to the bridle-rein.
<|endoftext|>

"My pretty girl," quoth the
fool, "take me up, For to ride with you
to the town I am fain.
<|endoftext|>

" But the maiden struck at his
upraised arm And pelted him hotly
with eggs, a score.
<|endoftext|>

The mule, lashed into
a fury, ran; The fool went back
to his stone and swore.
<|endoftext|>

Then out of the cloud of settling
dust The burly form of an abbot appeared, Reading
his office he rode to the town.
<|endoftext|>

And the
fool got up, for
his heart was cheered.
<|endoftext|>

He stood in the midst
of the long, white road And swept off his
cap till it touched the ground.
<|endoftext|>

"Ah, Reverent Sir, well
met," said the fool, "A worthier transport
never was found.
<|endoftext|>

"I pray you allow me to
mount with you, Your palfrey seems
both sturdy and young.
<|endoftext|>

" The abbot looked up
from the holy book And cried out in
anger, "Hold your tongue!
<|endoftext|>

"How dare you obstruct the King's
highroad, You saucy varlet,
get out of my way.
<|endoftext|>

" Then he gave the fool a
cut with his whip And leaving him smarting,
he rode away.
<|endoftext|>

The fool was angry,
the fool was sore, And he cursed
the folly of monks and maids.
<|endoftext|>

"If I could but meet with a
man," sighed the fool, "For a woman fears,
and a friar upbraids.
<|endoftext|>

" Then he saw a flashing of distant steel And the
clanking of harness greeted his ears, And up the road journeyed
knights-at-arms, With waving plumes and glittering spears.
<|endoftext|>

The fool took notice
and slowly arose, Not quite
so sure was his foolish heart.
<|endoftext|>

If priests and women
would none of him Was it likely
a knight would take his part?
<|endoftext|>

They sang as they rode, these lusty boys, When
one chanced to turn toward the highway's side, "There's a sorry
figure of fun," jested he, "Well, Sirrah!
<|endoftext|>

move back, there
is scarce room
to ride.
<|endoftext|>

" Then the
company halted
and laughed out loud.
<|endoftext|>

"Was such a
request ever
made to a knight?
<|endoftext|>

" "And where are your legs," asked
one, "if you start, You may be inside
the town gates to-night.
<|endoftext|>

" "'T is a lazy fellow, let
him alone, They've no room in the town
for such idlers as he.
<|endoftext|>

" But one bent from his saddle
and said, "My man, Art thou not ashamed
to beg charity!
<|endoftext|>

Purple tree-shadows crept over the road, The level
sun flung an orange light, And the fool laid his head on the hard, gray
stone And wept as he realized advancing night.
<|endoftext|>

A great, round moon rose over a hill And the
steady wind blew yet more cool; And crouched on a stone a wayfarer sobbed,
For at last he knew he was only a fool.

The stars hang thick in the apple
tree, The south wind smells of the pungent sea, Gold
tulip cups are heavy with dew.

The night's
for you, Sweetheart,
for you!

rains from the vaulted

The dancing
of unseen

Only a

an ear, Though the tulips stand on tiptoe to hear, So
give; ripe fruit must shrivel or fall.

are mine, Sweetheart,

Starfire
sparkles, your
coronal.
<|endoftext|>

What
is
poetry?
<|endoftext|>

Is it a mosaic
Of coloured stones which curiously
are wrought Into a pattern?
<|endoftext|>

Softly the water ripples Against
the canoe's curving side, Softly the birch trees rustle
Flinging over us branches wide.
<|endoftext|>

From the shore come the scents of a garden,
And between a gap in the trees A proud white statue
glimmers In cold, disdainful ease.
<|endoftext|>

The child of a southern people, The thought
of an alien race, What does she in this pale, northern
garden, How reconcile it with her grace?
<|endoftext|>

But the moon in her wayward beauty Is
ever and always the same, As lovely as when upon
Latmos She watched till Endymion came.
<|endoftext|>

Through the water the moon writes her legends
In light, on the smooth, wet sand; They endure for a moment,
and vanish, And no one may understand.
<|endoftext|>

All round us the secret of Nature Is telling
itself to our sight, We may guess at her meaning but
never Can know the full mystery of night.
<|endoftext|>

But her power of enchantment is on us,
We bow to the spell which she weaves, Made up of the murmur
of waves And the manifold whisper of leaves.
<|endoftext|>

To me
it is
not so.
<|endoftext|>

To me alone it is a time of pause, A
void and silent space between two worlds, When inspiration lags, and feeling
sleeps, Gathering strength for efforts yet to come.
<|endoftext|>

For life alone is creator of life, And closest
contact with the human world Is like a lantern shining in the
night To light me to a knowledge of myself.
<|endoftext|>

As for a moment he stands, in hardy masculine beauty,
Poised on the fircrested rock, over the pool which below him Gleams in the
wavering sunlight, waiting the shock of his plunging.
<|endoftext|>

So for a moment I stand, my feet
planted firm in the present, Eagerly scanning the
future which is so soon to possess me.
<|endoftext|>

" Later the solitude vanished,
confused and distracted the road Where many
were seeking and jostling.
<|endoftext|>

Left behind were the trees and the flowers,
The half-realized beauty of quiet, the sacred
unconscious communing.
<|endoftext|>

And now he is come to a river, a
line of gray, sullen water, Not blue and splashing, but dark,
rolling somberly on to the ocean.

be strong to uphold him, and bear him

I need to hear it often

and finds it hard to trust.
<|endoftext|>

Ah, Dearest, you are good to love me so, And yet
I would not have it goodness, rather Excess of selfishness in
you to need Me through and through, as flowers need the sun.
<|endoftext|>

waiting for this time, Not only

Dear Heart, I love you,

<|endoftext|>

I'm glad that you are beautiful, although Were

Yes, yes, once more Kiss me, and

even as I want you.

Will those to come
Bring unguessed sorrows into

<|endoftext|>

What does

have had to-night!
<|endoftext|>

To-night will make us
strong, for we believe Each in the other,
this is a sacrament.
<|endoftext|>

I know a country laced with roads, They join the
hills and they span the brooks, They weave like a shuttle between broad
fields, And slide discreetly through hidden nooks.
<|endoftext|>

They are canopied like
a Persian dome And carpeted
with orient dyes.
<|endoftext|>

They are myriad-voiced,
and musical, And scented with
happiest memories.
<|endoftext|>

O Winding roads that
I know so well, Every twist and
turn, every hollow and hill!
<|endoftext|>

They are set in my
heart to a pulsing tune Gay as a
honey-bee humming in June.
<|endoftext|>

O Uphill
roads and roads
that dip down!
<|endoftext|>

You curl your sun-spattered length along, And your march
is beaten into a song By the softly ringing hoofs of a
horse And the panting breath of the dogs I love.
<|endoftext|>

The pageant of Autumn
follows its course And the blue sky of
Autumn laughs above.
<|endoftext|>

And the song and the country become as one, I see
it as music, I hear it as light; Prismatic and shimmering, trembling
to tone, The land of desire, my soul's delight.
<|endoftext|>

And always it beats in my listening
ears With the gentle thud of a horse's stride, With the swift-falling steps
of many dogs, Following, following at my side.
<|endoftext|>

O Roads
that journey to
fairyland!
<|endoftext|>

How
still it
is!
<|endoftext|>

What sound is
that which echoes
through the wood?
<|endoftext|>

Is it the
reedy note of an
oaten pipe?
<|endoftext|>

Perchance a minute more will see the brood
Of the shaggy forest god, and on his lip Will
rest the rushes he is wont to play.
<|endoftext|>

His train in woven baskets bear ripe fruit And weave a
dance with ropes of gray acorns, So light their touch the grasses scarcely
sway As they the measure tread to the lilting flute.
<|endoftext|>

't is only
Fancy thus
adorns.
<|endoftext|>

A cloud drifts

shining sun.
<|endoftext|>

How damp
it seems, how silent, still,
and strange!
<|endoftext|>

Surely 't was here some
tragedy was done, And here the
chorus sang each coming change?
<|endoftext|>

The tread of
sandalled feet comes

<|endoftext|>

The silence almost is a sound, and dreams Take on
the semblances of finite things; So potent is the spell that what but
seems Elsewhere, is lifted here on Fancy's wings.
<|endoftext|>

The little woodland theatre seems to wait, All
tremulous with hope and wistful joy, For something that is sure to
come at last, Some deep emotion, satisfying, great.
<|endoftext|>

It grows a living presence,
bold and shy, Cradling the future
in a glorious past.
<|endoftext|>

The octagon tower casts a shade Cool and
gray like a cutlass blade; In sun-baked vines the cicalas spin,
The little green lizards run out and in.

A sail dips over
the ocean's rim, And bubbles

<|endoftext|>

The minstrel beseeches his silver strings, And holding the lady

the bridge to Avignon, One morning in the spring.
<|endoftext|>

Clouds sail over the distant trees, Petals

The beating

Down the road to Avignon, The long, long

I hear the sigh The goaded

Below, straight streets, monotonous, From north and south,

<|endoftext|>

Joyless emblem of the greed Of millions, robber

Whose soothing presence

Beggars By day, our wealth is having night

Nature forsakes this man-begot And festering wilderness,

<|endoftext|>

On winter nights beside the
nursery fire We read the fairy
tale, while glowing coals Builded its pictures.
<|endoftext|>

The foliage seemed
to rustle in the wind, A silent
murmur, carved in still, gray stone.
<|endoftext|>

A sudden blare of trumpets, and
the throng About the entrance parted as the guests Filed
singly in with rare and precious gifts.
<|endoftext|>

Our eager fancies
noted all they brought, The glorious,
unattainable delights!
<|endoftext|>

But always there was one
unbidden guest Who cursed the child
and left it bitterness.
<|endoftext|>

The fire falls asunder, all is
changed, I am no more a child, and what I see
Is not a fairy tale, but life, my life.
<|endoftext|>

The gifts are there, the many pleasant things:
Health, wealth, long-settled friendships, with a name Which honors all who bear it, and
the power Of making words obedient.
<|endoftext|>

This is much; But overshadowing
all is still the curse, That never shall I
be fulfilled by love!
<|endoftext|>

Along the parching
highroad of the world No other
soul shall bear mine company.
<|endoftext|>

So I behold my visions on
the ground No longer radiant, an ignoble
heap Of broken, dusty glass.
<|endoftext|>

And so, unlit, Even by
hope or faith, my dragging steps Force me
forever through the passing days.
<|endoftext|>

You came to me bearing bright roses, Red
like the wine of your heart; You twisted them into a garland
To set me aside from the mart.
<|endoftext|>

Red roses to crown
me your lover, And I walked

<|endoftext|>

Enslaved and encircled,
I bore it, Proud token of

The petals waned paler,

<|endoftext|>

Bitter thorns to proclaim
me your lover, A diadem

<|endoftext|>

Dear Bessie, would my tired rhyme Had force
to rise from apathy, And shaking off its

<|endoftext|>

But in my soul's high belfry, chill The bitter
wind of doubt has blown, The summer swallows all have
flown, The bells are frost-bound, mute and still.
<|endoftext|>

Upon the crumbling boards the snow
Has drifted deep, the clappers hang Prismed with icicles,
their clang Unheard since ages long ago.
<|endoftext|>

The rope I pull is stiff and cold, My
straining ears detect no sound Except a sigh, as round and
round The wind rocks through the timbers old.
<|endoftext|>

Below, I know the church is bright With
haloed tapers, warm with prayer; But here I only

<|endoftext|>

Beneath my feet the snow is lit And
gemmed with colours, red, and blue, Topaz, and green, where light
falls through The saints that in the windows sit.
<|endoftext|>

Here darkness seems a spectred thing, Voiceless
and haunting, while the stars Mock with a light of long

<|endoftext|>

Silent and winter-killed I stand, No carol
hymns my debt to you; But take this frozen thought in lieu,
And thaw its music in your hand.
<|endoftext|>

Thou father of the children of my brain By thee
engendered in my willing heart, How can I thank thee for this gift of
art Poured out so lavishly, and not in vain.
<|endoftext|>

What thou created never more can die, Thy
fructifying power lives in me And I conceive, knowing it
is by thee, Dear other parent of my poetry!
<|endoftext|>

All unknown, By me unguessed, by thee
not even dreamed, A tree has blossomed in a night
that seemed Of stubborn, barren wood.
<|endoftext|>

For thou hast sown This
seed of beauty in
a ground of truth.
<|endoftext|>

Humbly I dedicate myself, and
yet I tremble with a sudden fear to set New
music ringing through my fading youth.
<|endoftext|>

His eyelids burnt and quivered with the
strain Of looking, and against his temples beat The all
enshrouding, suffocating dark.
<|endoftext|>

He stumbled, lurched, and struck against a door That opened,
and a howl of obscene mirth Grated his senses, wallowing on the
floor Lay men, and dogs and women in the dirt.
<|endoftext|>

He sickened, loathing
it, and as he gazed The candle guttered,
flared, and then went out.
<|endoftext|>

Then as he leaned Unconscious where,
the great oak door blew back And cast him,
bruised and dripping, in the church.
<|endoftext|>

His eyes from long sojourning in
the night Were blinded now as by some glorious sun;
He slowly crawled toward the altar steps.
<|endoftext|>

He could not think, for heavy in his
ears An organ boomed majestic harmonies; He
only knew that what he saw was light!
<|endoftext|>

He bowed himself before
a cross of flame And shut his eyes
in fear lest it should fade.

Such

A little garden on a

All denied

A little

<|endoftext|>

Thou yellow

<|endoftext|>

Thou herald of

<|endoftext|>

Always bold To stand erect,
full in the dazzling play Of April's

<|endoftext|>

'T is you that
are the music,
not your song.
<|endoftext|>

The song is but a door which, opening
wide, Lets forth the pent-up melody inside, Your spirit's
harmony, which clear and strong Sings but of you.
<|endoftext|>

Throughout your whole life long Your songs, your thoughts, your
doings, each divide This perfect beauty; waves within a tide,
Or single notes amid a glorious throng.
<|endoftext|>

The song of earth has many different
chords; Ocean has many moods and many
tones Yet always ocean.
<|endoftext|>

In the damp Spring woods The painted
trillium smiles, while crisp pine cones Autumn
alone can ripen.
<|endoftext|>

So is this One
music with a thousand
cadences.
<|endoftext|>

So still it stands Surely
our own if we exert
our might.
<|endoftext|>

Never can'st thou grasp this fleeting gleam, Its glowing
flame would die if it were caught, Its value is that it doth always
seem But just a little farther on.
<|endoftext|>

Distraught, But lighted ever
onward, we are brought Upon our way
unknowing, in a dream.
<|endoftext|>

Brave idolatry
Which can conceive
a hero!
<|endoftext|>

No deceit, No knowledge taught
by unrelenting years, Can quench this fierce,
untamable desire.
<|endoftext|>

We know that what we
long for once achieved Will cease
to satisfy.
<|endoftext|>

Be still our fears; If what
we worship fail us, still the fire
Burns on, and it is much to have believed.
<|endoftext|>

Must all of worth be travailled
for, and those Life's brightest stars rise from
a troubled sea?
<|endoftext|>

Must years go by in sad uncertainty
Leaving us doubting whose the conquering
blows, Are we or Fate the victors?
<|endoftext|>

Time which shows All inner
meanings will reveal, but we Shall
never know the upshot.
<|endoftext|>

Sudden we lack The flash
of insight, life grows drear and gray, And hour
follows hour, nerveless, slack.
<|endoftext|>

Austere arbiter of each man's fate, By
whom he learns that Nature's steadfast laws Are as decrees immutable;
O pause Your even forward march!
<|endoftext|>

Not yet too late Teach me the needed
lesson, when to wait Inactive as a ship when no
wind draws To stretch the loosened cordage.
<|endoftext|>

What instinct forces
man to journey on, Urged by a longing
blind but dominant!
<|endoftext|>

Nothing he sees can
hold him, nothing daunt His never
failing eagerness.
<|endoftext|>

The wind is singing through the
trees to-night, A deep-voiced song of rushing
cadences And crashing intervals.
<|endoftext|>

Above the tossing trees shines down
a star, Quietly bright; this wild, tumultuous
joy Quickens nor dims its splendour.
<|endoftext|>

And
my mind, O
Star!
<|endoftext|>

is filled with your white light, from far,
So suffer me this one night to enjoy The freedom
of the onward sweeping wind.

The path runs straight between the flowering rows, A

<|endoftext|>

which throws Into the night these wafts of rich perfume
Which sweep across the garden like a plume.

Over

bright star glows.
<|endoftext|>

How is it that, being gone,
you fill my days, And all the long
nights are made glad by thee?
<|endoftext|>

No loneliness is this, nor misery, But great content
that these should be the ways Whereby the Fancy, dreaming as she strays,
Makes bright and present what she would would be.

And who shall say if
the reality Is not
with dreams so pregnant.

" It may
be vain
illusion.
<|endoftext|>

Alas, how few There are who strike in
us a chord we knew Existed, but so seldom
heard its tone We tremble at the half-forgotten sound.
<|endoftext|>

The world is full of rude awakenings And heaven-born
castles shattered to the ground, Yet still our human longing vainly
clings To a belief in beauty through all wrongs.
<|endoftext|>

O stay
your hand, and leave
my heart its songs!
<|endoftext|>

What torture lurks within a single thought When
grown too constant, and however kind, However welcome
still, the weary mind Aches with its presence.
<|endoftext|>

Dull remembrance taught Remembers on unceasingly; unsought
The old delight is with us but to find That all recurring joy is
pain refined, Become a habit, and we struggle, caught.
<|endoftext|>

You lie upon my heart as on a nest, Folded
in peace, for you can never know How crushed I am with
having you at rest Heavy upon my life.
<|endoftext|>

I love you so
You bind my freedom from
its rightful quest.
<|endoftext|>

In mercy
lift your drooping wings
and go.
<|endoftext|>

I do not care to talk to you although Your
speech evokes a thousand sympathies, And all my being's silent
harmonies Wake trembling into music.
<|endoftext|>

When you go It is as if some
sudden, dreadful blow Had severed all
the strings with savage ease.
<|endoftext|>

No, do not talk; but let
us rather seize This intimate
gift of silence which we know.
<|endoftext|>

Others may guess your thoughts
from what you say, As storms are guessed
from clouds where darkness broods.
<|endoftext|>

To me the very essence of the day Reveals
its inner purpose and its moods; As poplars feel the rain and then straightway
Reverse their leaves and shimmer through the woods.
<|endoftext|>

Now you
are
come!
<|endoftext|>

You tremble like a
star Poised where, behind earth's
rim, the sun has set.
<|endoftext|>

Your voice has sung across
my heart, but numb And mute, I have
no tones to answer.
<|endoftext|>

Far Within I kneel before
you, speechless yet, And life ablaze with
beauty, I am dumb.
<|endoftext|>

How empty
seems the town now
you are gone!
<|endoftext|>

A wilderness of sad streets, where gaunt walls Hide nothing
to desire; sunshine falls Eery, distorted, as it long
had shone On white, dead faces tombed in halls of stone.
<|endoftext|>

The whir of motors, stricken through with
calls Of playing boys, floats up at intervals; But all
these noises blur to one long moan.
<|endoftext|>

What quest
is worth
pursuing?
<|endoftext|>

And how strange
That other men still go
accustomed ways!
<|endoftext|>

I hate their interest
in the things they
do.
<|endoftext|>

A spectre-horde
repeating without change
An old routine.
<|endoftext|>

Alone I know
the days Are still-born, and the
world stopped, lacking you.
<|endoftext|>

All night I wrestled with
a memory Which knocked insurgent
at the gates of thought.
<|endoftext|>

The crumbled wreck of years behind has wrought Its
disillusion; now I only cry For peace, for power to
forget the lie Which hope too long has whispered.
<|endoftext|>

So I sought The sleep which
would not come, and night was fraught With old
emotions weeping silently.
<|endoftext|>

I heard your voice again,
and knew the things Which you had promised
proved an empty vaunt.
<|endoftext|>

I felt your clinging
hands while night's broad wings Cherished
our love in darkness.

From the lawn A
sudden, quivering
birdnote, like a taunt.
<|endoftext|>

My arms
held nothing but
the empty dawn.
<|endoftext|>

To make a pavement for your feet
I stripped My soul for you to walk upon, and slipped
Beneath your steps to soften all your ways.

But now my letters
are like blossoms pale We strew upon
a grave with hopeless tears.
<|endoftext|>

I ask no recompense, I shall not fail Although
you do not heed; the long, sad years Still pass, and still I scatter flowers
frail, And whisper words of love which no one hears.
<|endoftext|>

Throughout the echoing chambers of my
brain I hear your words in mournful cadence toll Like some slow
passing-bell which warns the soul Of sundering darkness.
<|endoftext|>

Unrelenting, fain To batter down resistance, fall
again Stroke after stroke, insistent diastole, The bitter blows of truth,
until the whole Is hammered into fact made strangely plain.

The little gallery winds round about
The middle of a most secluded room, Midway between
the ceiling and the floor.
<|endoftext|>

For books are more than books, they are the life, The very
heart and core of ages past, The reason why men lived, and worked, and
died, The essence and quintessence of their lives.
<|endoftext|>

Silently waiting that from time to time It may be
given them to illuminate Dull daily facts with pristine radiance For
some long-waited-for affinity Who lingers yet in the deep womb of time.
<|endoftext|>

The shifting sun pierces the young green leaves Of
elm trees, newly coming into bud, And splashes on the floor and on
the books Through old, high, rounded windows, dim with age.
<|endoftext|>

The noisy city-sounds of modern
life Float softened to us across
the old graveyard.
<|endoftext|>

The room is filled with a warm, mellow
light, No garish colours jar on our content,
The books upon the shelves are old and worn.
<|endoftext|>

'T was no belated effort nor attempt To
keep abreast with old as well as new That placed them here, tricked in a
modern guise, Easily got, and held in light esteem.
<|endoftext|>

A nothing truly, yet a link that binds All
ages to their own inheritance, And stretching backward, dim and
dimmer still, Is lost in a remote antiquity.
<|endoftext|>

Grapes do not come of thorns nor figs of
thistles, And even a great poet's divinest thought
Is coloured by the world he knows and sees.
<|endoftext|>

Nay, the little things Of daily
life it is which mold, and shape, And make him apt
for noble deeds and true.
<|endoftext|>

The long, still, fruitful hours slip away Shedding
their influences as they pass; We know ourselves the richer
to have sat Upon this dusty floor and dreamed our dreams.
<|endoftext|>

No other place to us
were quite the same, No other dreams so potent
in their charm, For this is ours!

forth a thought Peculiarly
inherent to the race.

And we, descendants of that elder

has been embodied to our years.

Near where I live there is a
lake As blue as blue can be, winds make
It dance as they go blowing by.

I think
it curtseys to

It's just a lake of lovely flowers And
my Mamma says they are ours; But they are not like

<|endoftext|>

We have a splendid garden, there Are lots
of flowers everywhere; Roses, and pinks, and four o'clocks
And hollyhocks, and evening stocks.

Mamma lets us
pick them, but never Must we
pick any gentians -- ever!
<|endoftext|>

For if we carried
them away They'd die of
homesickness that day.
<|endoftext|>

My Grandpapa lives in a wonderful house
With a great many windows and doors, There are stairs that go up, and
stairs that go down, And such beautiful, slippery floors.
<|endoftext|>

But of all of the rooms, even mother's and mine,
And the bookroom, and parlour and all, I like the green dining-room so
much the best Because of its ceiling and wall.
<|endoftext|>

Right over your head is a funny round hole With
apples and pears falling through; There's a big bunch of grapes all purply
and sweet, And melons and pineapples too.
<|endoftext|>

They tumble and tumble, but never come down Though I've
stood underneath a long while With my mouth open wide, for I always
have hoped Just a cherry would drop from the pile.
<|endoftext|>

No matter how early I run there to look It
has always begun to fall through; And one night when at bedtime I
crept in to see, It was falling by candle-light too.
<|endoftext|>

I am sure they are magical fruits, and each
one Makes you hear things, or see things, or go Forever invisible;
but it's no use, And of course I shall just never know.
<|endoftext|>

For the ladder's too heavy
to lift, and the chairs Are not nearly
so tall as I need.
<|endoftext|>

I've given up hope, and
I feel I shall die Without having
accomplished the deed.
<|endoftext|>

It's a little bit sad, when you seem very near
To adventures and things of that sort, Which nearly begin, and then
don't; and you know It is only because you are short.

He shouts in the sails of the ships at sea, He

Laughing, dancing, sunny wind, Whistling,
howling, rainy wind, North, South, East and West, Each is
the wind I like the best.
<|endoftext|>

He calls up the fog and hides the hills, He whirls

<|endoftext|>

The pine trees toss him their cones with glee, The
flowers bend low in courtesy, Each wave flings up a shower

To be an orphan,
To be fated to
be an orphan.

How

<|endoftext|>

When my father and mother
were alive I used to ride in a carriage
With four fine horses.
<|endoftext|>

But when they both died,
My brother and sister-in-law Sent me out
to be a merchant.
<|endoftext|>

In the south I travelled
to the “Nine Rivers” And in the east as

<|endoftext|>

My brother
told me to get
ready the dinner.
<|endoftext|>

My sister-in-law
told me to see after

<|endoftext|>

I was always going up

<|endoftext|>

My

<|endoftext|>

In the morning they
sent me to draw water, I
didn’t get back till night-fall.
<|endoftext|>

My hands
were all sore And
I had no shoes.
<|endoftext|>

and brambles.
<|endoftext|>

As I stopped to

bitter my heart was!

My tears fell and

<|endoftext|>

In winter I

It is

<|endoftext|>

The April winds blow
And the grass is

<|endoftext|>

In the third month--silkworms

I went out with the melon-cart
And just as I was coming home The

The people who came to help me were few, But

My brother and sister-in-law

Why does

She had been ill for years

She couldn’t say what she

a hurried traveller.
<|endoftext|>

A cup of wine together
will make us glad, And a little
friendship is no little matter.
<|endoftext|>

Yoking my
chariot I urge my stubborn
horses.
<|endoftext|>

I wander
about in the streets
of Wan and Lo.
<|endoftext|>

In Lo Town
how fine
everything is!
<|endoftext|>

The “Caps and
Belts” go seeking
each other out.
<|endoftext|>

The great boulevards are
intersected by lanes, Wherein
are the town-houses of Royal Dukes.
<|endoftext|>

The two palaces stare
at each other from afar, The
twin gates rise a hundred feet.
<|endoftext|>

By prolonging the feast
let us keep our hearts gay, And leave
no room for sadness to creep in.
<|endoftext|>

Of this day’s glorious
feast and revel The pleasure and delight
are difficult to describe.
<|endoftext|>

Plucking the lute they sent
forth lingering sounds, The new melodies
in beauty reached the divine.
<|endoftext|>

Skilful singers intoned
the high words, Those who knew the tune heard
the trueness of their singing.
<|endoftext|>

We sat there each with the same desire And
like thoughts by each unexpressed: “Man in the world lodging for a single life-time
Passes suddenly like dust borne on the wind.
<|endoftext|>

Then let us hurry out with high steps And be
the first to reach the highways and fords: Rather than stay at home
wretched and poor For long years plunged in sordid grief.
<|endoftext|>

” In the north-west there is
a high house, Its top level
with the floating clouds.
<|endoftext|>

Embroidered curtains thinly
screen its windows, Its storied tower
is built on three steps.
<|endoftext|>

From above there comes
a noise of playing and singing,
The tune sounding, oh!
<|endoftext|>

Who can it
be, playing so sad
a tune?
<|endoftext|>

Surely it
must be Ch’i Liang’s
wife.
<|endoftext|>

The tranquil “D” follows
the wind’s rising, The middle
lay lingers indecisive.
<|endoftext|>

To each note, two or
three sobs, Her high will conquered by
overwhelming grief.
<|endoftext|>

She does not regret that she
is left so sad, But minds that so few
can understand her song.
<|endoftext|>

She wants to become
those two wild geese That with beating
wings rise high aloft.
<|endoftext|>

Crossing the river I
pluck hibiscus-flowers: In the orchid-swamps
are many fragrant herbs.
<|endoftext|>

I gather
them, but who shall
I send them to?
<|endoftext|>

My love
is living in lands
far away.
<|endoftext|>

I turn and look towards
my own country: The long road
stretches on for ever.
<|endoftext|>

The same heart, yet a different
dwelling: Always fretting, till
we are grown old!
<|endoftext|>

A bright moon illumines
the night-prospect: The house-cricket chirrups on
the eastern wall.
<|endoftext|>

The Handle of the
Pole-star points to the Beginning
of Winter.
<|endoftext|>

The host of stars
is scattered over
the sky.
<|endoftext|>

The white dew wets the
moor-grasses,-- With sudden swiftness the
times and seasons change.
<|endoftext|>

The autumn cicada
sings among the trees, The swallows,
alas, whither are they gone?
<|endoftext|>

Once I had a
same-house friend, He took flight and
rose high away.
<|endoftext|>

He did not remember how
once we went hand in hand, But left me like
footsteps behind one in the dust.
<|endoftext|>

In the South is the Winnowing-fan
and the Pole-star in the North, And a Herd-boy whose ox
has never borne the yoke.
<|endoftext|>

A friend who is not firm as
a great rock Is of no profit
and idly bears the name.
<|endoftext|>

In the courtyard there grows
a strange tree, Its green leaves ooze
with a fragrant moisture.
<|endoftext|>

Holding the branch I cut a
flower from the tree, Meaning to send it away
to the person I love.
<|endoftext|>

Its sweet
smell fills my sleeves
and lap.
<|endoftext|>

The road is
long, how shall I get
it there?
<|endoftext|>

Such a thing is not fine enough
to send: But it may remind him of the time
that has past since he left.
<|endoftext|>

Far away twinkles the
Herd-boy star; Brightly shines the Lady
of the Han River.
<|endoftext|>

Slender, slender
she plies her white
fingers.
<|endoftext|>

Click, click go
the wheels of her
spinning-loom.
<|endoftext|>

At the end of the day
she has not finished her task; Her bitter
tears fall like streaming rain.
<|endoftext|>

The Han River runs
shallow and clear; Set between
them, how short a space!
<|endoftext|>

But the river water will not
let them pass, Gazing at each other
but never able to speak.
<|endoftext|>

Turning my
chariot I yoke my
horses and go.
<|endoftext|>

On and on down the
long roads The autumn winds shake
the hundred grasses.
<|endoftext|>

On every
side, how desolate
and bare!
<|endoftext|>

The things I meet are
all new things, Their strangeness hastens
the coming of old age.
<|endoftext|>

Prosperity
and decay each have their
season.
<|endoftext|>

Success is bitter
when it is slow
in coming.
<|endoftext|>

Man’s life is not metal
or stone, He cannot far prolong
the days of his fate.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly
he follows in the
way of things that change.
<|endoftext|>

Fame is the
only treasure that
endures.
<|endoftext|>

The Eastern Castle
stands tall and high; Far and wide
stretch the towers that guard it.
<|endoftext|>

The whirling wind uprises
and shakes the earth; The autumn grasses
grow thick and green.
<|endoftext|>

The four seasons alternate
without pause, The year’s end hurries
swiftly on.
<|endoftext|>

The Bird of the Morning Wind
is stricken with sorrow; The frail
cicada suffers and is hard pressed.
<|endoftext|>

Free and clear, let
us loosen the
bonds of our hearts.
<|endoftext|>

Why should we
go on always
restraining and binding?
<|endoftext|>

In Yen and Chao are
many fair ladies, Beautiful people
with faces like jade.
<|endoftext|>

Their clothes
are made all of
silk gauze.
<|endoftext|>

They stand at
the door practising
tranquil lays.
<|endoftext|>

The echo
of their singing,
how sad it sounds!
<|endoftext|>

By the pitch of
the song one knows the
stops have been tightened.
<|endoftext|>

To ease their minds they arrange
their shawls and belts; Lowering their song, a
little while they pause.
<|endoftext|>

“I should like to be those two flying
swallows Who are carrying clay to
nest in the eaves of your house.
<|endoftext|>

” I drive my chariot up to
the Eastern Gate; From afar I see
the graveyard north of the Wall.
<|endoftext|>

The white aspens how they
murmur, murmur; Pines and cypresses
flank the broad paths.
<|endoftext|>

Beneath lie men who
died long ago; Black, black is
the long night that holds them.
<|endoftext|>

Deep down beneath the
Yellow Springs, Thousands of
years they lie without waking.
<|endoftext|>

In infinite succession
light and darkness shift, And years vanish
like the morning dew.
<|endoftext|>

Man’s life is like a sojourning,
His longevity lacks the firmness
of stone and metal.
<|endoftext|>

For ever it has been
that mourners in their turn were mourned, Saint
and Sage,--all alike are trapped.
<|endoftext|>

Seeking by food to obtain
Immortality Many have
been the dupe of strange drugs.
<|endoftext|>

Better far to drink
good wine And clothe our bodies
in robes of satin and silk.
<|endoftext|>

The dead are gone and
with them we cannot
converse.
<|endoftext|>

The living are
here and ought to have
our love.
<|endoftext|>

Leaving the city-gate I look
ahead And see before me
only mounds and tombs.
<|endoftext|>

The old graves are ploughed
up into fields, The pines and cypresses
are hewn for timber.
<|endoftext|>

In the white aspens sad
winds sing; Their long murmuring
kills my heart with grief.
<|endoftext|>

I want to go
home, to ride to my
village gate.
<|endoftext|>

I want to go
back, but there’s no
road back.
<|endoftext|>

The years of a
lifetime do not
reach a hundred.
<|endoftext|>

Yet they contain
a thousand years’
sorrow.
<|endoftext|>

When days are short and
the dull nights long, Why not take a
lamp and wander forth?
<|endoftext|>

If you want to be happy
you must do it now, There is no
waiting till an after-time.
<|endoftext|>

The fool who’s loath to
spend the wealth he’s got Becomes the laughing-stock
of after ages.
<|endoftext|>

It is true that Master
Wang became immortal, But how can
_we_ hope to share his lot?
<|endoftext|>

Cold, cold the year draws to its
end, The crickets and grasshoppers
make a doleful chirping.
<|endoftext|>

The chill wind
increases its
violence.
<|endoftext|>

My wandering
love has no coat to
cover him.
<|endoftext|>

He gave his embroidered furs
to the Lady of Lo, But from me his
bedfellow he is quite estranged.
<|endoftext|>

Sleeping alone in the depth
of the long night In a dream I thought
I saw the light of his face.
<|endoftext|>

My dear one thought of our
old joys together, He came in his
chariot and gave me the front reins.
<|endoftext|>

I wanted so to prolong
our play and laughter, To hold his
hand and go back with him in his coach.
<|endoftext|>

But, when he had come he
would not stay long Nor stop to go with me
to the Inner Chamber.
<|endoftext|>

Truly without the falcon’s
wings to carry me How can I rival
the flying wind’s swiftness?
<|endoftext|>

I go and lean at the
gate and think of my grief, My falling
tears wet the double gates.
<|endoftext|>

At the beginning of
winter a cold spirit comes,
The North Wind blows--chill, chill.
<|endoftext|>

My sorrows being many, I
know the length of the nights, Raising my head I
look at the stars in their places.
<|endoftext|>

On the fifteenth day the bright
moon is full, On the twentieth
day the “toad and hare” wane.
<|endoftext|>

” I put the letter away
in the folds of my dress, For three years the
writing did not fade.
<|endoftext|>

How with an undivided
heart I loved you I fear that you
will never know or guess.
<|endoftext|>

The bright moon, oh, how white
it shines, Shines down on the gauze
curtains of my bed.
<|endoftext|>

Racked by sorrow
I toss and cannot
sleep.
<|endoftext|>

Picking up
my clothes, I wander
up and down.
<|endoftext|>

My absent love says that he
is happy, But I would rather he
said he was coming back.
<|endoftext|>

Out in the courtyard
I stand hesitating,
alone.
<|endoftext|>

To whom
can I tell the sad
thoughts I think?
<|endoftext|>

Staring before me I
enter my room again; Falling
tears wet my mantle and robe.
<|endoftext|>

Autumn wind
rises: white
clouds fly.
<|endoftext|>

Grass and
trees wither:
geese go south.
<|endoftext|>

Orchids all
in bloom: chrysanthemums
smell sweet.
<|endoftext|>

I think of my
lovely lady: I never
can forget.
<|endoftext|>

Across the mid-stream white waves rise Flute and
drum keep time to sound of the rowers’ song; Amidst revel
and feasting, sad thoughts come; Youth’s years how few!
<|endoftext|>

The sound
of her silk
skirt has stopped.
<|endoftext|>

On the marble
pavement dust
grows.
<|endoftext|>

Her empty
room is cold
and still.
<|endoftext|>

Fallen leaves are
piled against
the doors.
<|endoftext|>

Longing for that lovely
lady How can I bring my
aching heart to rest?
<|endoftext|>

Is it
or isn’t
it?
<|endoftext|>

I
stand
and look.
<|endoftext|>

The swish,
swish of
a silk skirt.
<|endoftext|>

How slow
she
comes!
<|endoftext|>

Our love was pure As the snow on the mountains:
White as a moon Between the clouds-- They’re telling me Your thoughts
are double That’s why I’ve come To break it off.
<|endoftext|>

To-day we’ll
drink A cup
of wine.
<|endoftext|>

To-morrow we’ll part Beside the Canal:
Walking about Beside the Canal, Where its
branches divide East and west.
<|endoftext|>

Alas
and alas, And
again alas.
<|endoftext|>

So must a girl Cry when she’s married,
If she find not a man Of single heart, Who will
not leave her Till her hair is white.
<|endoftext|>

Since our hair was plaited and
we became man and wife The love between
us was never broken by doubt.
<|endoftext|>

So let us be merry
this night together, Feasting and
playing while the good time lasts.
<|endoftext|>

I suddenly remember the
distance that I must travel; I spring from
bed and look out to see the time.
<|endoftext|>

The stars and planets are all
grown dim in the sky; Long, long is
the road; I cannot stay.
<|endoftext|>

I am going on
service, away to the battle-ground, And I
do not know when I shall come back.
<|endoftext|>

I hold your hand with only
a deep sigh; Afterwards, tears--in the days
when we are parted.
<|endoftext|>

With all your might enjoy the
spring flowers, But do not forget
the time of our love and pride.
<|endoftext|>

Know that if I live, I will come
back again, And if I die, we will go
on thinking of each other.
<|endoftext|>

The good time will never
come back again: In a moment,--our parting
will be over.
<|endoftext|>

Anxiously--we halt at the
road-side, Hesitating--we embrace
where the fields begin.
<|endoftext|>

The clouds above are floating
across the sky: Swiftly, swiftly passing:
or blending together.
<|endoftext|>

The waves in the wind lose
their fixed place And are rolled away each
to a corner of Heaven.
<|endoftext|>

From now onwards--long
must be our
parting.
<|endoftext|>

So let us
stop again for
a little while.
<|endoftext|>

I wish I could ride on
the wings of the morning wind And go with
you right to your journey’s end.
<|endoftext|>

I came ten thousand leagues Across
sandy deserts In the service of
my Prince, To break the Hun tribes.
<|endoftext|>

My way was
blocked and barred, My arrows
and sword broken.
<|endoftext|>

My armies had
faded away, My
reputation had gone.
<|endoftext|>

My old
mother is
long dead.
<|endoftext|>

Although I want
to requite my Prince How
can I return?
<|endoftext|>

My people have married me In a
far corner of Earth: Sent me away to a
strange land, To the king of the Wu-sun.
<|endoftext|>

A tent is my house,
Of felt are my walls; Raw flesh my
food With mare’s milk to drink.
<|endoftext|>

Always thinking
of my own country, My
heart sad within.
<|endoftext|>

Would I were a
yellow stork And could
fly to my old home!
<|endoftext|>

Solemn, solemn the
coachman gets ready to go:
“Chiang, chiang” the harness bells ring.
<|endoftext|>

At break of dawn I
must start on my long journey: At cock-crow
I must gird on my belt.
<|endoftext|>

I turn back and look at
the empty room: For a moment I
almost think I see you there.
<|endoftext|>

One parting, but ten thousand
regrets: As I take my seat, my
heart is unquiet.
<|endoftext|>

What shall
I do to tell
you all my thoughts?
<|endoftext|>

How can
I let you know of all
my love?
<|endoftext|>

Precious hairpins make
the head to shine And bright mirrors
can reflect beauty.
<|endoftext|>

Fragrant herbs banish
evil smells And the scholar’s
harp has a clear note.
<|endoftext|>

The man in the Book of Odes who
was given a quince Wanted to pay it
back with diamonds and rubies.
<|endoftext|>

When I think of all the things you
have done for me, How ashamed I am
to have done so little for you!
<|endoftext|>

Although I know that it is
a poor return, All I can give you is this
description of my feelings.
<|endoftext|>

My poor body is
alas unworthy: I was ill when
first you brought me home.
<|endoftext|>

Limp and weary
in the house-- Time passed and
I got no better.
<|endoftext|>

We could hardly ever
see each other: I could not
serve you as I ought.
<|endoftext|>

Then you received the Imperial
Mandate: You were ordered to go far
away to the City.
<|endoftext|>

Long, long must be our
parting: I was not destined
to tell you my thoughts.
<|endoftext|>

I stood on tiptoe gazing
into the distance, Interminably gazing
at the road that had taken you.
<|endoftext|>

With thoughts of you my mind is
obsessed: In my dreams I see
the light of your face.
<|endoftext|>

Now you are started on
your long journey Each day brings
you further from me.
<|endoftext|>

Oh that I had
a bird’s wings And high flying
could follow you.
<|endoftext|>

Long I sob and
long I cry: The tears fall
down and wet my skirt.
<|endoftext|>

On the Eastern Way at the city of
Lo-yang At the edge of the road peach-trees and plum-trees grow; On the two sides,--flower matched
by flower; Across the road,--leaf touching leaf.
<|endoftext|>

A spring wind rises
from the north-east; Flowers and leaves
gently nod and sway.
<|endoftext|>

Up the road somebody’s
daughter comes Carrying a basket,
to gather silkworms’ food.
<|endoftext|>

(_She sees the fruit trees in blossom
and, forgetting about her silkworms, begins
to pluck the branches.
<|endoftext|>

_) With her slender hand she
breaks a branch from the tree; The flowers
fall, tossed and scattered in the wind.
<|endoftext|>

“Lovely lady, I never
did you harm; Why should you hate
me and do me injury?
<|endoftext|>

Though in the autumn your
leaves patter to the ground, When spring
comes, your gay bloom returns.
<|endoftext|>

But in men’s lives when their
bright youth is spent Joy and love never
come back again.
<|endoftext|>

” When I was young, throughout
the hot season There were no carriages
driving about the roads.
<|endoftext|>

People shut their doors and
lay down in the cool: Or if they went
out, it was not to pay calls.
<|endoftext|>

Nowadays--ill-bred, ignorant
fellows, When they feel the heat,
make for a friend’s house.
<|endoftext|>

The unfortunate host,
when he hears someone coming Scowls and frowns,
but can think of no escape.
<|endoftext|>
