“There’s nothing for it but to rise
and go to the door,” And in his comfortable
seat he groans and sighs.
<|endoftext|>

The conversation
does not end quickly: Prattling and babbling,
what a lot he says!
<|endoftext|>

Only when one is almost
dead with fatigue He asks at last if
one isn’t finding him tiring.
<|endoftext|>

(One’s arm is almost in half
with continual fanning: The sweat is pouring
down one’s neck in streams.
<|endoftext|>

) Do not say that this is a
small matter: I consider the practice
a blot on our social life.
<|endoftext|>

I therefore caution
all wise men That August visitors
should not be admitted.
<|endoftext|>

I look up and see / his
curtains and bed: I look down and examine
/ his table and mat.
<|endoftext|>

The things
are there / just as
before.
<|endoftext|>

But the man
they belonged to / is
not there.
<|endoftext|>

His spirit suddenly
/ has taken flight And left me behind
/ far away.
<|endoftext|>

To whom shall
I look / on
whom rely?
<|endoftext|>

My tears flow
/ in an endless
stream.
<|endoftext|>

“Yu, yu” / cry the wandering
deer As they carry fodder
/ to their young in the wood.
<|endoftext|>

Flap, flap / fly the birds
As they carry their little
ones / back to the nest.
<|endoftext|>

I alone / am desolate
Dreading the days / of our long parting: My grieving
heart’s / settled pain No one else / can understand.
<|endoftext|>

There is a saying
/ among people “Sorrow makes
us / grow old.
<|endoftext|>

” Alas,
alas / for
my white hairs!
<|endoftext|>

All too
early
/ they have come!
<|endoftext|>

Long wailing, / long
sighing My thoughts are fixed on
my sage parent.
<|endoftext|>

They say the
good / live long: Then why
was he / not spared?
<|endoftext|>

My charioteer hastens
to yoke my carriage, For I must go
on a journey far away.
<|endoftext|>

“Where are you going
on your journey
far away?
<|endoftext|>

” To the land
of Wu where my
enemies are.
<|endoftext|>

But I must ride many
thousand miles, Beyond the
Eastern Road that leads to Wu.
<|endoftext|>

I want to take a skiff
and cross these rivers, But alas for
me, where shall I find a boat?
<|endoftext|>

To sit idle is not my
desire: Gladly enough would I
go to my country’s aid.
<|endoftext|>

In the North-west there is
a floating cloud Stretched on high, like
a chariot’s canvas-awning.
<|endoftext|>

Alas that I was born in
these times, To be blown along like
a cloud puffed by the wind!
<|endoftext|>

It has blown me away
far to the South-east, On and on
till I came to Wu-hui.
<|endoftext|>

Wu-hui is not my country:
Why should I go on staying
and staying here?
<|endoftext|>

I will give it up and never
speak of it again,-- This being abroad
and always living in dread.
<|endoftext|>

I climb to the ridge of Pei
Mang Mountain And look down on
the city of Lo-yang.
<|endoftext|>

In Lo-yang
how still it
is!
<|endoftext|>

Palaces and
houses all burnt
to ashes.
<|endoftext|>

Walls and fences all
broken and gaping, Thorns and brambles
shooting up to the sky.
<|endoftext|>

I do not see the
old old-men: I only see
the new young men.
<|endoftext|>

I turn aside, for the straight
road is lost: The fields are overgrown and
will never be ploughed again.
<|endoftext|>

I have been away such
a long time That I do not
know which street is which.
<|endoftext|>

How sad and
ugly the empty
moors are!
<|endoftext|>

A thousand miles
without the smoke of a
chimney.
<|endoftext|>

I think of the house
I lived in all those years: I am
heart-tied and cannot speak.
<|endoftext|>

Smoke is on the plaster,
Scarred the shower-burghs, Shorn and shattered,
By eld under-eaten.
<|endoftext|>

Earth’s grip
haveth Wealders and
workmen.
<|endoftext|>

” Our wandering
eyes are sated with
the dancer’s skill.
<|endoftext|>

Our ears are
weary with the
sound of “kung” and “shang.
<|endoftext|>

” Our host is silent
and sits doing nothing: All the guests go
on to places of amusement.
<|endoftext|>

On long benches the
sportsmen sit ranged Round a cleared room,
watching the fighting-cocks.
<|endoftext|>

The gallant birds are
all in battle-trim: They raise their tails and
flap defiantly.
<|endoftext|>

Their beating wings stir
the calm air: Their angry eyes
gleam with a red light.
<|endoftext|>

Where their beaks have struck, the fine feathers
are scattered: With their strong talons they
wound again and again.
<|endoftext|>

Their long cries enter the
blue clouds; Their flapping wings
tirelessly beat and throb.
<|endoftext|>

“Pray God the lamp-oil lasts a little
longer, Then I shall not leave without
winning the match!
<|endoftext|>

” In the Nine Provinces there is not room enough: I
want to soar high among the clouds, And, far beyond the Eight Limits of the
compass, Cast my gaze across the unmeasured void.
<|endoftext|>

The doors of Heaven
swing open, The double gates
shine with a red light.
<|endoftext|>

The Lord God lies at his western
lattice: And the lesser Spirits are together
in the eastern gallery.
<|endoftext|>

They wash me in a bath
of rainbow-spray And gird me with a
belt of jasper and rubies.
<|endoftext|>

I wander at my ease
gathering divine herbs: I bend down
and touch the scented flowers.
<|endoftext|>

Wang-tzŭ gives me drugs
of long-life And Hsien-mēn
hands me strange potions.
<|endoftext|>

By the partaking of food I evade
the rites of Death: My span is extended to the
enjoyment of life everlasting.
<|endoftext|>

Flap, flap, you curtain
in front of our
bed!
<|endoftext|>

I hung you there
to screen us from
the light of day.
<|endoftext|>

I brought you with me when I
left my father’s house; Now I am
taking you back with me again.
<|endoftext|>

I will fold
you up and lay you
flat in your box.
<|endoftext|>

Curtain--shall I
ever take you out
again?
<|endoftext|>

When I was young I
learnt fencing And was better at
it than Crooked Castle.
<|endoftext|>

My spirit was high
as the rolling clouds And my fame
resounded beyond the World.
<|endoftext|>

I took my sword to
the desert sands, I drank my
horse at the Nine Moors.
<|endoftext|>

My flags and banners
flapped in the wind, And nothing was heard
but the song of my drums.
<|endoftext|>

War and its travels have made me sad, And a
fierce anger burns within me: It’s thinking of how I’ve wasted
my time That makes this fury tear my heart.
<|endoftext|>

I will cast
out Wisdom and
reject Learning.
<|endoftext|>

My thoughts
shall wander in
the Great Void (_bis_).
<|endoftext|>

Always repenting
of wrongs done Will never bring
my heart to rest.
<|endoftext|>

I cast my hook in a single
stream; But my joy is as though I
possessed a Kingdom.
<|endoftext|>

I loose my hair and
go singing; To the four frontiers
men join in my refrain.
<|endoftext|>

This is the purport
of my song: “My thoughts shall wander
in the Great Void.
<|endoftext|>

” A gentle wind fans
the calm night: A bright moon shines
on the high tower.
<|endoftext|>

A voice whispers, but no one
answers when I call: A shadow stirs, but
no one comes when I beckon.
<|endoftext|>

The kitchen-man brings in
a dish of lentils: Wine is there,
but I do not fill my cup.
<|endoftext|>

Contentment with poverty
is Fortune’s best gift: Riches and Honour are
the handmaids of Disaster.
<|endoftext|>

Though gold and gems by the
world are sought and prized, To me they
seem no more than weeds or chaff.
<|endoftext|>

How sad it is
to be a
woman!
<|endoftext|>

Nothing
on earth is held
so cheap.
<|endoftext|>

Boys stand leaning
at the door Like Gods fallen
out of Heaven.
<|endoftext|>

Their hearts brave the Four
Oceans, The wind and dust of
a thousand miles.
<|endoftext|>

No one is glad when
a girl is born: By _her_ the
family sets no store.
<|endoftext|>

When she grows up, she hides
in her room Afraid to look
a man in the face.
<|endoftext|>

No one cries when
she leaves her home-- Sudden
as clouds when the rain stops.
<|endoftext|>

She bows her head and composes
her face, Her teeth are pressed on her red lips:
She bows and kneels countless times.
<|endoftext|>

She must humble
herself even to
the servants.
<|endoftext|>

_His_ love is distant as
the stars in Heaven, Yet the sunflower
bends toward the sun.
<|endoftext|>

Their hearts more sundered than water
and fire-- A hundred evils
are heaped upon her.
<|endoftext|>

Her face will follow
the years’ changes: Her lord
will find new pleasures.
<|endoftext|>

They that were once like
substance and shadow Are now
as far as Hu from Ch’in.
<|endoftext|>

Yet Hu and Ch’in shall sooner
meet Than they whose parting is like
Ts’an and Ch’ēn.
<|endoftext|>

When I was young I played with
a soft brush And was passionately
devoted to reading all sorts of books.
<|endoftext|>

But then the arrows
began singing at
the frontier.
<|endoftext|>

And a winged summons
came flying to the
City.
<|endoftext|>

Although arms were
not my profession, I had
once read Jang-Chū’s war-book.
<|endoftext|>

I shouted aloud and my cries
rent the air: I felt as though Tung Wu were
already annihilated.
<|endoftext|>

The scholar’s knife cuts best at its
first use And my dreams hurried on to
the completion of my plan.
<|endoftext|>

I wanted at a stroke to
clear the Yang-tze and Hsiang, And at a glance to
quell the Tibetans and Hu.
<|endoftext|>

When my task was done, I should not accept
a barony, But refusing with a bow, retire
to a cottage in the country.
<|endoftext|>

Flap, flap, the captive bird
in the cage Beating its wings
against the four corners.
<|endoftext|>

Depressed, depressed the scholar
in the narrow street: Clasping a shadow,
he dwells in an empty house.
<|endoftext|>

When he goes out, there is nowhere
for him to go: Bunches and brambles
block up his path.
<|endoftext|>

He composes a memorial,
but it is rejected and unread, He is left stranded,
like a fish in a dry pond.
<|endoftext|>

Without--he has not a single farthing
of salary: Within--there is not a peck
of grain in his larder.
<|endoftext|>

His relations upbraid him
for his lack of success: His friends and callers
daily decrease in number.
<|endoftext|>

I once hoped to pluck
the fruits of life: But now alas,
they are all withered and dry.
<|endoftext|>

Though one drinks at a river, one cannot
drink more than a bellyful; Enough is good, but
there is no use in satiety.
<|endoftext|>

The bird in a forest can
perch but on one bough, And this should
be the wise man’s pattern.
<|endoftext|>

At Pei-mang how they rise
to Heaven, Those high mounds, four
or five in the fields!
<|endoftext|>

What men lie
buried under
these tombs?
<|endoftext|>

All of
them were Lords
of the Han world.
<|endoftext|>

When the dynasty was falling,
tumult and disorder arose, Thieves and
robbers roamed like wild beasts.
<|endoftext|>

Of earth they have carried away
more than one handful, They have gone into
vaults and opened the secret doors.
<|endoftext|>

Over everything the
tangled thorns are growing: A herd-boy pushes
through them up the path.
<|endoftext|>

Down in the thorns rabbits
have made their burrows: The weeds and thistles
will never be cleared away.
<|endoftext|>

Over the tombs the ploughshare
will be driven And peasants will have their
fields and orchards there.
<|endoftext|>

They that were once lords of a
thousand hosts Are now become the dust
of the hills and ridges.
<|endoftext|>

” When I was alive, I wandered in
the streets of the Capital: Now that I am
dead, I am left to lie in the fields.
<|endoftext|>

In the morning I drove
out from the High Hall: In the evening I
lodged beneath the Yellow Springs.
<|endoftext|>

When the white sun had sunk in
the Western Chasm I hung up my chariot
and rested my four horses.
<|endoftext|>

Now, even the mighty
Maker of All Could not bring the
life back to my limbs.
<|endoftext|>

Shape and substance day
by day will vanish: Hair and teeth will
gradually fall away.
<|endoftext|>

Forever from of
old men have been so: And none
born can escape this thing.
<|endoftext|>

Living in retirement beyond the World,
Silently enjoying isolation, I pull the rope of my door
tighter And stuff my window with roots and ferns.
<|endoftext|>

My spirit is tuned to
the Spring-season: At the fall of the
year there is autumn in my heart.
<|endoftext|>

Thus imitating
cosmic changes My cottage becomes
a Universe.
<|endoftext|>

Shady, shady the wood
in front of the Hall: At midsummer
full of calm shadows.
<|endoftext|>

The south wind follows summer’s
train: With its eddying-puffs it blows
open my coat.
<|endoftext|>

I am free from
ties and can live a life of
retirement.
<|endoftext|>

When I rise
from sleep, I play with
books and harp.
<|endoftext|>

The lettuce in the garden
still grows moist: Of last year’s grain there is
always plenty left.
<|endoftext|>

Self-support should maintain strict
limits: More than enough
is not what I want.
<|endoftext|>

I grind millet and make
good wine: When the wine is heated,
I pour it out for myself.
<|endoftext|>

My little children are
playing at my side, Learning to
talk, they babble unformed sounds.
<|endoftext|>

These things have made me happy
again And I forget my
lost cap of office.
<|endoftext|>

Distant, distant I gaze at the white
clouds: With a deep yearning I think of the
Sages of Antiquity.
<|endoftext|>

In the quiet of the morning
I heard a knock at my door: I threw on my
clothes and opened it myself.
<|endoftext|>

I asked who it was who had come so
early to see me: He said he was a peasant,
coming with good intent.
<|endoftext|>

He brought a present of
wine and rice-soup, Believing that I had
fallen on evil days.
<|endoftext|>

“You live in rags under a
thatched roof And seem to have no
desire for a better lot.
<|endoftext|>

The rest of mankind have
all the same ambitions: You, too, must
learn to wallow in their mire.
<|endoftext|>

” “Old man, I am impressed
by what you say, But my soul is
not fashioned like other men’s.
<|endoftext|>

To drive in their rut I might
perhaps learn: To be untrue to myself
could only lead to muddle.
<|endoftext|>

Let us drink and enjoy together
the wine you have brought: For my course is set and
cannot now be altered.
<|endoftext|>

” A long time ago I went
on a journey, Right to the corner
Of the Eastern Ocean.
<|endoftext|>

The road there Was
long and winding, And stormy
waves Barred my path.
<|endoftext|>

What made me
Go this
way?
<|endoftext|>

Hunger drove
me Into
the World.
<|endoftext|>

I tried hard To fill
my belly: And even a
little Seemed a lot.
<|endoftext|>

But this was clearly
A bad bargain, So I went
home And lived in idleness.
<|endoftext|>

High and low, wise and
simple, all busily hoard
up the moments of life.
<|endoftext|>

Therefore I have to the uttermost exposed the
bitterness both of Substance and Shadow, and have made Spirit show how,
by following Nature, we may dissolve this bitterness.
<|endoftext|>

Heaven and Earth exist
for ever: Mountains and rivers
never change.
<|endoftext|>

But herbs and trees in perpetual rotation
Are renovated and withered by the dews and frosts: And Man the wise,
Man the divine-- Shall he alone escape this law?
<|endoftext|>

Fortuitously appearing
for a moment in the World He suddenly
departs, never to return.
<|endoftext|>

How can he know that
the friends he has left Are missing
him and thinking of him?
<|endoftext|>

Only the things that
he used remain; They look upon
them and their tears flow.
<|endoftext|>

Me no magical
arts can save, Though you may hope
for a wizard’s aid.
<|endoftext|>

I beg you listen
to this advice-- When you can get
wine, be sure to drink it.
<|endoftext|>

There is
no way to preserve
life.
<|endoftext|>

Drugs of
Immortality
are instruments of folly.
<|endoftext|>

I would gladly wander
in Paradise, But it is far
away and there is no road.
<|endoftext|>

Since the day that I
was joined to you We have shared
all our joys and pains.
<|endoftext|>

While you rested in the shade,
I left you a while: But till the end
we shall be together.
<|endoftext|>

Our joint existence
is impermanent: Sadly together
we shall slip away.
<|endoftext|>

That when the body decays Fame
should also go Is a thought
unendurable, burning the heart.
<|endoftext|>

Let us strive and
labour while yet we may To
do some deed that men will praise.
<|endoftext|>

Wine may in truth dispel
our sorrow, But how compare
it with lasting Fame?
<|endoftext|>

God can only set
in motion: He cannot control
the things he has made.
<|endoftext|>

Man, the second
of the Three Orders, Owes his
precedence to Me.
<|endoftext|>

Though I am different from you, We were
born involved in one another: Nor by any means can we
escape The intimate sharing of good and ill.
<|endoftext|>

The Three
Emperors were saintly
men, Yet to-day--where are they?
<|endoftext|>

P’ēng lived to a
great age, Yet he went at last, when
he longed to stay.
<|endoftext|>

And late or soon,
all go: Wise and simple
have no reprieve.
<|endoftext|>

Wine may bring
forgetfulness, But does
it not hasten old-age?
<|endoftext|>

If you set your heart
on noble deeds, How do you
know that any will praise you?
<|endoftext|>

Chill and harsh the year draws
to its close: In my cotton dress
I seek sunlight on the porch.
<|endoftext|>

In the southern orchard
all the leaves are gone: In the north
garden rotting boughs lie heaped.
<|endoftext|>

I empty my cup and
drink it down to the dregs: I look towards
the kitchen, but no smoke rises.
<|endoftext|>

Poems and books lie piled beside
my chair: But the light is going and I shall
not have time to read them.
<|endoftext|>

My life here is not like the
Agony in Ch’ēn, But often I
have to bear bitter reproaches.
<|endoftext|>

Let me then remember, to
calm my heart’s distress, That the Sages
of old were often in like case.
<|endoftext|>

White hair covers my temples, I am
wrinkled and seared beyond repair, And though I have
got five sons, They all hate paper and brush.
<|endoftext|>

A-shu is eighteen: For
laziness there is
none like him.
<|endoftext|>

A-hsüan does his
best, But really
loathes the Fine Arts.
<|endoftext|>

But
does not
know “six” from “seven.
<|endoftext|>

” T’ung-tzŭ in his ninth
year Is only concerned
with things to eat.
<|endoftext|>

If Heaven treats
me like this, What can I
do but fill my cup?
<|endoftext|>

I built my hut in a zone
of human habitation, Yet near me there
sounds no noise of horse or coach.
<|endoftext|>

Would you know
how that is
possible?
<|endoftext|>

A heart that is
distant creates a
wilderness round it.
<|endoftext|>

I pluck chrysanthemums under
the eastern hedge, Then gaze long at the
distant summer hills.
<|endoftext|>

The mountain air is fresh at
the dusk of day: The flying birds
two by two return.
<|endoftext|>

In these things there lies a
deep meaning; Yet when we would express it,
words suddenly fail us.
<|endoftext|>

My old desire to live in the
Southern Village Was not because I had taken
a fancy to the house.
<|endoftext|>

But I heard it was a place
of simple-minded men With whom it were a joy to
spend the mornings and evenings.
<|endoftext|>

Many years I had longed
to settle here: Now at last I have
managed to move house.
<|endoftext|>

I do not mind if my cottage
is rather small So long as there’s room enough
for bed and mat.
<|endoftext|>

Often and often the
neighbours come to see me And with brave words
discuss the things of old.
<|endoftext|>

Rare writings we read together
and praise: Doubtful meanings we examine
together and settle.
<|endoftext|>

When I was young, I was out
of tune with the herd: My only love
was for the hills and mountains.
<|endoftext|>

Unwitting I fell into
the Web of the World’s dust And was not
free until my thirtieth year.
<|endoftext|>

The migrant bird longs
for the old wood: The fish in the tank thinks
of its native pool.
<|endoftext|>

I had rescued from wildness a patch
of the Southern Moor And, still rustic, I
returned to field and garden.
<|endoftext|>

My ground covers no more than
ten acres: My thatched cottage
has eight or nine rooms.
<|endoftext|>

Elms and willows cluster
by the eaves: Peach trees and plum trees
grow before the Hall.
<|endoftext|>

Hazy, hazy
the distant hamlets
of men.
<|endoftext|>

Steady the smoke of the half-deserted village,
A dog barks somewhere in the deep lanes, A cock crows at
the top of the mulberry tree.
<|endoftext|>

At gate and courtyard--no murmur
of the World’s dust: In the empty
rooms--leisure and deep stillness.
<|endoftext|>

Long I lived checked by the
bars of a cage: Now I have turned again
to Nature and Freedom.
<|endoftext|>

In the month of June the
grass grows high And round my cottage
thick-leaved branches sway.
<|endoftext|>

There is not a bird but delights
in the place where it rests: And I
too--love my thatched cottage.
<|endoftext|>

I have done
my ploughing: I
have sown my seed.
<|endoftext|>

Again I have
time to sit and
read my books.
<|endoftext|>

In the narrow lane there are
no deep ruts: Often my friends’
carriages turn back.
<|endoftext|>

In high spirits I pour
out my spring wine And pluck the
lettuce growing in my garden.
<|endoftext|>

A gentle rain comes stealing
up from the east And a sweet wind bears
it company.
<|endoftext|>

My thoughts float idly over the story
of King Chou My eyes wander over the
pictures of Hills and Seas.
<|endoftext|>

At a single
glance I survey the whole
Universe.
<|endoftext|>

He will never be
happy, whom such pleasures
fail to please!
<|endoftext|>

The lingering clouds, rolling,
rolling, And the settled rain, dripping, dripping,
In the Eight Directions--the same dusk.
<|endoftext|>

The level
lands--one great
river.
<|endoftext|>

Wine I have, wine I
have: Idly I drink at the
eastern window.
<|endoftext|>

Longingly--I think
of my friends, But neither
boat nor carriage comes.
<|endoftext|>

Swiftly
the years, beyond
recall.
<|endoftext|>

Solemn the stillness
of this fair
morning.
<|endoftext|>

I will clothe myself
in spring-clothing And visit the
slopes of the Eastern Hill.
<|endoftext|>

By the mountain-stream a
mist hovers, Hovers a moment,
then scatters.
<|endoftext|>

There comes a wind blowing
from the south That brushes the
fields of new corn.
<|endoftext|>

I collected
together All
my friends.
<|endoftext|>

Loud talk And simple feasting:
Discussion of philosophy,
Investigation of subtleties.
<|endoftext|>

Tongues loosened
And minds at
one.
<|endoftext|>

Hearts refreshed
By discharge of
emotion!
<|endoftext|>

High rises the
Eastern Peak Soaring up
to the blue sky.
<|endoftext|>

Among the rocks--an
empty hollow, Secret,
still, mysterious!
<|endoftext|>

Uncarved and unhewn,
Screened by nature with a
roof of clouds.
<|endoftext|>

Times and Seasons, what
things are you Bringing to my
life ceaseless change?
<|endoftext|>

I will lodge for ever
in this hollow Where Springs
and Autumns unheeded pass.
<|endoftext|>

All night I could
not sleep Because of the
moonlight on my bed.
<|endoftext|>

I kept on hearing
a voice calling: Out of Nowhere,
Nothing answered “yes.
<|endoftext|>

” I will carry my coat and
not put on my belt; With unpainted eyebrows
I will stand at the front window.
<|endoftext|>

My tiresome petticoat
keeps on flapping about; If it opens a little,
I shall blame the spring wind.
<|endoftext|>

I heard my love was
going to Yang-chou And went with
him as far as Ch’u-shan.
<|endoftext|>

For a moment when you held
me fast in your outstretched arms I thought the river
stood still and did not flow.
<|endoftext|>

I have brought my pillow and am
lying at the northern window, So come to
me and play with me awhile.
<|endoftext|>

With so much quarrelling
and so few kisses How long do you
think our love can last?
<|endoftext|>

Her door opened on the white water Close
by the side of the timber bridge: That’s where the little lady
lived All alone without a lover.
<|endoftext|>

Green rushes with red shoots, Long leaves
bending to the wind-- You and I in the same boat
Plucking rushes at the Five Lakes.
<|endoftext|>

We started at dawn from
the orchid-island: We rested under
the elms till noon.
<|endoftext|>

You and I plucking
rushes Had not plucked a handful
when night came!
<|endoftext|>

“Seeing the plum-tree I thought of the Western
Island And I plucked a branch to send
to the North Country.
<|endoftext|>

I put on my dress of
apricot-yellow silk And bound up my hair
black as the crow’s wing.
<|endoftext|>

But which is the road
that leads to the Western
Island?
<|endoftext|>

I’ll ask the man at
the ferry by the
Bridge of Boats.
<|endoftext|>

But the sun is sinking and the
orioles flying home: And the wind is blowing
and sighing in the walnut-tree.
<|endoftext|>

I’ll stand under the tree just
beside the gate: I’ll stand by the door
and show off my enamelled hair-pins.
<|endoftext|>

” She’s opened the gate, but
her lover has not come: She’s gone out
at the gate to pluck red lotus.
<|endoftext|>

As she plucks the lotus on the
southern dyke in autumn, The lotus flowers
stand higher than a man’s head.
<|endoftext|>

She bends down--and plays with
the lotus seeds, The lotus
seeds are green like the lake-water.
<|endoftext|>

She gathers the flowers and
puts them into her gown-- The lotus-bud
that is red all through.
<|endoftext|>

She thinks of her lover, her lover
that does not come: She looks up and sees the wild geese flying--
The Western Island is full of wild geese.
<|endoftext|>

To look for her
lover she climbs
the Blue Tower.
<|endoftext|>

The tower is high: she
looks, but cannot see: All day she leans
on the balcony rails.
<|endoftext|>

The rail is
twisted into
a twelve-fold pattern.
<|endoftext|>

She lets fall her
hand white like the colour
of jade.
<|endoftext|>

She rolls up the awning,
she sees the wide sky, And the sea-water
waving its vacant blue.
<|endoftext|>

“The sea shall carry my dreams far
away, So that you shall be sorry
at last for my sorrow.
<|endoftext|>

If the South wind--only knew my
thoughts It would blow my dreams till they got
to the Western Island.
<|endoftext|>

” I was brought up under
the Stone Castle: My window opened
on to the castle tower.
<|endoftext|>

In the castle were
beautiful young men Who waved to me as
they went in and out.
<|endoftext|>

Chiang-nan is a glorious
and beautiful land, And Chin-ling an
exalted and kingly province!
<|endoftext|>

The green canals of the city
stretch on and on And its high towers
stretch up and up.
<|endoftext|>

Flying gables lean over
the bridle-road: Drooping willows cover
the Royal Aqueduct.
<|endoftext|>

Shrill flutes sing by the coach’s
awning, And reiterated
drums bang near its painted wheels.
<|endoftext|>

The names of the
deserving shall be carved
on the Cloud Terrace.
<|endoftext|>

And for those who
have done valiantly
rich reward awaits.
<|endoftext|>

Now late I follow Time’s
Necessity: Mounting a barricade
I pacify remote tribes.
<|endoftext|>

Discarding my sash I don a
coat of rhinoceros-skin: Rolling up my
skirts I shoulder a black bow.
<|endoftext|>

Even at the very
start my strength fails: What will become of me
before it’s all over?
<|endoftext|>

Red hills lie athwart us
as a menace in the west, And fiery
mountains glare terrible in the south.
<|endoftext|>

The body burns, the
head aches and throbs: If a bird light here, its
soul forthwith departs.
<|endoftext|>

Warm springs Pour from
cloudy pools And hot smoke issues
between the rocks.
<|endoftext|>

The sun and moon are
perpetually obscured: The
rain and dew never stay dry.
<|endoftext|>

There are red serpents
a hundred feet long, And black snakes
ten girths round.
<|endoftext|>

The sand-spitters shoot their poison at
the sunbeams: The flying insects are
ill with the shifting glare.
<|endoftext|>

The hungry monkeys dare not
come down to eat: The morning birds
dare not set out to fly.
<|endoftext|>

At the Ching river many die
of poison: Crossing the Lu one is lucky
if one is only ill.
<|endoftext|>

Our living feet
walk on dead ground: Our high
wills surmount the snares of Fate.
<|endoftext|>

The Spear-boat General
got but little honour: The
Wave-subduer met with scant reward.
<|endoftext|>

If our Prince still grudges the things
that are easy to give, Can he hope that his
soldiers will give what is hardest to give?
<|endoftext|>

“I heard at night your
long sighs And knew that you
were thinking of me.
<|endoftext|>

” As she spoke, the doors of Heaven
opened And our souls conversed
and I saw her face.
<|endoftext|>

She set me a
pillow to rest on And
she brought me meat and drink.
<|endoftext|>

I stood beside her where she lay, But suddenly
woke and she was not there: And none knew how my soul was torn, How
the tears fell surging over my breast.
<|endoftext|>

In the high trees--many doleful
winds: The ocean waters--lashed
into waves.
<|endoftext|>

If the sharp sword be not
in your hand, How can you hope your friends
will remain many?
<|endoftext|>

Do you not
see that sparrow
on the fence?
<|endoftext|>

Seeing the hawk
it casts itself
into the snare.
<|endoftext|>

The fowler to catch the
sparrow is delighted: The Young Man to
see the sparrow is grieved.
<|endoftext|>

He takes his sword and
cuts through the netting: The yellow sparrow
flies away, away.
<|endoftext|>

Away, away, up
to the blue sky And down again
to thank the Young Man.
<|endoftext|>

A beautiful place
is the town of Lo-yang: The big streets
are full of spring light.
<|endoftext|>

The lads go driving out with harps
in their hands: The mulberry girls go out
to the fields with their baskets.
<|endoftext|>

Golden whips
glint at the
horses’ flanks.
<|endoftext|>

Gauze sleeves
brush the green
boughs.
<|endoftext|>

Racing dawn, the carriages
come home,-- And the girls with their high baskets
full of fruit.
<|endoftext|>

My bed is so empty that
I keep on waking up: As the cold increases,
the night-wind begins to blow.
<|endoftext|>

It rustles the curtains, making
a noise like the sea: Oh that those were waves
which could carry me back to you!
<|endoftext|>

Entering the Hall, she
meets the new wife: Leaving the gate, she runs
into her former husband.
<|endoftext|>

Words stick: she does not manage to
say anything: She presses her hands
together and hesitates.
<|endoftext|>

Agitates moon-like fan--sheds pearl-like tears-- Realizes
she loves him just as much as ever: That her present
pain will never come to an end.
<|endoftext|>

Who says That it’s by my
desire, This separation,
this living so far from you?
<|endoftext|>

My dress still smells of the
lavender you gave: My hand still holds
the letter that you sent.
<|endoftext|>

Round my waist I wear
a double sash: I dream that it binds us
both with a same-heart knot.
<|endoftext|>

Did not you know that people
hide their love, Like a flower that seems too
precious to be picked?
<|endoftext|>

Of marsh-mallows my
boat is made, The
ropes are lily-roots.
<|endoftext|>

The pole-star is
athwart the sky: The
moon sinks low.
<|endoftext|>

It’s at
the ferry I’m plucking
lilies.
<|endoftext|>

But it might be the Yellow River--
So afraid you seem of the wind and waves, So long
you tarry at the crossing.
<|endoftext|>

The road that I came by mounts
eight thousand feet: The river that I
crossed hangs a hundred fathoms.
<|endoftext|>

The brambles
so thick that in summer
one cannot pass!
<|endoftext|>

The snow so
high that in winter
one cannot climb!
<|endoftext|>

With branches that interlace
Lung Valley is dark: Against cliffs that tower
one’s voice beats and echoes.
<|endoftext|>

I turn my head, and it
seems only a dream That I ever
lived in the streets of Hsien-yang.
<|endoftext|>

The evening river is level
and motionless-- The spring colours
just open to their full.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly a wave carries
the moon away And the tidal water
comes with its freight of stars.
<|endoftext|>

Kao Huan, that
son of a mouse Dared
to attack King Pi.
<|endoftext|>

But at the first stroke of sword
and bow, The aggressor’s plot
recoiled on himself.
<|endoftext|>

Tchirek River Lies under the
Dark Mountains: Where the sky is like the sides of a
tent Stretched down over the Great Steppe.
<|endoftext|>

The sky is gray, gray: And the
steppe wide, wide: Over grass that the wind has battered
low Sheep and oxen roam.
<|endoftext|>

Business men boast of their skill
and cunning But in philosophy they
are like little children.
<|endoftext|>

Bragging to each other of successful
depredations They neglect to consider the
ultimate fate of the body.
<|endoftext|>

“Tell me now, what should a
man want But to sit alone, sipping
his cup of wine?
<|endoftext|>

Then I could jog through a
happy five-score years And, at the
end, need no Paradise.
<|endoftext|>

These days, continually
fuddled with drink, I fail to satisfy
the appetites of the soul.
<|endoftext|>

But seeing men all
behaving like drunkards, How can I
alone remain sober?
<|endoftext|>

“His subjects were always original,
but his poems are seldom worth quoting,” is
a Chinese opinion of him.
<|endoftext|>

I loved you dearly,
Stone Fish Lake, With your rock-island shaped
like a swimming fish!
<|endoftext|>

On the fish’s back is
the Wine-cup Hollow And round the fish,--the flowing
waters of the Lake.
<|endoftext|>

The boys on the shore sent little
wooden ships, Each made to carry a
single cup of wine.
<|endoftext|>

The island-drinkers emptied
the liquor-boats And set their sails and
sent them back for more.
<|endoftext|>

On the shores of the Lake were
jutting slabs of rock And under the rocks
there flowed an icy stream.
<|endoftext|>

Heated with wine, to rinse
our mouths and hands In those cold waters
was a joy beyond compare!
<|endoftext|>

Of gold and jewels I
have not any need; For Caps and
Coaches I do not care at all.
<|endoftext|>

But I wish I could sit on the
rocky banks of the Lake For ever and ever
staring at the Stone Fish.
<|endoftext|>

Above the lake are deep
mountain valleys, And men dwelling
whose hearts are without guile.
<|endoftext|>

Gay like children, they swarm
to the tops of the trees; And run to
the water to catch bream and trout.
<|endoftext|>

Their pleasures are the same as
those of beasts and birds; They put no restraint
either on body or mind.
<|endoftext|>

Far I have wandered throughout
the Nine Lands; Wherever I went such
manners had disappeared.
<|endoftext|>

I find myself standing and
wondering, perplexed, Whether Saints and Sages
have really done us good.
<|endoftext|>

The hills and rivers
of the lowland country You
have made your battle-ground.
<|endoftext|>

How do you suppose
the people who live there Will
procure “firewood and hay”?
<|endoftext|>

Do not let me hear you talking together
About titles and promotions; For a single general’s
reputation Is made out of ten thousand corpses.
<|endoftext|>

Families, when a
child is born Want it to
be intelligent.
<|endoftext|>

I, through intelligence, Having
wrecked my whole life, Only hope the baby will
prove Ignorant and stupid.
<|endoftext|>

Then he will crown a tranquil
life By becoming a Cabinet
Minister.
<|endoftext|>

An old man selling
charms in a cranny of
the town wall.
<|endoftext|>

He writes out spells
to bless the silkworms and spells
to protect the corn.
<|endoftext|>

With the money he
gets each day he only
buys wine.
<|endoftext|>

But he does not worry
when his legs get wobbly, For he
has a boy to lean on.
<|endoftext|>

Away and away
I sail in my light boat; My heart leaps with
a great gust of joy.
<|endoftext|>

Through the leafless branches I
see the temple in the wood; Over the dwindling
stream the stone bridge towers.
<|endoftext|>

Down the grassy lanes sheep
and oxen pass; In the misty
village cranes and magpies cry.
<|endoftext|>

Back in my home I drink
a cup of wine And need not fear
the greed of the evening wind.
<|endoftext|>

In the southern village
the boy who minds the ox With his naked
feet stands on the ox’s back.
<|endoftext|>

Through the hole in his coat
the river wind blows; Through his broken
hat the mountain rain pours.
<|endoftext|>

On the long dyke he seemed to
be far away; In the narrow lane
suddenly we were face to face.
<|endoftext|>

The boy is home and the
ox is back in its stall; And a dark smoke
oozes through the thatched roof.
<|endoftext|>

Of Spring water,--thirty
or forty miles: In the
evening sunlight,--three or four houses.
<|endoftext|>

Youths and boys minding
geese and ducks: Women and girls tending
mulberries and hemp.
<|endoftext|>

The place,--remote: their coats
and scarves old: The year,--fruitful: their
talk and laughter gay.
<|endoftext|>

The old wanderer moors
his flat boat And staggers up the bank to
pluck wistaria flowers.
<|endoftext|>

The little cart
jolting and banging through the
yellow haze of dusk.
<|endoftext|>

The man pushing
behind: the woman
pulling in front.
<|endoftext|>

They have left the city
and do not know where
to go.
<|endoftext|>

“Green, green, those elm-tree leaves: _they_ will cure
my hunger, If only we could find some quiet
place and sup on them together.
<|endoftext|>

” The wind has flattened the yellow
mother-wort: Above it in the distance they
see the walls of a house.
<|endoftext|>

“_There_ surely must be
people living who’ll give you
something to eat.
<|endoftext|>

” They tap at the door, but
no one comes: they look in, but
the kitchen is empty.
<|endoftext|>

They stand hesitating
in the lonely road and their
tears fall like rain.
<|endoftext|>

Since I left my home
to seek official state Seven
years I have lived in Ch’ang-an.
<|endoftext|>

What
have I
gained?
<|endoftext|>

We have roamed on horseback under
the flowering trees; We have walked in the snow
and warmed our hearts with wine.
<|endoftext|>

We have met and parted at
the Western Gate And neither of us
bothered to put on Cap or Belt.
<|endoftext|>

We did not go up together
for Examination; We were not serving in
the same Department of State.
<|endoftext|>

The bond that joined us
lay deeper than outward things; The rivers
of our souls spring from the same well!
<|endoftext|>

I look down on
the Twelve City Streets:-- Red dust
flanked by green trees!
<|endoftext|>

Coaches and horsemen alone
fill my eyes; I do not see whom my
heart longs to see.
<|endoftext|>

Of all that walk on
the North-South Road There is not one that I care
for more than the rest!
<|endoftext|>

At the palace doors the smell of
meat and wine; On the road the bones of
one who was frozen to death.
<|endoftext|>

Ten leagues riding, always
facing to the North; The cold wind
almost blew off my ears.
<|endoftext|>

I waited for the bell outside
the Five Gates; I waited for the summons
within the Triple Hall.
<|endoftext|>

My hair and beard were frozen
and covered with icicles; My coat
and robe--chilly like water.
<|endoftext|>

At the western window I
paused from writing rescripts; The pines and bamboos
were all buried in stillness.
<|endoftext|>

The moon rose and a
calm wind came; Suddenly, it was like
an evening in the hills.
<|endoftext|>

And so, as I dozed, I dreamed
of the South West And thought I was staying
at the Hsien-yu Temple.
<|endoftext|>

When I woke and heard the dripping
of the Palace clock I still thought it the
murmur of a mountain stream.
<|endoftext|>

The snow has
gone from Chung-nan; spring is
almost come.
<|endoftext|>

Lovely in the distance
its blue colours, against the
brown of the streets.
<|endoftext|>

A thousand coaches, ten thousand
horsemen pass down the Nine Roads; Turns his head
and looks at the mountains,--not one man!
<|endoftext|>

We talked together
in the Yung-shou Temple; We parted to the
north of the Hsin-ch’ang dyke.
<|endoftext|>

Going home--I shed a
few tears, Grieving about
things,--not sorry for you.
<|endoftext|>

Long, long the road to
Lan-t’ien; You said yourself you would
not be able to write.
<|endoftext|>

Reckoning up your halts
for eating and sleeping-- By this time
you’ve crossed the Shang mountains.
<|endoftext|>

Last night the clouds scattered
away; A thousand leagues, the
same moonlight scene.
<|endoftext|>

When dawn came, I dreamt I saw
your face; It must have been that you
were thinking of me.
<|endoftext|>

In my dream, I thought I
held your hand And asked you to tell
me what your thoughts were.
<|endoftext|>

And _you_ said: “I miss you
bitterly, But there’s no one here to
send to you with a letter.
<|endoftext|>

” When I awoke, before
I had time to speak, A knocking on
the door sounded “Doong, doong!
<|endoftext|>

” They came and told me a
messenger from Shang-chou Had brought a
letter,--a single scroll from you!
<|endoftext|>

Up from my pillow I
suddenly sprang out of bed, And
threw you my clothes, all topsy-turvy.
<|endoftext|>

I undid the knot and
saw the letter within; A single sheet
with thirteen lines of writing.
<|endoftext|>

At the top it told the sorrows
of an exile’s heart; At the bottom it described
the pains of separation.
<|endoftext|>

The sorrows and pains took
up so much space There was no room left
to talk about the weather!
<|endoftext|>

But you said that when you wrote You were staying for the
night to the east of Shang-chou; Sitting alone, lighted by a solitary
candle Lodging in the mountain hostel of Yang-Ch’ēng.
<|endoftext|>

Night was late when you
finished writing, The mountain moon
was slanting towards the west.
<|endoftext|>

What is it
lies aslant across
the moon?
<|endoftext|>

A single tree of purple _paulovnia_ flowers--
Paulovnia flowers just on the point of falling Are a
symbol to express “thinking of an absent friend.
<|endoftext|>

” Lovingly--you wrote on the back
side, To send in the letter, your “Poem of
the Paulovnia Flower.
<|endoftext|>

” The Poem of the Paulovnia
Flower has eight rhymes; Yet these eight couplets have
cast a spell on my heart.
<|endoftext|>

They have taken hold of this
morning’s thoughts And carried them to yours, the
night you wrote your letter.
<|endoftext|>

The whole poem
I read three times; Each verse ten
times I recite.
<|endoftext|>

So precious to me are the
fourscore words That each letter changes
into a bar of gold!
<|endoftext|>

When the yellow bird’s note was almost stopped; And half
formed the green plum’s fruit; Sitting and grieving that spring things were over,
I rose and entered the Eastern Garden’s gate.
<|endoftext|>

I carried my cup and was
dully drinking alone: Suddenly I
heard a knocking sound at the door.
<|endoftext|>

Dwelling secluded, I was
glad that someone had come; How much the more,
when I saw it was Ch’ēn Hsiung!
<|endoftext|>

At ease and leisure,--all day
we talked; Crowding and jostling,--the
feelings of many years.
<|endoftext|>

How great a
thing is a single
cup of wine!
<|endoftext|>

For it makes us
tell the story of
our whole lives.
<|endoftext|>

When I was almost forty
I had a daughter whose name
was Golden Bells.
<|endoftext|>

Now it is just a year since
she was born; She is learning to
sit and cannot yet talk.
<|endoftext|>

Ashamed,--to find that I have
not a sage’s heart: I cannot resist
vulgar thoughts and feelings.
<|endoftext|>

Henceforward I am
tied to things outside myself: My only reward,--the
pleasure I am getting now.
<|endoftext|>

If I am spared the grief of
her dying young, Then I shall have the
trouble of getting her married.
<|endoftext|>

My plan for retiring
and going back to the hills Must now
be postponed for fifteen years!
<|endoftext|>

Ruined and ill,--a man
of two score; Pretty and guileless,--a
girl of three.
<|endoftext|>

Not a boy,--but, still better
than nothing: To soothe one’s feeling,--from
time to time a kiss!
<|endoftext|>

There came a day,--they suddenly
took her from me; Her soul’s shadow
wandered I know not where.
<|endoftext|>

At last, by thinking of the time
before she was born, By thought and reason
I drove the pain away.
<|endoftext|>

Since my heart forgot her, many
days have passed And three times winter
has changed to spring.
<|endoftext|>

This morning, for a little,
the old grief came back, Because, in the
road, I met her foster-nurse.
<|endoftext|>

Sad, sad--lean with long illness;
Monotonous, monotonous--days
and nights pass.
<|endoftext|>

The summer trees have clad
themselves in shade; The autumn “lan” already
houses the dew.
<|endoftext|>

The eggs that lay in the
nest when I took to bed Have changed into little
birds and flown away.
<|endoftext|>

The worm that then lay hidden
in its hole Has hatched into a cricket
sitting on the tree.
<|endoftext|>

The Four Seasons go on for
ever and ever: In all Nature nothing stops
to rest Even for a moment.
<|endoftext|>

Only the sick
man’s heart Deep down still aches
as of old!
<|endoftext|>

Deep the waters of the Black Pool,
coloured like ink; They say a Holy Dragon
lives there, whom men have never seen.
<|endoftext|>

Beside the Pool they have built a shrine; the authorities
have established a ritual; A dragon by itself remains
a dragon, but men can make it a god.
<|endoftext|>

Prosperity and disaster, rain and drought,
plagues and pestilences-- By the village people were all
regarded as the Sacred Dragon’s doing.
<|endoftext|>

They all made offerings of sucking-pig and poured
libations of wine; The morning prayers and evening gifts
depended on a “medium’s” advice When the dragon comes, ah!
<|endoftext|>

The wind stirs
and sighs Paper
money thrown, ah!
<|endoftext|>

Silk
umbrellas
waved.
<|endoftext|>

When the
dragon goes,
ah!
<|endoftext|>

The
wind
also--still.
<|endoftext|>

The cups
and vessels
are cold.
<|endoftext|>

Meats lie stacked on the rocks
of the Pool’s shore; Wine flows on the grass
in front of the shrine.
<|endoftext|>

I do not know, of all those offerings,
how much the Dragon eats; But the mice of the woods and the foxes
of the hills are continually drunk and sated.
<|endoftext|>

Why are
the foxes
so lucky?
<|endoftext|>

What have the sucking-pigs done,
That year by year _they_ should be killed,
merely to glut the foxes?
<|endoftext|>

That the foxes are robbing the Sacred
Dragon and eating His sucking-pig, Beneath the nine-fold depths
of His pool, does He know or not?
<|endoftext|>

There came an officer
knocking by night at my door-- In a
loud voice demanding grain-tribute.
<|endoftext|>

My house-servants dared not wait till
the morning, But brought candles
and set them on the barn-floor.
<|endoftext|>

Passed through the sieve,
clean-washed as pearls, A whole cart-load, thirty
bushels of grain.
<|endoftext|>

But still they cry that it is
not paid in full: With whips and curses
they goad my servants and boys.
<|endoftext|>

Once, in error, I entered
public life; I am inwardly ashamed
that my talents were not sufficient.
<|endoftext|>

In succession I
occupied four official posts; For
doing nothing,--ten years’ salary!
<|endoftext|>

Often have I heard that
saying of ancient men That “good and ill
follow in an endless chain.
<|endoftext|>

” And to-day it ought to set
my heart at rest To return to others
the corn in my great barn.
<|endoftext|>

In the land of Tao-chou Many
of the people are dwarfs; The tallest of them
never grow to more than three feet.
<|endoftext|>

They were sold in the market as dwarf
slaves and yearly sent to Court; Described as “an offering
of natural products from the land of Tao-chou.
<|endoftext|>

” A strange “offering of natural products”;
I never heard of one yet That parted men from
those they loved, never to meet again!
<|endoftext|>

Old men--weeping for their
grandsons; mothers for
their children!
<|endoftext|>

One day--Yang Ch’ēng came to govern
the land; He refused to send up dwarf slaves
in spite of incessant mandates.
<|endoftext|>

” The Emperor’s heart was deeply
moved and he sealed and sent a scroll “The yearly tribute
of dwarfish slaves is henceforth annulled.
<|endoftext|>

” The people of
Tao-chou, Old ones and young ones,
how great their joy!
<|endoftext|>

Father with son and brother with
brother henceforward kept together; From that day
for ever more they lived as free men.
<|endoftext|>

The people
of Tao-chou Still enjoy
this gift.
<|endoftext|>

And even now when
they speak of the Governor
Tears start to their eyes.
<|endoftext|>

And lest their children and their children’s children
should forget the Governor’s name, When boys are born the
syllable “Yang” is often used in their forename.
<|endoftext|>

Of cord and cassia-wood
is the harp compounded: Within it
lie ancient melodies.
<|endoftext|>

Ancient melodies--weak and
savourless, Not appealing
to present men’s taste.
<|endoftext|>

Light and colour are
faded from the jade stops: Dust has
covered the rose-red strings.
<|endoftext|>

Decay and ruin came
to it long ago, But the sound that
is left is still cold and clear.
<|endoftext|>

I do not refuse to play
it, if you want me to: But even if
I play, people will not listen.
<|endoftext|>

How did it come
to be neglected
so?
<|endoftext|>

Because of
the Ch’iang flute and the
Ch’in flageolet.
<|endoftext|>

The singers have hushed their
notes of clear song: The red sleeves of the
dancers are motionless.
<|endoftext|>

Hugging his lute, the old
harper of Chao Rocks and sways as he
touches the five chords.
<|endoftext|>

The loud notes swell and
scatter abroad: “Sa, sa,” like
wind blowing the rain.
<|endoftext|>

The soft notes dying
almost to nothing: “Ch’ieh, ch’ieh,” like the
voice of ghosts talking.
<|endoftext|>

Now as glad as the magpie’s
lucky song: Again bitter as the
gibbon’s ominous cry.
<|endoftext|>

And those who sit and listen
to the tune he plays Of soul and body
lose the mastery.
<|endoftext|>

And those who pass that way as
he plays the tune, Suddenly stop
and cannot raise their feet.
<|endoftext|>

Alas, alas that the
ears of common men Should love the
modern and not love the old.
<|endoftext|>

Thus it is that the harp in
the green window Day by day is
covered deeper with dust.
<|endoftext|>

In the Royal City
spring is almost over: Tinkle, tinkle--the
coaches and horsemen pass.
<|endoftext|>

We tell each other “This is the
peony season”: And follow with the
crowd that goes to the Flower Market.
<|endoftext|>

“Cheap and dear--no uniform price:
The cost of the plant depends on the
number of blossoms.
<|endoftext|>

For the fine flower,--a hundred
pieces of damask: For the cheap
flower,--five bits of silk.
<|endoftext|>

Above is spread an awning
to protect them: Around is woven a
wattle-fence to screen them.
<|endoftext|>

If you sprinkle water and
cover the roots with mud, When they are transplanted,
they will not lose their beauty.
<|endoftext|>

” Each household thoughtlessly
follows the custom, Man by man, no
one realizing.
<|endoftext|>

There happened to be
an old farm labourer Who
came by chance that way.
<|endoftext|>

He bowed his head and sighed
a deep sigh: But this sigh
nobody understood.
<|endoftext|>

He was thinking, “A cluster
of deep-red flowers Would pay the taxes of
ten poor houses.
<|endoftext|>

” Tartars
led in chains, Tartars
led in chains!
<|endoftext|>

Their ears pierced, their
faces bruised--they are driven
into the land of Ch’in.
<|endoftext|>

The Son of Heaven
took pity on them and would not
have them slain.
<|endoftext|>

A petty officer in a yellow coat
took down their names and surnames: They were led from the city
of Ch’ang-an under escort of an armed guard.
<|endoftext|>

Their bodies were covered
with the wounds of arrows, their
bones stood out from their cheeks.
<|endoftext|>

They had grown so
weak they could only march
a single stage a day.
<|endoftext|>

In the morning they must satisfy hunger
and thirst with neither plate nor cup: At night they must lie in
their dirt and rags on beds that stank with filth.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly they came to the
Yangtze River and remembered the
waters of Chiao.
<|endoftext|>

With lowered hands
and levelled voices they sobbed
a muffled song.
<|endoftext|>

Then one Tartar lifted up his
voice and spoke to the other Tartars, “_Your_ sorrows are none
at all compared with _my_ sorrows.
<|endoftext|>

” Those that were with him in the same
band asked to hear his tale: As he tried to speak
the words were choked by anger.
<|endoftext|>

He told them
“I was born and bred in the town
of Liang-yüan.
<|endoftext|>

In the frontier wars
of Ta-li I fell into
the Tartars’ hands.
<|endoftext|>

Since the days the Tartars took me alive
forty years have passed: They put me into a coat
of skins tied with a belt of rope.
<|endoftext|>

Only on the first
of the first month might I wear
my Chinese dress.
<|endoftext|>

As I put on my
coat and arranged my cap,
how fast the tears flowed!
<|endoftext|>

I made in my heart a secret vow I
would find a way home: I hid my plan from my Tartar wife
and the children she had borne me in the land.
<|endoftext|>

I thought to myself, ‘It is well for me that
my limbs are still strong,’ And yet, being old, in my heart I
feared I should never live to return.
<|endoftext|>

The Tartar chieftains shoot so well that the
birds are afraid to fly: From the risk of their arrows I
escaped alive and fled swiftly home.
<|endoftext|>

Hiding all day and walking all night,
I crossed the Great Desert: Where clouds are dark and the moon
black and the sands eddy in the wind.
<|endoftext|>

But the moving horsemen did not hear
that I spoke the Han tongue: Their Captain took me for
a Tartar born and had me bound in chains.
<|endoftext|>

They are sending me away to the south-east,
to a low and swampy land: No one now will take pity
on me: resistance is all in vain.
<|endoftext|>

Thinking of this, my voice chokes and I
ask of Heaven above, Was I spared from death only
to spend the rest of my years in sorrow?
<|endoftext|>

My native village of Liang-yüan I
shall not see again: My wife and children in the Tartars’
land I have fruitlessly deserted.
<|endoftext|>

When I fell among Tartars and was taken prisoner,
I pined for the land of Han: Now that I am back in the land
of Han, they have turned me into a Tartar.
<|endoftext|>

Had I but known what
my fate would be, I would not
have started home!
<|endoftext|>

For the two lands, so
wide apart, are alike in
the sorrow they bring.
<|endoftext|>

Tartar
prisoners
in chains!
<|endoftext|>

Of all the sorrows
of all the prisoners mine
is the hardest to bear!
<|endoftext|>

Never in the world has so great a wrong
befallen the lot of man,-- A Han heart and a Han
tongue set in the body of a Turk.
<|endoftext|>

” A
Government-bull yoked to
a Government-cart!
<|endoftext|>

Moored by the bank
of Ch’an River, a barge
loaded with gravel.
<|endoftext|>

A single load
of gravel, How many
pounds it weighs!
<|endoftext|>

Carrying at
dawn, carrying at dusk, what
is it all for?
<|endoftext|>

They are carrying
it towards the Five Gates, To
the West of the Main Road.
<|endoftext|>

Under the shadow
of green laurels they are
making a gravel-drive.
<|endoftext|>

For yesterday arrove, newly appointed, The
Assistant Chancellor of the Realm, And was terribly afraid
that the wet and mud Would dirty his horse’s hoofs.
<|endoftext|>

The Chancellor’s horse’s hoofs Stepped on the
gravel and remained perfectly clean; But the bull employed
in dragging the cart Was almost sweating blood.
<|endoftext|>

The Assistant Chancellor’s
business Is to “save men, govern the country
And harmonize Yin and Yang.
<|endoftext|>

” Whether the bull’s neck
is sore Need not trouble
him at all.
<|endoftext|>

There was once a man who dreamt
he went to Heaven: His dream-body
soared aloft through space.
<|endoftext|>

He rode on the back of
a white-plumed crane, And was led on his flight
by two crimson banners.
<|endoftext|>

Whirring of wings
and flapping of
coat tails!
<|endoftext|>

Jade bells
suddenly
all a-tinkle!
<|endoftext|>

Half way to Heaven, he
looked down beneath him, Down on the dark
turmoil of the World.
<|endoftext|>

Gradually he
lost the place of his native town; Mountains
and water--nothing else distinct.
<|endoftext|>

The Eastern Ocean--a single
strip of white: The Hills of China,--five
specks of green.
<|endoftext|>

Gliding past him a host of fairies
swept In long procession to the Palace
of the Jade City.
<|endoftext|>

They take him to the presence of
the Mighty Jade Emperor: He bows his
head and proffers loyal homage.
<|endoftext|>

The Emperor says: “We see
you have fairy talents: Be of good
heart and do not slight yourself.
<|endoftext|>

We shall send to fetch you in
fifteen years And give you a place in the
Courtyard of Immortality.
<|endoftext|>

” Twice bowing, he acknowledged
the gracious words: Then woke from sleep, full
of wonder and joy.
<|endoftext|>

He hid his secret and dared
not tell it abroad: But vowed a vow
he would live in a cave of rock.
<|endoftext|>

From love and affection he severed
kith and kin: From his eating and drinking he
omitted savoury and spice.
<|endoftext|>

His morning meal was a
dish of coral-dust: At night he sipped an
essence of dewy mists.
<|endoftext|>

In the empty mountains he
lived for thirty years Daily watching for
the Heavenly Coach to come.
<|endoftext|>

The time of appointment
was already long past, But
of wings and coach-bells--still no sound.
<|endoftext|>

His teeth and hair daily withered
and decayed: His ears and eyes gradually
lost their keenness.
<|endoftext|>

One morning he suffered the Common
Change And his body was one with the
dust and dirt of the hill.
<|endoftext|>

Gods
and
fairies!
<|endoftext|>

If indeed such things there
be, Their ways are beyond the
striving of mortal men.
<|endoftext|>

” Though you sweat and
toil, what shall your
trouble bring?
<|endoftext|>

You will only
shorten the five-score
years of your span.
<|endoftext|>

Sad, alas, the
man who dreamt of
Fairies!
<|endoftext|>

For a single
dream spoiled his
whole life.
<|endoftext|>

Boundless,
the great
sea.
<|endoftext|>

Straight down,--no
bottom: sideways,--no
border.
<|endoftext|>

Of cloudy waves and misty billows
down in the uttermost depths Men have fabled,
in the midst there stand three sacred hills.
<|endoftext|>

On the
hills, thick growing,--herbs that banish
Death.
<|endoftext|>

Wings grow on those who
eat them and they turn into
heavenly “hsien.
<|endoftext|>

” The Lord of Ch’in and Wu of Han
believed in these stories: And magic-workers year by
year were sent to gather the herbs.
<|endoftext|>

The Blessed Islands,
now and of old, what
but an empty tale?
<|endoftext|>

The misty waters
spread before them and they knew
not where to seek.
<|endoftext|>

Dauntless,
the mighty
wind.
<|endoftext|>

Their eyes search but
cannot see the shores of
the Blessed Islands.
<|endoftext|>

They cannot find the Blessed Isles and yet
they dare not return: Youths and maidens that began
the quest grew grey on board the boat.
<|endoftext|>

Do you not see The graves
on the top of Black Horse Hill
and the tombs at Mo-ling?
<|endoftext|>

What is left but the
sighing wind blowing in the
tangled grasses?
<|endoftext|>

The Two Red Towers
North and south rise facing
each other.
<|endoftext|>

I beg to
ask, to whom do
they belong?
<|endoftext|>

The two Princes blew on their flutes and
drew down fairies from the sky, Who carried them off through the
Five Clouds, soaring away to Heaven.
<|endoftext|>

Their halls and houses, that they
could not take with them, Were turned into Temples
planted in the Dust of the World.
<|endoftext|>

In the tiring-rooms and dancers’ towers all
is silent and still; Only the willows like dancers’
arms, and the pond like a mirror.
<|endoftext|>

When the flowers are falling at yellow
twilight, when things are sad and hushed, One does not hear
songs and flutes, but only chimes and bells.
<|endoftext|>

The Imperial Patent on the
Temple doors is written in letters of gold; For nuns’ quarters
and monks’ cells ample space is allowed.
<|endoftext|>

For green moss and bright moonlight--plenty of room
provided; In a hovel opposite is a sick
man who has hardly room to lie down.
<|endoftext|>

I remember once when at P’ing-yang they were
building a great man’s house How it swallowed up the housing
space of thousands of ordinary men.
<|endoftext|>

The Immortals are leaving us, two
by two, and their houses are turned into Temples; I begin to
fear that the whole world will become a vast convent.
<|endoftext|>

An old charcoal-seller Cutting wood
and burning charcoal in the forests
of the Southern Mountain.
<|endoftext|>

His face, stained with dust
and ashes, has turned to
the colour of smoke.
<|endoftext|>

The hair on his temples
is streaked with gray: his ten
fingers are black.
<|endoftext|>

The money he gets
by selling charcoal, how
far does it go?
<|endoftext|>

It is just enough
to clothe his limbs and put food
in his mouth.
<|endoftext|>

Although, alas, the
coat on his back is a coat
without lining.
<|endoftext|>

He hopes for the coming
of cold weather, to send up
the price of coal!
<|endoftext|>

Last night, outside the city,--a whole
foot of snow; At dawn he drives the charcoal
wagon along the frozen ruts.
<|endoftext|>

Oxen,--weary; man,--hungry: the sun, already
high; Outside the Gate, to the south of the Market,
at last they stop in the mud.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly,
a pair of prancing
horsemen.
<|endoftext|>

Who
can it be
coming?
<|endoftext|>

A public official
in a yellow coat and a
boy in a white shirt.
<|endoftext|>

In their hands they hold a written warrant:
on their tongues--the words of an order; They turn back the wagon and curse
the oxen, leading them off to the north.
<|endoftext|>

A whole wagon
of charcoal, More than
a thousand pieces!
<|endoftext|>

If officials choose
to take it away, the woodman
may not complain.
<|endoftext|>

Half a piece of red silk and a single yard
of damask, The Courtiers have tied to the oxen’s collar,
as the price of a wagon of coal!
<|endoftext|>

I was going to the City to
sell the herbs I had plucked; On the way I rested
by some trees at the Blue Gate.
<|endoftext|>

Along the road there came
a horseman riding; Whose face was pale
with a strange look of dread.
<|endoftext|>

Friends and relations, waiting
to say good-bye, Pressed at his side, but
he did not dare to pause.
<|endoftext|>

I, in wonder, asked the
people about me Who he was and
what had happened to him.
<|endoftext|>

They told me this was a
Privy Councillor Whose grave duties were
like the pivot of State.
<|endoftext|>

His food allowance was ten
thousand cash; Three times a day the
Emperor came to his house.
<|endoftext|>

Yesterday he was called to
a meeting of Heroes: To-day he is banished
to the country of Yai-chou.
<|endoftext|>

So always, the Counsellors
of Kings; Favour and ruin changed
between dawn and dusk!
<|endoftext|>

Green, green,--the grass of the Eastern
Suburb; And amid the grass, a
road that leads to the hills.
<|endoftext|>

Resting in peace among the
white clouds, At last he has made
a “coup” that cannot fail!
<|endoftext|>

At Hsin-fēng an old man--four-score and
eight; The hair on his head and the hair of
his eyebrows--white as the new snow.
<|endoftext|>

Leaning on the shoulders of his great-grandchildren,
he walks in front of the Inn; With his left arm he leans
on their shoulders; his right arm is broken.
<|endoftext|>

I asked the old man how many years
had passed since he broke his arm; I also asked the cause of the
injury, how and why it happened?
<|endoftext|>

The old man said he was born and reared
in the District of Hsin-fēng; At the time of his
birth--a wise reign; no wars or discords.
<|endoftext|>

“Often I listened in the Pear-Tree Garden to
the sound of flute and song; Naught I knew of banner and
lance; nothing of arrow or bow.
<|endoftext|>

Then came the wars of T’ien-pao
and the great levy of men; Of three men
in each house,--one man was taken.
<|endoftext|>

And those to
whom the lot fell, where
were they taken to?
<|endoftext|>

When the great army waded across, the
water seethed like a cauldron; When barely ten had entered
the water, two or three were dead.
<|endoftext|>

To the north of my village,
to the south of my village the sound of
weeping and wailing.
<|endoftext|>

Children parting
from fathers and mothers; husbands
parting from wives.
<|endoftext|>

Everyone says that in expeditions
against the Min tribes Of a million men who
are sent out, not one returns.
<|endoftext|>

I, that am old, was then
twenty-four; My name and fore-name were written down
in the rolls of the Board of War.
<|endoftext|>

In the depth of the night not daring
to let any one know I secretly took a huge
stone and dashed it against my arm.
<|endoftext|>

Bones broken and sinews wounded
could not fail to hurt; I was ready enough to
bear pain, if only I got back home.
<|endoftext|>

My arm--broken ever
since; it was sixty
years ago.
<|endoftext|>

One limb, although
destroyed,--whole body
safe!
<|endoftext|>

But even now on winter
nights when the wind and rain blow From evening on till day’s
dawn I cannot sleep for pain.
<|endoftext|>

Not sleeping for pain Is a
small thing to bear, Compared with the joy of being
alive when all the rest are dead.
<|endoftext|>

For otherwise, years ago, at the ford
of Lu River My body would have died and my soul
hovered by the bones that no one gathered.
<|endoftext|>

Over the graves of
ten thousand soldiers, mournfully
hovering.
<|endoftext|>

” So
the old man
spoke.
<|endoftext|>

And I bid you listen to his words Have you not
heard That the Prime Minister of K’ai-yüan, Sung K’ai-fu, Did not reward frontier exploits,
lest a spirit of aggression should prevail?
<|endoftext|>

And have you not heard That the Prime Minister
of T’ien-Pao, Yang Kuo-chung Desiring to win imperial
favour, started a frontier war?
<|endoftext|>

But long before he could win the
war, people had lost their temper; Ask the man with
the broken arm in the village of Hsin-fēng?
<|endoftext|>

White billows and huge waves block the river
crossing; Wherever I go, danger and difficulty;
whatever I do, failure.
<|endoftext|>

Just as in my worldly career I
wander and lose the road, So when I come to the river
crossing, I am stopped by contrary winds.
<|endoftext|>

Of fishes and prawns sodden in the rain
the smell fills my nostrils; With the stings of insects that
come with the fog, my whole body is sore.
<|endoftext|>

I am growing
old, time flies, and my short
span runs out.
<|endoftext|>

While I sit in
a boat at Chiu-k’ou,
wasting ten days!
<|endoftext|>

I take your poems in my hand and
read them beside the candle; The poems are finished:
the candle is low: dawn not yet come.
<|endoftext|>

With sore eyes by the guttering candle
still I sit in the dark, Listening to waves that, driven
by the wind, strike the prow of the ship.
<|endoftext|>

I have still to travel in my solitary
boat three or four leagues-- By misty waters and rainy
sands, while the yellow dusk thickens.
<|endoftext|>

The forest trees are leafless and withered,--after
the mountain rain; The roofs of the houses are hidden
low among the river mists.
<|endoftext|>

The horses, fed on water grass, are
too weak to carry their load; The cottage walls of wattle
and thatch let the wind blow on one’s bed.
<|endoftext|>

In the distance I see red-wheeled coaches
driving from the town-gate; They have taken the trouble, these civil
people, to meet their new Prefect!
<|endoftext|>

There is no one among men that
has not a special failing: And my failing
consists in writing verses.
<|endoftext|>

I have broken away from
the thousand ties of life: But this
infirmity still remains behind.
<|endoftext|>

And often, when I have finished
a new poem, Alone I climb the road
to the Eastern Rock.
<|endoftext|>

I lean my body on the
banks of white stone: I pull down with my
hands a green cassia branch.
<|endoftext|>

My mad singing startles
the valleys and hills: The apes and
birds all come to peep.
<|endoftext|>

Fearing to become a
laughing-stock to the world, I choose a place that is
unfrequented by men.
<|endoftext|>

At Nine Rivers, in the tenth year,
in winter,--heavy snow; The river-water covered with ice
and the forests broken with their load.
<|endoftext|>

The birds of the air, hungry and
cold, went flying east and west; And with them flew a migrant
“yen,” loudly clamouring for food.
<|endoftext|>

Among the snow it pecked for grass; and rested
on the surface of the ice: It tried with its wings to scale
the sky; but its tired flight was slow.
<|endoftext|>

The boys of the river spread a net
and caught the bird as it flew; They took it in their hands
to the city-market and sold it there alive.
<|endoftext|>

I that was once a man of the
North am now an exile here: Bird and man, in their
different kind, are each strangers in the south.
<|endoftext|>

And because the sight of an exiled bird
wounded an exile’s heart, I paid your ransom and set
you free, and you flew away to the clouds.
<|endoftext|>

Yen, Yen, flying
to the clouds, tell me, whither
shall you go?
<|endoftext|>

The official army and the rebel army
have grown old in their opposite trenches; The soldier’s rations
have grown so small, they’ll be glad of even you.
<|endoftext|>

The brave boys, in their hungry plight, will shoot
you and eat your flesh; They will pluck from your body those
long feathers and make them into arrow-wings!
<|endoftext|>

_You_, so bravely
splashing reds
and blues!
<|endoftext|>

Just when _I_
am getting wrinkled
and old.
<|endoftext|>

Why should you waste the moments
of inspiration Tracing the withered
limbs of a sick man?
<|endoftext|>

Tall, tall is the Palace
of Ch’i-lin; But my deeds have not been
frescoed on its walls.
<|endoftext|>

Minutely limned on a
foot of painting silk-- What can I do
with a portrait such as _that_?
<|endoftext|>

Yesterday I heard
that such-a-one was gone; This morning they
tell me that so-and-so is dead.
<|endoftext|>

Of friends and acquaintances
more than two-thirds Have suffered change and
passed to the Land of Ghosts.
<|endoftext|>

Those that are gone I shall not
see again; They, alas, are for
ever finished and done.
<|endoftext|>

Those that are
left,--where are
they now?
<|endoftext|>

They are all scattered,--a
thousand miles
away.
<|endoftext|>

Those I have known and loved
through all my life, On the fingers of
my hand--how many do I count?
<|endoftext|>

Longing for each other we are
all grown gray; Through the Fleeting World rolled
like a wave in the stream.
<|endoftext|>

Alas that the feasts and
frolics of old days Have withered and
vanished, bringing us to this!
<|endoftext|>

When shall we meet and
drink a cup of wine And laughing gaze
into each other’s eyes?
<|endoftext|>

Up and up,
the Incense-burner
Peak!
<|endoftext|>

In my heart
is stored what my eyes
and ears perceived.
<|endoftext|>

All the year--detained by
official business; To-day at last
I got a chance to go.
<|endoftext|>

Grasping the creepers, I
clung to dangerous rocks; My hands
and feet--weary with groping for hold.
<|endoftext|>

There came with me
three or four friends, But two friends dared
not go further.
<|endoftext|>

At last we reached the topmost
crest of the Peak; My eyes were blinded,
my soul rocked and reeled.
<|endoftext|>

The chasm beneath
me--ten thousand feet; The ground I
stood on, only a foot wide.
<|endoftext|>

If you have not exhausted the scope
of seeing and hearing, How can you realize
the wideness of the world?
<|endoftext|>

The waters of the River
looked narrow as a ribbon, P’ēn Castle
smaller than a man’s fist.
<|endoftext|>

How it clings,
the dust of the world’s
halter!
<|endoftext|>

It chokes my
limbs: I cannot
shake it away.
<|endoftext|>

Thinking of retirement,
I heaved an envious sigh, Then, with lowered
head, came back to the Ants’ Nest.
<|endoftext|>

My new Province is a
land of bamboo-groves: Their shoots in spring
fill the valleys and hills.
<|endoftext|>

The mountain woodman cuts an
armful of them And brings them down to
sell at the early market.
<|endoftext|>

Things are cheap in proportion
as they are common; For two farthings,
I buy a whole bundle.
<|endoftext|>

I put the shoots in a
great earthen pot And heat them up
along with boiling rice.
<|endoftext|>

The purple nodules
broken,--like an old brocade; The
white skin opened,--like new pearls.
<|endoftext|>

Now every day
I eat them recklessly; For a
long time I have not touched meat.
<|endoftext|>

Sent as a present
from Annam-- A red
cockatoo.
<|endoftext|>

Coloured like the peach-tree
blossom, Speaking with
the speech of men.
<|endoftext|>

And they did to it
what is always done To the
learned and eloquent.
<|endoftext|>

They took a
cage with stout bars And shut
it up inside.
<|endoftext|>

After lunch--one short
nap: On waking up--two
cups of tea.
<|endoftext|>

Raising my head, I
see the sun’s light Once again
slanting to the south-west.
<|endoftext|>

Those who are happy regret
the shortness of the day; Those who are sad
tire of the year’s sloth.
<|endoftext|>

But those whose hearts are devoid
of joy or sadness Just go on living,
regardless of “short” or “long.
<|endoftext|>

” Above, a mountain ten
thousand feet high: Below, a river
a thousand fathoms deep.
<|endoftext|>

A strip of green, walled by
cliffs of stone: Wide enough for the
passage of a single reed.
<|endoftext|>

Long before night the
walls are black with dusk; Without
wind white waves rise.
<|endoftext|>

The big rocks are like
a flat sword: The little rocks
resemble ivory tusks.
<|endoftext|>

We are stuck
fast and cannot
move a step.
<|endoftext|>

How much the
less, three hundred
miles?
<|endoftext|>

Frail and slender, the twisted-bamboo
rope: Weak, the dangerous hold
of the towers’ feet.
<|endoftext|>

A single slip--the whole
convoy lost: And _my_ life
hangs on _this_ thread!
<|endoftext|>

I have heard a saying “He that has
an upright heart Shall walk scathless through
the lands of Man and Mo.
<|endoftext|>

” How can I believe that
since the world began In every
shipwreck none have drowned but rogues?
<|endoftext|>

And how can I, born
in evil days And fresh from failure,
ask a kindness of Fate?
<|endoftext|>

Often I fear that
these un-talented limbs Will be laid
at last in an un-named grave!
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly, suddenly,--bearing
a stricken heart I left the gates,
with nothing to comfort me.
<|endoftext|>

Henceforward,--relegated to deep seclusion In a bottomless
gorge, flanked by precipitous mountains, Five months on end the passage of boats
is stopped By the piled billows that toss and leap like colts.
<|endoftext|>

The inhabitants of Pa
resemble wild apes; Fierce and lusty,
they fill the mountains and prairies.
<|endoftext|>

Among such as these I cannot hope
for friends And am pleased with anyone who
is even remotely human!
<|endoftext|>

I took money and bought
flowering trees And planted them out on
the bank to the east of the Keep.
<|endoftext|>

I simply bought whatever
had most blooms, Not caring whether
peach, apricot, or plum.
<|endoftext|>

A hundred fruits, all mixed
up together; A thousand branches,
flowering in due rotation.
<|endoftext|>

Each has its season coming
early or late; But to all alike the
fertile soil is kind.
<|endoftext|>

The red flowers hang like
a heavy mist; The white flowers gleam
like a fall of snow.
<|endoftext|>

The wandering bees cannot
bear to leave them; The sweet birds also
come there to roost.
<|endoftext|>

In front there flows an ever-running
stream; Beneath there is built a little
flat terrace.
<|endoftext|>

Sometimes I sweep the flagstones
of the terrace; Sometimes, in the wind,
I raise my cup and drink.
<|endoftext|>

The flower-branches screen my head
from the sun; The flower-buds fall
down into my lap.
<|endoftext|>

Alone drinking, alone singing
my songs I do not notice that the
moon is level with the steps.
<|endoftext|>

The people of Pa do not
care for flowers; All the spring
no one has come to look.
<|endoftext|>

But their Governor General,
alone with his cup of wine Sits till evening
and will not move from the place!
<|endoftext|>

My niece, who is six
years old, is called “Miss Tortoise”; My
daughter of three,--little “Summer Dress.
<|endoftext|>

” One is beginning to learn
to joke and talk; The other can already
recite poems and songs.
<|endoftext|>

At morning they play clinging
about my feet; At night they sleep
pillowed against my dress.
<|endoftext|>

Why, children, did you reach
the world so late, Coming to me
just when my years are spent?
<|endoftext|>

Young things draw our
feelings to them; Old people
easily give their hearts.
<|endoftext|>

The sweetest vintage
at last turns sour; The full
moon in the end begins to wane.
<|endoftext|>

And so with men the bonds of
love and affection Soon may change to a
load of sorrow and care.
<|endoftext|>

But all the world is bound
by love’s ties; Why did I think that I
alone should escape?
<|endoftext|>

Trees growing--right in front of
my window; The trees are high
and the leaves grow thick.
<|endoftext|>

the distant mountain
view Obscured by this, dimly
shows between.
<|endoftext|>

One morning I took
knife and axe; With my own hand I
lopped the branches off.
<|endoftext|>

Ten thousand leaves fall about
my head; A thousand hills came
before my eyes.
<|endoftext|>

Suddenly, as when clouds or mists break And straight through,
the blue sky appears; Again, like the face of a friend one has
loved Seen at last after an age of parting.
<|endoftext|>

First there came a gentle
wind blowing; One by one the birds
flew back to the tree.
<|endoftext|>

To ease my mind I gazed to
the South East; As my eyes wandered,
my thoughts went far away.
<|endoftext|>

Of men there is none that has
not some preference; Of things there is
none but mixes good with ill.
<|endoftext|>

It was not that I did
not love the tender branches; But
better still,--to see the green hills!
<|endoftext|>

I have been ill so long that
I do not count the days; At the southern
window, evening--and again evening.
<|endoftext|>

Sadly chirping in the
grasses under my eaves The winter
sparrows morning and evening sing.
<|endoftext|>

By an effort I rise and lean
heavily on my bed; Tottering I
step towards the door of the courtyard.
<|endoftext|>

By chance I meet a friend who
is coming to see me; Just as if
I had gone specially to meet him.
<|endoftext|>

They took my couch and placed it in
the setting sun; They spread my rug and
I leaned on the balcony-pillar.
<|endoftext|>

Tranquil talk was better than
any medicine; Gradually the
feelings came back to my numbed heart.
<|endoftext|>

Little sleeping and much
grieving,--the traveller Rises at midnight
and looks back towards home.
<|endoftext|>

The sands are bright with moonlight
that joins the shores; The sail is white with
dew that has covered the boat.
<|endoftext|>

Nearing the sea, the river grows
broader and broader: Approaching autumn,--the
nights longer and longer.
<|endoftext|>

Thirty times we have
slept amid mists and waves, And
still we have not reached Hang-chow!
<|endoftext|>

I grew up
at Jung-yang; I was still
young when I left.
<|endoftext|>

On and on,--forty years passed
Till again I stayed for
the night at Jung-yang.
<|endoftext|>

When I went away, I
was only eleven or twelve;
This year I am turned fifty-six.
<|endoftext|>

Yet thinking back to the
times of my childish games, Whole and undimmed,
still they rise before me.
<|endoftext|>

The old houses have all
disappeared; Down in the village
none of my people are left.
<|endoftext|>

It is not only that streets
and buildings have changed; But steep is level
and level changed to steep!
<|endoftext|>

Alone unchanged, the waters
of Ch’iu and Yu Passionless,--flow
in their old course.
<|endoftext|>

While on the road to his new province, Hang-chow, in 822,
he sends a silver spoon to his niece A-kuei, whom he had been obliged to leave
behind with her nurse, old Mrs.
<|endoftext|>

Little girls ought to
be daintily
fed: Mrs.
<|endoftext|>

Ts’ao,
please see to
this!
<|endoftext|>

That’s why I’ve packed and sent a
silver spoon; You will think of me and
eat up your food nicely!
<|endoftext|>

Long ago to a white-haired
gentleman You made the present
of a black gauze hat.
<|endoftext|>

The gauze hat still sits on
my head; But you already are gone
to the Nether Springs.
<|endoftext|>
