The thing is old, but still
fit to wear; The man is gone and
will never be seen again.
<|endoftext|>

Out on the hill the moon is
shining to-night And the trees on your tomb
are swayed by the autumn wind.
<|endoftext|>

That so many of the
poor should suffer from cold what can
we do to prevent?
<|endoftext|>

To bring warmth to
a single body
is not much use.
<|endoftext|>

I wish I had a big rug
ten thousand feet long, Which at one time could cover
up every inch of the City.
<|endoftext|>

Our party scattered at yellow dusk
and I came home to bed; I woke at midnight and went for
a walk, leaning heavily on a friend.
<|endoftext|>

As I lay on my pillow my vinous
complexion, soothed by sleep, grew sober; In front of the tower the ocean
moon, accompanying the tide, had risen.
<|endoftext|>

The swallows, about to return to the beams,
went back to roost again; The candle at my window, just
going out, suddenly revived its light.
<|endoftext|>

All the time till dawn came, still my thoughts
were muddled; And in my ears something sounded
like the music of flutes and strings.
<|endoftext|>

_Then_ on the shore,--building
sand-pagodas; _Now_, at Court, covered with
tinkling jade.
<|endoftext|>

This and that,--equally childish
games, Things whose substance passes in
a moment of time!
<|endoftext|>

While the hands are busy, the
heart cannot understand; When there are no
Scriptures, then Doctrine is sound.
<|endoftext|>

Even should one zealously
strive to learn the Way, That very striving
will make one’s error more.
<|endoftext|>

All the morning I have
lain perversely in bed; Now at
dusk I rise with many yawns.
<|endoftext|>

My warm stove is quick to
get ablaze; At the cold mirror I
am slow in doing my hair.
<|endoftext|>

With melted snow I
boil fragrant tea; Seasoned with
curds I cook a milk-pudding.
<|endoftext|>

At my sloth and greed there
is no one but me to laugh; My cheerful vigour
none but myself knows.
<|endoftext|>

The taste of my wine is mild and
works no poison; The notes of my harp
are soft and bring no sadness.
<|endoftext|>

To the Three Joys in the book
of Mencius I have added the
fourth of playing with my baby-boy.
<|endoftext|>

I break up cypress
and make a book-box; The box
well-made,--and the cypress-wood tough.
<|endoftext|>

In it shall be
kept what author’s
works?
<|endoftext|>

The inscription
says PO
LO-T’IEN.
<|endoftext|>

All my life has been spent
in writing books, From when I was young
till now that I am old.
<|endoftext|>

First and last,--seventy
whole volumes; Big and little,--three
thousand themes.
<|endoftext|>

All I can do is to divide them
among my daughters To be left by them
to give to my grandchildren.
<|endoftext|>

Between thirty and forty, one is
distracted by the Five Lusts; Between seventy and eighty,
one is a prey to a hundred diseases.
<|endoftext|>

But from fifty to
sixty one is free from all ills;
Calm and still--the heart enjoys rest.
<|endoftext|>

I have put behind me Love and Greed; I
have done with Profit and Fame; I am still short of illness
and decay and far from decrepit age.
<|endoftext|>

Strength of limb I still possess to
seek the rivers and hills; Still my heart has spirit
enough to listen to flutes and strings.
<|endoftext|>

At leisure I open new
wine and taste several cups; Drunken I recall
old poems and sing a whole volume.
<|endoftext|>

” Hundreds of houses,
thousands of houses,--like
a chess-board.
<|endoftext|>

The twelve streets
like a field planted with
rows of cabbage.
<|endoftext|>

In the distance perceptible, dim,
dim--the fire of approaching dawn; And a single row
of stars lying to the west of the Five Gates.
<|endoftext|>

Mounting on high I begin to realize
the smallness of Man’s Domain; Gazing into distance I begin
to know the vanity of the Carnal World.
<|endoftext|>

I turn my head and hurry
home--back to the Court and Market, A single
grain of rice falling--into the Great Barn.
<|endoftext|>

Two top-knots not
yet plaited into
one.
<|endoftext|>

Of thirty
years--just beyond
half.
<|endoftext|>

You who are really a lady
of silks and satins Are now become my
hill and stream companion!
<|endoftext|>

At the spring fountains together
we splash and play: On the lovely trees
together we climb and sport.
<|endoftext|>

Her cheeks grow rosy, as
she quickens her sleeve-dancing: Her brows grow sad,
as she slows her song’s tune.
<|endoftext|>

Don’t go singing the Song of
the Willow Branches, When there’s no one
here with a heart for you to break!
<|endoftext|>

At night you came and took my hand and we wandered
together in my dream; When I woke in the morning there was no one to
stop the tears that fell on my handkerchief.
<|endoftext|>

On the banks of the Ch’ang my aged body
three times has passed through sickness; At Hsien-yang to the grasses
on your grave eight times has autumn come.
<|endoftext|>

You lie buried beneath
the springs and your bones are
mingled with the clay.
<|endoftext|>

I--lodging in the
world of men; my hair
white as snow.
<|endoftext|>

A-wei and Han-lang both followed in
their turn; Among the shadows of the Terrace
of Night did you know them or not?
<|endoftext|>

At night, in my
dream, I stoutly climbed
a mountain.
<|endoftext|>

Going out alone
with my staff of
holly-wood.
<|endoftext|>

A thousand crags, a hundred hundred valleys-- In
my dream-journey none were unexplored And all the while my feet never grew tired
And my step was as strong as in my young days.
<|endoftext|>

Can it be that when the
mind travels backward The body also
returns to its old state?
<|endoftext|>

And can it be, as between
body and soul, That the body may
languish, while the soul is still strong?
<|endoftext|>

Soul and body--both are
vanities: Dreaming and waking--both
alike unreal.
<|endoftext|>

In the day my feet are palsied
and tottering; In the night my steps
go striding over the hills.
<|endoftext|>

As day and night are
divided in equal parts-- Between the
two, I _get_ as much as I _lose_.
<|endoftext|>

Congratulating himself on
the comforts of his life after his
retirement from office.
<|endoftext|>

Lined coat, warm cap and easy felt slippers,
In the little tower, at the low window, sitting
over the sunken brazier.
<|endoftext|>

Body at rest,
heart at peace; no need to
rise early.
<|endoftext|>

I wonder if the courtiers
at the Western Capital know
of these things, or not?
<|endoftext|>

No new poems his
brush will trace: Even
his fame is dead.
<|endoftext|>

His old poems are deep
in dust At the bottom of
boxes and cupboards.
<|endoftext|>

Once lately, when someone was singing,
Suddenly I heard a verse-- Before I had time
to catch the words A pain had stabbed my heart.
<|endoftext|>

“Those who speak know
nothing; Those who know
are silent.
<|endoftext|>

” These words, as
I am told, Were spoken
by Lao-tzŭ.
<|endoftext|>

If we are to believe that Lao-tzŭ
Was himself _one who knew_, How comes it that he wrote
a book Of five thousand words?
<|endoftext|>

Chuang-tzŭ levels all things
And reduces them to
the same Monad.
<|endoftext|>

But _I_ say that
even in their sameness
Difference may be found.
<|endoftext|>

A traveller
came from across the seas Telling
of strange sights.
<|endoftext|>

“In a deep fold of the
sea-hills I saw a terrace
and tower.
<|endoftext|>

In the midst there
stood a Fairy Temple With
one niche empty.
<|endoftext|>

They all told me
this was waiting
For Lo-t’ien to come.
<|endoftext|>

” Traveller, I have studied the Empty Gate;
I am no disciple of Fairies The story you
have just told Is nothing but an idle tale.
<|endoftext|>

The hills of
ocean shall never
be Lo-t’ien’s home.
<|endoftext|>

When I leave the earth
it will be to go To the Heaven
of Bliss Fulfilled.
<|endoftext|>

They have put my bed beside the
unpainted screen; They have shifted my stove
in front of the blue curtain.
<|endoftext|>

I listen to my grandchildren,
reading me a book; I watch the servants,
heating up my soup.
<|endoftext|>

With rapid pencil I answer
the poems of friends; I feel in my pockets
and pull out medicine-money.
<|endoftext|>

When this superintendence of
trifling affairs is done, I lie back on my
pillows and sleep with my face to the South.

Freely

are

ye streets but

<|endoftext|>

Genius, art
thou
alive?
<|endoftext|>

Yes, here within thy sanctified
walls there's a soul in each object,
ROMA eternal.
<|endoftext|>

For me, only,
are all things
yet mute.
<|endoftext|>

Who will then tell me in whispers
and where must I find just the window Where one day she'll
be glimpsed: creature who'll scorch me with love?
<|endoftext|>

Can't I divine yet the paths through
which over and over To her and from her
I'll go, squandering valuable time?
<|endoftext|>

Visiting churches and palaces, all
of the ruins and the pillars, I, a responsible
man, profit from making this trip.
<|endoftext|>

With my business accomplished, ah,
then shall only one temple, AMOR's temple
alone, take the initiate in.
<|endoftext|>

Rome, thou art a whole world, it
is true, and yet without love this World would not be the
world, Rome would cease to be Rome.
<|endoftext|>

More than ever I
dreamed, I have found it: my
happy good fortune!
<|endoftext|>

Cupid
sagaciously led
past those palazzos so fine.
<|endoftext|>

He of course knows very well (and I
have also discovered) What, beneath tapestries
rich, gilded boudoirs conceal.
<|endoftext|>

One may if one wishes
call him a blind, wanton boy--but I know you,
Clever Cupid, too well!
<|endoftext|>

O,
incorruptible

<|endoftext|>

Here he provides me with ev'rything,

<|endoftext|>

--Isn't that
heaven

<|endoftext|>

Say, beautiful
Lady Borghese, What would you

<|endoftext|>

Banquets and game

<|endoftext|>

These but deprive
my sweet boy of his

<|endoftext|>

Finery,
haughtiness do

<|endoftext|>

Does one not lift a

<|endoftext|>

If she's to press in comfort a

<|endoftext|>

Must not the jewelry, and then the

<|endoftext|>

I encounter
no troubles
like those.
<|endoftext|>

Simple dress of rough homespun,
At but a lover's mere touch, tumbles

<|endoftext|>

Quickly he carries the girl as
she's clad in chemise of coarse linen-- Just as a nursemaid
might, playfully up to her bed.
<|endoftext|>

Drapings of satin

<|endoftext|>

Large is this room where
the bed offers its
comfort for two.
<|endoftext|>

Jupiter's welcome to more from his
Juno if he can get it; Let any mortal find

We are content with Cupid's delights,

Some do but scratch us:
Slow and insidious these
poison our hearts over years.
<|endoftext|>

When gods and goddesses in days of
heroes made love, then Lust followed look and desire,
with no delay, was indulged.
<|endoftext|>

Surely you don't think the goddess of
love lost a moment reflecting When, in
Idean grove, Anchises caught her eye.
<|endoftext|>

Nor did Luna delay about kissing
that beautiful dreamer-- Jealous Aurora had

<|endoftext|>

Today a proud Rome
claims to be queen
of the world.

<|endoftext|>

Sweet

<|endoftext|>

For those ashamed of him Cupid
reserves the bitterest passions, Mingling for hypocrites
their pleasure in vice and remorse.
<|endoftext|>

But, at the same time,
the goddess seeks him, she's
watching and list'ning.
<|endoftext|>

Should find him with you, ill disposed will she be: Frighten
you, frowning austerely, contemptuously, violently casting Into
the worst of repute houses he's known to frequent.
<|endoftext|>

Ah, it's the
same with
me, too.
<|endoftext|>

I haven't
escaped her, the
goddess.
<|endoftext|>

Jealously she
seeks me out, sweet secret
love to expose.
<|endoftext|>

I will submit to the ancient
law and in silence revere her, For, when great lords
fall out, I like the Greeks must atone.
<|endoftext|>

However comely be strength, or
free and undaunted comportment, Secrecy is for
a man most important of all.
<|endoftext|>

Mighty subduer of cities,
Discretion, O princess of nations, Goddess whom
I adore, safely you've led me thus far.
<|endoftext|>

Now, though, what
fate shall befall
me?
<|endoftext|>

My frivolous muse has
now opened --Cupid, the scamp--opens lips
hitherto sealed so well.
<|endoftext|>

Difficult is it, alas, to conceal the
shame of a monarch; Hide it can neither his crown, nor a
tight Phrygian cap: Midas has asses ears!
<|endoftext|>

the first
servant discovers--O
horror!
<|endoftext|>

Shame of this secret
so weighs, Midas
unburdens his heart.
<|endoftext|>

Into the earth for safekeeping the
servant must bury the story, Easing in this
way the king:  earth must conceal the tale.
<|endoftext|>

Reeds in a trice are sprouting and
rustling in murmuring breezes: "Midas, o Midas
the King--bears the ears of an ass!
<|endoftext|>

" Mine is a secret more pleasant, but
even more difficult keeping: Out of abundance
of heart eagerly speaketh my mouth.
<|endoftext|>

None of my ladyfriends dare I confide
in, for they would but chide me; Nor any gentleman
friend, lest he be rival to me.
<|endoftext|>

Rapture proclaim to
the grove, to the echoing
cliffs perorate it?
<|endoftext|>

One can do that if
one's young, or if one's
lonely enough.
<|endoftext|>

I to hexameters tell, in
pentameters I will confide it: During the
day she was joy, happiness all the night long.
<|endoftext|>

She's coming, and must
not be seen by
the neighbor!
<|endoftext|>

Breezes, rustle
the leaves: muffle the
sound of her feet.
<|endoftext|>

And as for you, little poems, o
grow and flower, your blossoms Cradling themselves in
the air, tepid and soft with love's breath.
<|endoftext|>

Wafting, betray to Quirites, as
Midas' reeds did with cheap gossip, One happy couple in
love, and their sweet secret, at last.
<|endoftext|>

I in the back of the garden,
the last of the gods, in a corner,
Ineptly formed, must I stand.
<|endoftext|>

Evil
the inroads
of time.
<|endoftext|>

Cucumber vines grow entwining
about this primeval lingam, Cracking it almost
in two under the weight of the fruit.
<|endoftext|>

Summer's no better: the servants
Empty their bowels and show insolent,
naked behinds.
<|endoftext|>

Filth,
above and
below!
<|endoftext|>

I was clearly in danger
of turning Into filth myself, toadstool,
rotten wood!
<|endoftext|>

Now, by your efforts, O
noblest of artists, I shall recover With
fellow gods my just place.
<|endoftext|>

And it's
no more than
my due.
<|endoftext|>

Jupiter's throne, so dishonestly
won, it was I who secured it: Color and ivory,
marble and bronze, not to mention the poems.
<|endoftext|>

Now, all intelligent
men look upon me
in kindness.
<|endoftext|>

They like to Form their
own image of me, just as
the poet has done.
<|endoftext|>

Nor do the girls take
offense when they see me--by no
means the matrons.
<|endoftext|>

None finds me ugly
today, though I am
monstrously strong.
<|endoftext|>
